INDONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRIFICATION PROJECT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:   Republic of Indonesia
Beneficiary: State Electricity Corporation (P.T. PLN (PERSERO))
Amount:    US$ 398.0 million equivalent
Terms:      Repayable in 20 years, including five years of grace at the standard variable
interest rate
Onlending:  The proceeds of the loan, except $2.2 million which would be made available to
MOC for the implementation of its project component, would be onlent by the
Government of Indonesia (GOI) to PLN for 20 years, including a grace period
of 5 years; the subsidiary loan will be denominated in foreign currency withdrawn
and the onlending interest rate would be equal to the Bank's standard variable
interest rate plus 0.5 percent per annum. PLN would bear the foreign exchange
risk.
Project:    The proposed project would support the development and implementation of an
efficient RE program in Indonesia and consists of two components, for PLN and
for the Ministry of Cooperatives and Small Enterprises Development (MOC).
The PLN component includes: (a) electrification of 7000 rural villages in the
second and third years of Repelita VI (95/96-96/97) through the construction of
an estimated 28,000 kmc of MV lines and 35,000 kmc of LV lines, 1.3 million
poles and 833 MVA of distribution transformer capacity; (b) economic sub-
transmission schemes aimed at promoting private sector participation in small
scale generation by interconnecting captive power plants, cogeneration plants and
other small power producers that are prepared to sell power to a PLN regional
or mini-grid serving a rural area on the basis of a published small power purchase
tariff to be offered by PLN. This tariff is derived in a transparent manner from
PLN's "avoided cost of supply" in different regions, and made available to all
such potential sellers on a non-discriminatory and non-negotiated basis as per the
terms of a simple and standard power purchase contract; (c) development of five
mini-hydro power projects with an aggregate capacity of 7.45 MW and and field
test drilling of two mini-geothermal fields in Lombok and Ambon for which
power plant construction would be considered for financing under a proposed
follow-on Renewable Energy Development project; (d) pilot projects to test the
adaptability of low cost "low tech" equipment designs, low cost network



- jj -
reticulation designs and more efficient construction management methods for full
scale implementation at a subsequent stage, possibly supported by a Bank loan in
RE III; and (e) expansion of the ongoing rural business services component to
promote productive uses of electricity, including participation by NGOs; (f)
technical assistance for: (i) enhancing the RE planning systems capability within
PLN and transferring it to PLN Regions and branches; (ii) preparation of a
Renewable Energy Master Plan for rural electrification in areas not likely to be
covered by PLN's major regional grids within the next ten years and; (iii)
implementing RE-specific institutional changes required to enhance the
organizational and management efficiency of undertaking RE within PLN,
consistent with the ongoing restructuring of PLN and its conversion to Persero
status. The MOC component includes: (a) implementation support for expansion
of schemes for local participation by village level cooperatives and organizations
in power distribution related activities; and (b) preparation of up to two
geographic areas for possible conversion to a "Pola III" mode of organization,
wherein responsibility for all distribution functions including ownership of assets
is transferred from PLN to a third party.
Benefits:    The proposed project would increase the effectiveness of RE delivery by the
further development and implementation of an efficient RE program in Indonesia,
and hence lead to higher returns and speedier coverage. It would help reduce
present and future unit costs of RE and help address a primary constraint to
further enhancing the efficiency of RE delivery as well as the long term
sustainability of the RE program; by facilitating a significant change in PLN's
present organizational structure and management processes for the RE program.
In addition, it would help extend the supply of electricity in rural areas in
Indonesia during 1995-1997 to 7,000 villages and thereby contributing to a
significant improvement in the quality of life of 2.1 million rural households --
with women and children being primary beneficiaries -- and more broadly
contributing to the development of rural areas, by facilitating economic
opportunity through productive uses of electricity.
Risks:      No significant technical or implementation risks are foreseen with respect to the
physical network development program, that involves routine grid extensions and
consumer connections. PLN has consistently exceeded annual rural electrification
targets and implementation schedules. The risk associated with implementation
of the mini-hydro is minimized by a technical design that places maximum
emphasis on standardization, and ensuring appointment of highly qualified
construction management and supervisory contractor in the case of mini-hydro;
and in the case of the mini-geothermal component by employing an internationally
reputed firm under a single integrated drilling services contract. Risks associated
with implementation of the overall policy reform agenda in general and other
elements of the policy component specific to RE -- institutional restructuring, and



- Hii -
implementation of the published small power purchase tariff and standardized
contract and the bulk supply tariff -- are minimal in view of the Governrment's
and PLN's satisfactory record in implementation once agreement is reached.

1
THE ENERGY AND POWER SECTORS
Energy Sector Overview
1.1       Indonesia is richly endowed with diverse and large energy resources including oil, natural
gas, coal, hydropower and geothermal. The oil and gas sector continue to play a critical role in the
Indonesian economy; together accounting for over 85 percent of commercial net energy consumption and
accounting for about 30 percent of the country's export receipts.
1.2       Indonesia's basic goals and policies for the future development of the energy sector are
described in the Outlines of State Policy (Garis-Garis Besar Haulan Negara or GBHN), promulgated by
the People's Consultative Assembly (MPR) in March 1993 to guide the formulation of the Sixth Medium-
Term (five-year 1994/95-1998/99) Development Plan (REPELITA VI); and the Second Stage Long-Term
(twenty-five year) Development Strategy (PJPT-II). The GBHN highlights the importance for Indonesia's
sustained economic and social development of meeting its rapidly growing energy needs efficiently -
including through conservation and diversification of primary energy resources and their more efficient
utilization -- and of minimizing the adverse enviromnental and social impacts of energy use. In the power
sub-sector, the GBHN recognizes that an adequate, reliable and reasonably priced electricity supply is
essential for the country's continuing development, emphasizing its essential role in serving the needs of
the productive sectors and in contributing directly to improving the living standards of the people in all
regions of the country. Rural electrification (RE), is a key and integral part of the Government's rural
development strategy and the long-term goal is to electrify all villages and enable the basic services
provided by energy.
1.3       A key and continuing thrust of the Government's energy strategy is to slow down the pace
of Indonesia's transition to net oil importer status, by diversifying domestic energy consumption towards
alternative and more economic and indigenous energy resources that either have a non-exportable surplus,
such as coal and natural gas, or are non-tradeable (hydro, geothermal). Otn the demand-side, to promote
efficient utilization of oil-based fuels, the Government's policy is to maintain average petroleum product
prices at or above international parity (efficiency pricing), while cross-subsidizing as necessary the price
of kerosene in order to ensure its affordability, with an implicit tax on gasoline.
1.4       In the power sector, which is a major user of petroleum fuels, plans call for a further
reduction of oil's share of power generation from about 50 percent in 1992/93 (about 34 million barrels
of diesel and fuel oil), to about 7 percent by the end of this decade (14 million barrels per year).
1.5       Considerable potential exists for substituting conventional energy supplies and especially for
meeting the decentralized energy needs of the rural sector, by the use of economic non-conventional and
renewable energy supplies, particularly mini-hydro and mini-geothermal power schemes, biomass-based
power projects (including cogeneration), and solar photovoltaic energy systems. The Government has
been undertaking several solar pilot projects for demonstration purposes.



2
1.6        The Government oflndonesia (GOI), has also relied on the following policies in support of
its objectives for the energy sector (para 1.2):
(a) electricity tariffs: Electricity prices have been maintained at low levels for small residential,
commercial and industrial users who account for about 40 percent of PLN's sales. Periodic
adjustments in the average sale price (the latest average increase of about 8 percent, effective
November 1, 1994), have generally maintained this average close to the long-run marginal cost
of supply in Java (para 5.10);
(b) private sector participation: The private sector operates significant amounts of captive power
capacity, and is expected to play an increasingly important role in the supply of gas and electricity.
The Government has already signed power purchase agreements with independent power producers
(IPPs), to supply about 3,000 MW of bulk power to PLN. The Government's plans indicate that
about 5,000 MW of IPP capacity is expected to be commissioned during REPELITA VI, on build-
own-operate (BOO) basis, of which about 600 MW would be Outside Java.
(c) regionally balanced development: The GOI has (i) maintained a uniform price structure for
electricity and petroleum products in all parts of the country, and (ii) extended the supply of
electricity in a balanced manner to all parts of the country. This policy entails the subsidization of
higher cost energy supply to the Outer Islands and to the rural population.
(d) environmental protection: The Government has established and utilizes uniform procedures
and guidelines for the preparation, and approval of environmental impact analysis (ANDAL), for
issuance of environmental clearances, as well as periodic reporting requirements on environmental
management and monitoring during project implementation.
Sector Institutions
1.7        The principal agency responsible for implementing Government policies in the energy sector
is the Ministry of Mines and Energy (MME); which coordinates all activities in the energy sector and
supervises the state enterprises in the sector: PERTAMINA (oil, gas and geothermal), P.T. Bukit Asam
coal), PGN (gas distribution) and PLN (electricity). Other ministries and agencies are also involved in
the sector, for example, the Ministry of Public Works is responsible for hydropower resource surveys
and the operation of multipurpose hydro plants, the National Atomic Energy commission is responsible
for nuclear development, and the Ministry of Cooperatives and Small Enterprises Development (MOC)
is responsible for enhancing the role of cooperatives in rural electrification. An inter-ministerial National
Energy Board (BAKOREN) coordinates energy policies and development with those of other sectors.
BAKOREN is supported by a Technical Committee (PTE) consisting of senior officials in different
departments, chaired by the Director General of Electricity and Energy Development (DGEED). The
electricity subsector is regulated by the MME through the DGEED. The rural electrification Steering
Committee, chaired by the DGEED, is responsible to ensure inter-agency coordination and cooperation
in matters related to the Government's rural electrification program. The organization chart of MME is
shown in Annex 1.1.



3
Electricity Subsector
1.8        The Electricity Act (Law No. 15 of 1985) defines the legal framework for electricity sector.
Under this Act, PLN, the State Electricity Corporation that was established by Government Regulation
No. 18/1972, has both the right and obligation to supply power in Indonesia. The provisions of the
Electricity Act are amplified in Government Regulation No. 17/1990 for PLN, the State Electricity
Corporation; and No. 10/1989 for others. The Electricity Act permits establishment of private power
producers, distributors and licensees. Presidential Decree No. 37/1992 specifically authorizes private
sector participation under BOO schemes, and permits cooperatives and other legal entities to generate,
transmit, and distribute power for public use.
1.9        At present, the sector comprises: (i) PLN; (ii) captive plants installed by private parties for
their own use; (iii) three Government-sponsored rural electric cooperatives; and (iv) a large number of
informal micro-enterprises providing electricity to clusters of customers in rural villages not served by
PLN. PLN accounts for about half of the electricity used by industries, and the rest is provided by
captive generation facilities. In 1992, the total installed captive generation capacity was about 8,500 MW
of which about 3,300 MW was reserve capacity that served as a backup to PLN supply.
1.10       Indonesia's power sector has grown rapidly during the 1980s and 1990s. Electricity sales
of PLN grew at an average annual rate of about 16 percent over the fifteen year period 1978/79 to
1993/94, from 4,287 GWh to 41,675 GWh. During the same period, the number of customers grew over
eight-fold, from 2.2 million to about 15 million and; the number of villages electrified increased eight-
fold, from 3,400 to over 29,000. Annex 1.2 shows the historic growth in PLN's customers and sales.
In order to meet this growth, PLN's installed capacity increased nearly six-fold from 2,288 MW in
1978/79 to 13,128 MW in 1993/1994. Over the same period, PLN's implementation capacity has grown
significantly, whereby it now connects over one million new consumers annually, a pace un-matched by
most utilities and, PLN carries out an annual investment program of about US$ 3.5 billion.
1.11       In spite of the rapid growth, there is considerable unmet demand at economic prices. In
1993/94, PLN was able to serve just over one-third of all households (44 percent in urban areas and 29
percent in rural areas); whereas at least 90 percent of urban households and two-thirds of rural households
are estimated to be able to purchase electricity at the economic cost of supply. In the industrial sector,
PLN accounts for about 50 percent of electricity used by industries, with the remainder provided by
captive generation plant.
Power Sector Development and Investment Program
1.12       Demand Forecast The potential for future growth in electricity consumption remains high
in Indonesia. Residential energy sales will increase as the household electrification ratios and incomes
rise. PLN's core development program targets household electrification ratios of 60 percent by the end
of REPELITA VI (1994/95-1998/99), and 75 percent by the end of REPELITA VII (1999/00-2003/04)
in Java-Bali. The corresponding targets for Outside Java are 45 percent and 56 percent, respectively. The
rise in industrial consumption is underpinned by the industrial sector GDP growth rate projected to be
about 10 to 11 percent per annum during this decade; and by the targeted share of industrial electricity
consumption to be supplied by PLN (50 percent by the end of REPELITA VI). This core plan implies
an average annual growth rate in total electricity consumption over the period 1991/92 to 2003/04 to be
about 12 percent; with PLN sales projected to grow at about 12 to 14 percent per year over 1992/93-
2003/04 (Annex 1.3). It is expected that private power producers will commence operations in future and



4
sell substantial amounts of bulk energy to PLN starting 1998/99 in Java-Bali as well as the Outer Islands.
For example, about 20,000 GWH (28 percent) of PLN sales in Java-Bali in 1999/00 are projected to be
supplied by private generators.
1.13       Development Program   A core development program for the power sector has been
determined to meet the projected increase in electricity demand in a least cost manner; taking into account
likely participation of private generators and the outer envelope of financing available to PLN for
REPELITA VI from all sources -- Government contributions, self-financing, and borrowings -- that has
been estimated to be about Rp. 50 trillion (US$ 17.4 billion in constant 1993 prices, excluding financing
costs). The core program envisages generating capacity additions to the Java-Bali grid over the period
1994-1998 of about 8,000 MW, of which 2,135 MW (about 27%) is expected from private projects. In
the Outer Islands, the installed capacity is projected to increase by about 2,700 MW by 1998, of which
about 480 MW (18%) is expected to come from the private sector. Annexes 1.4 and 1.5 summarize the
program's generation expansion components for the Java-Bali system and for the Outer Islands as a
whole, through 2003/04.
1.14      Investment Requirements The overall investment requirements for REPELITA VI are
estimated to be US$ 21.2 billion in constant 1993 prices and excluding financing costs; not counting the
costs incurred by industry for installing captive plant in the future. Of this amount, about US$ 3.8 billion
is estimated private sector investment, including initial expenditures for plants that will be commissioned
after REPELITA VI. The balance of about US$ 17.4 billion represents PLN investment. Annexes 1.6
and 1.7 provide estimates of annual investments for PLN in Java-Bali and in the Outer Islands. Including
financing costs, these estimates indicate a sector financing requirement -- PLN plus large private projects
-- of the order of US$ 25-30 billion for REPELITA VI, which represents a formidable challenge even
for Indonesia's growing economy.
Strategic Sector-wide Issues and Priorities for Reform
1.15      The task ahead. Following two decades of rapid growth, Indonesia's power sector is now
at a major crossroads. Significant changes, based on a new system of sector governance and organization
and requiring the introduction of major reforms, are needed to ensure that the sector can effectively meet
the daunting challenges of future expansion. Today, about five out of ten urban households and seven out
of ten rural households must do without electricity; industry is forced to self-generate nearly 50 percent
of its electricity requirements, much at higher cost than the cost of efficient grid supply; and electricity
consumption averages about 270 kwh per capita, a level below that in many countries with lower per
capita incomes than Indonesia. Furthermore, the rapid pace of sector growth, historically, has posed
challenges that have strained PLN's evolving institutional and management capacities to a point where
customer service, supply reliability and quality have been compromised; and costs remain stubbornly
high, especially in the Outer Islands.
1.16      Challenges. Mobilizing sufficient resources -- financial, managerial and technical -- for
ensuring the adequacy and reliability of power supply and at competitive prices, and quickly and
efficiently expanding the access of electricity to households throughout the country, poses a formidable
challenge to the continued development of the sector. Prospects for attracting the large sums of money
required year-in-and-out to just one sector in Indonesia -- US$ 5-6 billion per year (para 1.14) -- must
also be viewed against the backdrop that in the future the Government will be hard pressed to maintain
past levels of financial contributions to PLN, because of increasing demands on its resources from priority
social sectors. These challenges of the future are several orders of magnitude larger and more complex



5
than faced hitherto and cannot be met by a mere intensification and replication of efforts along established
lines of business-as-usual. The Government recognizes the necessity for fundamental changes to policies,
regulations, organizational structures and management approaches that have guided sector development
and operations in the past; and that several key issues have to be addressed in a coordinated manner in
order to bring about these changes.
1.17       Private sector participation. The GOI has concluded that private sector participation is a
prerequisite for reducing the financial burden on the GOI as well as increasing the efficiency of electricity
production. Projects with signed power purchase agreements, projects for which negotiations are
reportedly nearing completion, together with projects earmarked for private investors and to be
commissioned within the next eight years, account for over 7,000 MW of generation power plant. Major
policy, institutional and sector structure changes are needed to ensure that this participation -- entry as
well as operations -- is based on competition and transparent rules, and that the resulting power purchase
prices are the lowest possible.
1.18       PLN's performance. While PLN has been steadily improving its operations, and has
successfully constructed and operated the Java-Bali grid, PLN's performance has not yet reached best
utility practices. Furthermore, key to the long-term sustainability of the rural electrification program in
Indonesia and accelerating the pace of extending RE coverage will be PLN's ability and success in
enhancing the efficiency of RE design, implementation and delivery, and on a continuing and ongoing
basis. Piece-meal, incremental changes resulting in marginal improvements are no longer adequate for
meeting the challenge ahead. Instead, fundamental structural changes are needed to PLN's organization
structure and management processes in order to further improve PLN's performance.
1.19       Sectoral development and regulation. With the emergence of a significant role for the
private sector, a new sectoral agency would be required to regulate both PLN and the private operators.
There would also be a need for a central agency to jointly optimize PLN's and private investors'
development strategies, based on global least-cost considerations.
1.20       Power tariffs. The ad hoc tariff setting modalities of the past will not be appropriate in the
emerging environment. A mechanism for setting power tariffs should allow PLN to deal effectively with
the private sector, while also providing the necessary incentives to PLN to bring the efficiency of its
operations in line with best practices.
Strategy in Support of Sector Reform
1.21       Dialogue with Govermnent. Based on the dialogue between the Bank and GOI, a consensus
has emerged regarding the main issues facing the sector and on the fundamental policy changes needed
for efficient long-run development of the power sector (paras 1.16 through 1.20). The Government has
formulated its strategy and action plan in a Policy Statement titled: "Goals and Policies for the
Development of the Electric Power Subsector," (Annex 1.8); and has stated its intention to implement
it. The Bank supports the GOI's reform agenda for the power sector. The proposed project together with
the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project will facilitate implementation of the first stage of the power
sector reform agenda; while lending operations to follow during the course of the next several years -
Renewable Energy Development, Power Transmission and Distribution II -- are intended to facilitate and
support implementation of the subsequent stages of the reform agenda for sector restructuring. The major
elements of the Bank's support are summarized in the following.



6
1.22       Policy Framework. The Government's overall strategy to address the major sectoral issues
centers around the vision of creating an environment in the sector wherein PLN and all other participants
will be motivated to their maximum capacities to enhance efficiency, reduce costs and improve service.
As a general direction, the creation of such an environment will be achieved through a shift towards
greater reliance on guidance through incentives; introducing sound commercial principles and where
appropriate competition, attracting private capital -- domestic and foreign -- and implementing supportive
sector regulations and administrative arrangements that are transparently and uniformly applied to all
participants. The four pillars of the Government's power sector reform implementation strategy are: (i)
expanding entry to private investors and operators, to accelerate expansion of supply and upgrade sector
operating standards and practices through actual competition or "competition by comparison"; (ii)
restructuring and corporatizing PLN, to improve the company's performance, customer service reliability
and responsiveness and its ability to directly access the capital markets, while continuing to draw upon
its extensive organizational and geographical outreach and unique implementation experience in rural
electrification; (iii) reforming regulatory and sectoral functions by implementing regulations and
supporting administrative and legal arrangements, to be applied uniformly and transparently to all
participants (PLN and private), that promote efficiency in sector operations and expansion and facilitate
maximization of private sector participation at least cost; and (iv) strengthening sector finance by the
regularization of tariff adjustments to maintain tariffs at adequate levels.
1.23       Private Sector Participation. The Government has high expectations for the extent of
private sector participation in satisfying Indonesia's electricity needs in the future (para 1.13), and
alongside PLN, to help ensure adequate, reliable and economical supplies of electricity. At present, such
participation is constrained: (i) by lack of a policy context in which GOI defines clearly and in adequate
detail, its rationale, objectives and strategies for encouraging private sector participation; (ii) by the lack
of supporting laws and transparent, comprehensive and consistent regulatory framework that clearly
defines the rules under which state-owned and private-power enterprises will compete to gain entry to
various segments of the power market, and regulations governing the subsequent operations should they
gain entry; (iii) by the lack of specific regulations regarding, (a) the fair and efficient pricing of power
sales to the grid and to third parties by private suppliers, (b) wheeling charges for transmission services
on PLN's grid, and (c) efficient pricing of power sales by PLN to distributors; (iv) by the lack of rules
and procedures for promoting private sector participation in modes other than BOO schemes that prima
facie appear to be more efficient in terms of securing private participation at lower cost to the nation,
such as purchase of electricity on the basis of competitive bidding and unsolicited proposals with
transmission access provided if necessary, licensing private generation and cogeneration in industrial
estates and franchising geographical areas to licensees for the generation and or distribution of electricity.
1.24       Some steps towards addressing the constraints identified above have already been completed.
First, the Government has established a Directorate of Private Power under DGEED in the Ministry of
Mines and Energy. Second, the Government is following-up on the findings of a study sponsored by the
Bank with financing from the Norwegian Consultant Trust Fund, which addressed, inter alia, structural
changes to promote an increased private sector role, by undertaking an action-oriented study ("Private
Power Development Study"), to develop a comprehensive Private Power Program. Detailed rules and
procedures to govern private sector participation, are expected to be finalized and implemented by the
end of 1995.
1.25       Under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project, agreement was reached with the
Government that it will: (a) undertake and complete a study on private power development in accordance
with terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank; (b) upon completion of the said study, furnish the same



7
to, and discuss the results and recommendations thereof, with the Bank; and (c) based on the said study's
results and recommendations and subsequent review, comments and discussions prepare draft rules and
procedures to govern private sector participation including the solicitation and evaluation of all forms of
proposals for private power supply, furnish the said draft rules and procedures for review and comments
to the Bank, and (iii) by December 31, 1995, finalize, adopt and enforce the same taking into account
the comments, if any, thereon by the Bank. During negotiations for the proposed project, this covenant
was also agreed to.
1.26       In the context of the Private Power Program, the GOI recognizes the importance of
implementing bulk power purchase and supply tariffs. Agreements regarding the implementation of these
tariffs have been reached in the proposed project (paras 2.32 and 2.34), and the GOI has also expressed
its intention to develop a transmission access procedures and tariff. Bulk purchase power tariffs, pegged
to PLN's avoided costs, will encourage efficient private sector levels and modes of participation in
electricity generation by small producers; the rates for large independent producers would continue to be
set by negotiations in a competitive environment. Similarly, a bulk power supply tariff, based on PLN's
economic cost of supply, will encourage efficient private sector participation in distribution.
1.27       Restructuring PLN. The GOI's strategy for restructuring PLN has two inter-related
aspects: (i) decentralization and unbundling its operations, and (ii) commercialization and corporatization.
To improve its financial performance as well as the quality and efficiency of service, PLN has already
initiated a reorganization of its structure and management system, based on a plan developed with the
assistance of consultants under Bank financing. Improving PLN's efficiency and quality of its services,
necessitate significant changes to the present organizational structure, management processes and
transformation of its corporate culture to that typically found in a commercial enterprise, where
management has strong motivation and autonomy to undertake the necessary actions in pursuit of clearly
established objectives and expectations. In this regard, the Government's policy implementation plan calls
for decentralization of PLN organization and management processes along functional lines in Java
(generation, transmission and distribution), and along geographical lines for Outside Java operations. This
would entail creation of corresponding profit centers and management units, with a separation between
RE and non-RE management, finances and operations. The unit managers would be held accountable for
their performance as measured against a performance contract-based set of objectives and performance
indicators and incentive structure, once these are developed and implemented. To assist in the ongoing
reorganization of PLN, and to further develop a detailed action plan for restructuring PLN ("PLN
Restructuring Plan"), including recommendations for an appropriate organizational and management
model for effectively undertaking rural electrification that can co-exist with other PLN operations that
would be operated along commercial lines, an international consulting firm financed as part of Technical
Assistance under the Suralaya Thermal Power Project (Loan 3501-IND) is underway, with an action plan
for PLN restructuring to be finalized in consultation with the Bank, and implementation to begin by June
30, 1995 (para 3.6).
1.28       Another key element of the GOI's strategy in restructuring PLN is a change in its corporate
status from a State-owned agency with a social purpose (Perum) to a group of profit-oriented limited
liability companies (Perseros), with private participation in their ownership as soon as their financial
performance permits. As an initial step, the Government converted PLN 's corporate status to a Persero,
effective August 1, 1994. Details of the multi-company/subsidiary structure are being worked on at
present. Over time, the GOI intends that the group of companies that emerge as a consequence of
restructuring and unbundling and corporatizing PLN, would compete in a market-oriented environment
and subject to oversight by the regulatory regime that is implemented (para 1.30). The Government would



8
only exercise strategic control over PLN on an "arm's length" basis through performance contracts that
specify clear objectives and efficiency indicators. Under such an agreement, GOI's financial contributions
to PLN would be phased out over time, and ultimately would be limited to: (i) provision of cost recovery
for socially mandated activities (such as expansion of the network and supply capability for increasing
rural electrification coverage); (ii) coverage of operating losses to the extent that the Goverrnent
mandated retail tariffs to end-users do not cover such costs and; (ii) cost recovery in instances where a
Government mandated transfer price -- for bulk supply to distributors -- is lower than the efficient cost
of supply.
1.29       Regulatory Refonn.  A Bank sponsored study, with financing from the Norwegian
Consultant Trust Fund, identified the broad elements of regulatory reform needed in view of the evolution
of the power sector. An initial step towards the establishment of a sectoral regulatory oversight agency
will be the separation of those functions that relate to the power industry's regulation and oversight from
the functions that relate to power sector policy and planning. To this end, agreement was reached that
the Government shall by June 30, 1995, establish a Directorate of Regulation within DGEED with
adequate staff and funds and necessary functions and power to administer and oversee compliance with
regulations (including pricing and technical regulations) regarding the electricity sector. Subsequently,
this Directorate would be converted to a higher level agency with greater autonomy and authority.
1.30       In order to identify and define further specific changes needed, the Ministry of Mines and
Energy will undertake a major review, with the assistance of consultants to be financed by the Bank as
Technical Assistance under the Cirata Hydroelectric Phase II Project (Loan 3602-IND), the power sector's
current organization and legal and regulatory framework, with the aim of formulating and implementing
further regulations and strengthening the Directorate of Regulation ("Regulatory Reform Study"). The
Regulatory Reform Study will also evaluate, inter alia: (i) a "cost plus approach" to electricity pricing,
which would include explicit efficiency benchmarks to regulate recovery of "allowable costs" in the base
tariff; and (ii) a "price cap" regulation approach, wherein the incentive to improve efficiency is implicit
in the price cap set and in subsequent adjustments thereof. Under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power
Project, agreement was reached with the Government that it will: (a) undertake and complete a study on
regulatory reform in the power sector in accordance with terms of reference and in a manner satisfactory
to the Bank; (b) upon completion of the said study, furnish the same for review and comments to, and
discuss the results and recommendations thereof with, the Bank; and (c) based on the said study's results
and recommendations and subsequent review, comments and discussions: (i) prepare draft regulations for
the power sector, (ii) furnish the said draft regulations for review and comments to the Bank, and (iii)
by June 30, 1996, finalize, adopt and enforce the same taking into account the comments, if any, thereon
by the Bank. During negotiations for the proposed project, this covenant was also agreed to.
1.31       Sectoral Development. Effective management of increased private sector participation will
require a transfer of responsibility for power sector development to a central agency in the MME, which
would ensure that the total sectoral demand for electricity is met in jointly optimized least-cost manner,
taking account of the roles of both PLN and private investors. The central agency would project the
future demand for electricity, and determine the most effective mode of meeting the demand, based on
an assessment of private sector participation under the prevailing system of rules and incentives, and of
PLN's resource availability and implementation capabilities. This would help to ensure that the timing,
size and location of all uncommitted large generation plants, including those of PLN and private
promoters, are part of an overall least-cost generation expansion plan. The Government has initiated the
process of upgrading DGEED's capacities in the areas of demand forecasting and least-cost system
expansion planning. In this context, PLN is planning to formulate rolling contingency plans to respond



9
to any supply/demand imbalance due to changes in the anticipated schedule of private sector participation.
1.32       Under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project, agreement was reached with GOI that
it shall: (a) by December 31 each year commencing in 1995, (i) review with the Bank, its power sector
development program, the least-cost planning analysis used to formulate the said program, the roles of
PLN and the private sector in the said program, and the transparency and appropriateness of the business
environment for private sector participation; and (ii) review with the Bank, PLN's development and
investment programs with respect to their consistency with the power sector development program, the
balance among generation, transmission and distribution investments, and the balanced development of
regions. In addition, it was agreed that the Borrower shall ensure that PLN has access to sufficient funds
to finance capital expenditures for its investment program pursuant to the said reviews. During
negotiations for the proposed project, this covenant was also agreed to.
1.33       Regularizing Electricity Tariff Adjustments. The Bank supports the importance that the
GOI attaches to the pricing of electricity so that there is efficiency in consumption, while also providing
strong incentives for PLN to further improve the efficiency of its operations. To this end, the Bank-
financed Regulatory Reform Study TA (para 1.31 ), will provide recommendations for the Government
on a regulatory regime that effectively complements the emerging market structure, in fostering
competitive pressures and an operating environment wherein all participants are motivated to deliver
efficient performance while seeking to optimize the narrower self interests of their respective
organizations. Furthermore, as PLN's purchases of power from independent power producers increase,
it will become increasingly important that PLN's tariffs adjust in response to changes in purchased power
prices. To this end, the Government approved on November 1, 1994, a Periodic Tariff Adjustment
Mechanism to ensure PLN's financial health and to facilitate private power entry into the sector.
1.34       Energy Efficiency. The GOI uses efficiency pricing, complemented by other appropriate
measures, to encourage and induce sound electricity use decisions. In the power sector, the GOI is
focusing on more efficient utilization of electricity, introduction of renewable energy in the rural areas,
and demand-side management (DSM). Under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project, the Bank has
provided financing to strengthen PLN's institutional capability for designing, implementing and evaluating
DSM options and programs, development of a DSM Annual Plan, and implementing DSM within a
framework of integrated resource planning. This TA will assist PLN to: (i) develop a rationalized strategy
for DSM and the corresponding action plan, based upon data and conditions specific to Indonesia, (ii)
establish realistic targets of penetration and savings to be achieved, (iii) determine efficient delivery and
incentive mechanisms, and (iv) formulate the financing plan for DSM.



I



2
RURAL ELECTRIFICATION
The National Development Context
2.1        The basic goals of electric power development in Indonesia as stated in the Government's
Outlines of National Policy (GBHN), are to improve the welfare of the population and to support and
stimulate economic activities both in rural and urban areas. These derive from the three national
principles of development (" Trilogi Pembangunan"): equity, growth and sound economic and fiscal
management. Until the mid-1970s, electricity development efforts were primarily concentrated on the
expansion of urban-industrial areas. By the late 1970s, rural electrification (RE) emerged as a specific
objective of the Government, arising out of concern about the wide disparity in electrification coverage
between urban and rural areas. From then onwards, RE has been recognized as a key and explicit
component of the government's village development strategy (para 1.2).
2.2        The primary means for achieving the national development goals for rural electrification is
through realization of the explicit targets set for village electrification in the national Five Year
Development Plans (REPELITA), starting with REPELITA III (1979/80-1983/84). For this purpose, RE
is defined as the supply of electricity to consumers living in rural areas, identified as areas that are
outside the boundary of the national, provincial and district capitals, and excluding municipal and
administrative towns. The primary agent for implementing the Government's targets for RE is PLN, the
State Electricity Corporation.
2.3        The Government's pursuit of RE is on the basis of its developmental merits. There is a
distinct and positive impact on the quality of life in rural areas upon the introduction of electricity.
Improved lighting makes it possible to extend available hours for work, educational purposes and reading,
as well as recreation, while outdoor illumination contributes to improved security. Reduced air pollution
levels from displacement of kerosene lamps, and greater usage of appliances help mitigate human
suffering and drudgery, with women and children being the greatest beneficiaries, since they spend more
time at home. Furthermore, the economic benefits of electricity made possible from productive uses go
far beyond the increases in productivity, value-added or cost efficiency, and arise as a consequence of
the change over to a electricity using technology. This technology change is a first step in the structural
transformation process of rural development, as communities adjust to changing competitive conditions
and increasingly become integrated in the regional and wider economies. It is these broader development
impacts associated with technological change over time, and made possible by the introduction of
electricity, that provides another central justification for rural electrification. While electricity on its own
will not lead to a more productive rural economy, for most rural areas, development will not occur
without it. It would not be overstating the case that no other single source of energy comes close to
symbolizing the hopes and socioeconomic aspirations of the vast majority of rural households, who look
to the arrival of electricity as a defining milestone event in their lives, with the potential for bringing
about quantum improvements in their daily lives and living conditions and whose arrival symbolizes their
connection to the modern world.



11
The Rural Village Environment
2.4        The Republic of Indonesia is the largest archipelago and the fourth most populous in the
world, comprising of over 13,600 islands, and spread over 5,100 kilometers from east to west and nearly
1800 kilometers from north to south. The population of about 186 million, organized into more than 40
million households and comprising over 300 ethnic groups speaking 200 languages, is bound together by
a common language, Bahasa Indonesia, and a commitment to the shared vision that is embodied in the
concepts of Pancasila as expressed in the Constitution. The island of Java, with about 61 percent of the
total population (114 million), and only about seven percent of the total land area, is the political,
commercial and demographic center of the country. The Outer Islands -- islands other than Java and Bali
-- with a population of about 72 million, include the large islands of Sumatra with a population of about
34 million, Kalimantan with a population of about 9 million and Sulawesi with a population of about 13
million. The balance of the population of about 16 million is dispersed across the 3000 populated islands.
2.5        The rural areas account for nearly 70 percent of Indonesia's population, comprising about
31 million households. Nearly 60 percent of the rural population lives on the islands of Java and Bali,
where the average population density, at about 600 persons/square km. ranks amongst the highest in the
world. The remaining 40 percent of the rural population is dispersed and distributed across the Outer
Islands, where the average population density is about 30 persons/square km. These sharply contrasting
geographic and demographic characteristics serve to highlight the differing development challenges faced
by the RE program in Java-Bali and in the Outer Islands. Java-Bali is better placed for increasing
electricity access to new villages by virtue of the existence of a high voltage backbone transmission grid
supplying a well developed 20kV distribution system; with power generated by large and efficient
baseload plants that operate economically. By contrast, in the Outer Islands, expanding RE access
involves resorting to one of three modes: (i) extending the seven regional grids that operate at lower
transmission voltages and therefore experience higher network losses and that are fed by power generated
by less efficient smaller-scale power plants; (ii) expanding the dozens of existing mini-grids -- served by
one or more interconnected diesel power plants in the 1 -12 MW size range or; (iii) setting up an entirely
new and isolated small diesel power plant based operation -- in the 20 kW to 200 kW size range -- of
which there are over 900 such isolated and very high operating cost systems in operation today, many
only supplying power for a few hours in the evening and night time.
2.6        Rural areas are characterized by a lower level of economic and social development. Average
rural incomes are about 60 percent of income in urban areas; stemming in part from differences in the
patterns of employment. Whereas in rural areas about 70 percent of the employment is in the primary
sectors (mainly agriculture), 8 percent in secondary sectors and 20 percent in services, the corresponding
shares in urban areas are 11 percent, 18 percent and 64 percent. The percentage of rural households
below the poverty line is higher; 17 percent in comparison to 14 percent in urban areas, according to
Government statistics. The rural-urban income differentials are also accompanied by substantial
differences in social development indicators; such as in the case of primary school completion rates (61
percent versus 86 percent), child malnutrition (48 percent versus 39 percent), under five child mortality
(116 versus 84 per thousand births), and fertility rates (3.2 versus 2.6 children per woman). The urban-
rural contrast is also evident in the access to electricity. As of 1992/93, about 50 percent of urban
households had PLN electricity supply versus 32 percent of rural households.
2.7         For development purposes, and from an administrative point of view as well as for statistical
monitoring, the basic unit is a village (desa); which is identified as geographic area occupied by a
population that forms a social unit and which also represents the lowest level of Government



12
administration that has the right to run its own (local) administration under the jurisdiction of a village
head (kepala desa). A village typically comprises a central core population of households and several
scattered hamlets -- individual clusters of 5 to 50+ households -- and isolated farmsteads within the
village area boundary. The average number of households in a rural village is about 900 in Java-Bali and
300 in the Outer islands. The average village population is about 4000 persons/village in Java-Bali and
1500 persons/village in the Outer Islands. A further difference is in the population density within a
village; which is about 600 to 800 persons/square km. in Java-Bali in contrast to about 30 to 60 persons
per square km. in the Outer Islands.
2.8         Village classification. The government uses a three-tiered classification of rural villages
to plan and monitor interventions related to the development status of each village. The Ministry of Home
Affairs classification scheme identifies these village types based on the level of development as reflected
by seven indicators: source of income, level of production, level of modernization, existence of village
institutions, education level, community spirit, and availability of infrastructure. On this basis, all villages
are classified into three types; swasembada ("self-sufficient") villages, swakarva (transitional) villages
and swadava (traditional) villages. As of the 1990 census, in Java-Bali 77 percent were reported to be
in the swasembada category, 18 percent in the swakarya category and 5 percent in the swadaya category.
Comparable numbers in the case of the Outer Islands are, respectively, 25 percent, 52 percent and 23
percent. This classification scheme has been used by the government as a guide to coordinate the rural
development efforts of government agencies, including PLN. For example, swasembada villages are
entitled to electricity supply, paved roads and bank branches; swakarya villages receive priority for water
supply, gravel roads, elementary schools etc.. The long-term goal is to see that every village transitions
to the swasembada status and is fully serviced with the basic human infrastructure and utility services.
2.9         Three step village screening process. A three step process is used to identify the specific
villages to be electrified each year. First, the Government decides on the annual village electrification
targets, national total and provincial targets. Second, PLN employs a screening aimed at identifying the
specific villages to be recommended for electrification in a given region/rural area. This preliminary
screening by PLN, is based on an evaluation method which scores each candidate village on fourteen
variables, one of which is the village classification (swasembada, swakaraya, swadaya). Other parameters
include variables such as distance to the nearest 20 kV grid connection point, pattern of settlement,
installed capacity of captive generators. The weighted average scores provide the basis for rank-ordering
the villages. In the third step, final selections of the specific villages are the outcome of discussions with
regional Government authorities (BAPPEDAS), with priority accorded to villages that have a high
economic potential (for which a swasembada classification serves as a proxy); a sub-district capital
(kecamatan) village; a village that is close to an existing or planned 20 kV grid; villages that are
important from the view point of regional development i.e., villages that have small-scale industries or
potential for productive uses.
Extent Of Rural Electrification
2.10        PLN has been connecting rural consumers ever since it was established in 1972. However,
the "RE Program" at PLN formally started only in 1977/78, with the establishment of a separate RE sub-
directorate, when rural electrification first emerged as a specific objective of the Government and explicit
targets for village electrification to be achieved during REPELITA III (1979/80-1983/84) were set by the
Government. Since then, PLN has consistently met or exceeded the Government's targets for RE and
the record is impressive. At the end of REPELITA 11 (1978/79), the village electrification ratio in
Indonesia was about 5 percent (7 percent in Java-Bali), whereas the household electrification ratio was



13
about 2 percent (3 percent in Java-Bali). By the end of REPELITA V (1993/94), the national village
electrification ratio rose to about 51 percent (72 percent in Java-Bali)'; and the household electrification
ratio was about 32 percent (38 percent in Java and 23 percent outside Java). Annexes 2.1 and 2.2,
respectively, show the growth in village and household electrification achieved over the period 1978/79-
1993/94. The household coverage of rural electrification in Indonesia lags considerably, comparable
development among its regional peers; with the household electrification ratio in Thailand reported to
about 75 percent and about 98 percent in Malaysia.
2.11       Rural electric cooperatives. In addition to PLN, there are three rural electric cooperatives
(Kooperasi Listrik Pedasaan, KLP) in the Outer Islands; that function as vertically integrated utilities
independent of PLN. These KLPs are in: Lampung, South Sumatra (covering about 30 villages and
14,000 consumers and a peak load of about 3.4 MW); in Luwu, South Sulawesi (covering 66 villages
and 16,000 consumers, with a peak load of about 3.1 MW) ; and in Lombok island (covering 97 villages
and 17,000 consumers, with a peak load of about 4.2 MW). This effort was initiated in 1978 with a
USAID-financed project; with the Ministry of Cooperatives (MOC) as the implementing agency, to set
up and demonstrate the concept of rural electric cooperatives in areas that were not planned to be
electrified by PLN in the near future.
2.12       While these rural cooperatives provided a reasonable standard of service for a few years
early on, more recently they have been plagued by technical, financial and management problems. In spite
of the fact that the effective prices for small residential consumers charged by the KLPs range between
two to three times those under PLN's retail tariff structure -- Rp. 230-324 per kWh for a household with
monthly consumption of 30 kWh -- these cooperatives have been unable to cover even operating costs
in recent years; which has resulted in serious deterioration of the distribution network as well as the
generating plant to the point where losses are excessive, reliability of service is extremely poor and as
a consequence, bill collection rates have fallen substantially. Two out of the three KLPs, are financially
bankrupt and the third barely covers its operating costs today. MOC is presently evaluating options to
restructure the KLPs; the most promising of which appears to be a conversion to Pola III scheme (para
2.17); whereby each cooperative would be connected to the nearest PLN grid, thus enabling the
retirement of its expensive and old and inefficient diesel power plants. The cooperatives would then
function as a distributor of electricity, buying power in bulk from PLN and retailing to end users. This
restructuring will require implementing a financial workout plan; keys to which are Government decisions
about the capital structure of the new entity, and the extent to which the Government is prepared to take
an equity position, in order to ensure that retail tariffs for the distributor can be maintained at a
reasonable level and that the workout results in a revitalized cooperative that is sustainable, and no longer
functioning as a demonstration experiment requiring ongoing and special attention.
2.13       Private enterprises. There are reportedly over 17,000 privately owned and unlicensed
micro-enterprises in rural areas, each engaged in selling electricity to a few households (5 to 25);
typically employing a small diesel generator set (1 to 5 kVA) and using a makeshift wiring arrangement
strung from house-to-house. Most often this service is provided for a few hours in the evening, and prices
that effectively range between Rp. 400 to 800 per kWh, with fees upwards of Rp.5,000 per month for
a service to two light bulbs of 25 watts each. Such makeshift arrangements provide service of low
standard and serve solely as a temporary solution until PLN supply becomes available in that area.
I/ A village is classified as "electrified" if at least 30 households are connected to PLN supply.



14
RE Sector Priorities
2.14        The primary objective of the national RE program is to support the development of rural
areas by increasing the welfare of the people and stimulating the growth of economic activities. RE is
a key and integral part of the Government's rural development strategy, and the long-term target is to
electrify all villages and to enable the people to afford the basic services provided by energy.
Coordination of the RE program to expand electricity coverage with Government investments in other
infrastructure and social services is carried out through the provincial Planning Commissions (BAPPEDA)
in each province. This coordination broadly relies on the three-tiered village classification scheme of the
Government (para 2.8).
2.15        Village selection criteria. An equally important objective of the RE program is to promote
the development of the country in a balanced manner; not only with respect to mitigating urban/rural but
geographical/regional disparities as well. This priority is prominently reflected in the process and
procedure used by PLN to arrive at the specific list of villages to be electrified each year (para 2.9). This
approach for village selection has by-and-large yielded reasonable results. However, there is scope for
sharpening the ranking and selection criteria in order to improve the sequencing of villages to be
electrified and their adherence to economic priority needs, primarily by utilizing a smaller and non-
overlapping set of criteria that are purely economic in nature. With these changes, the first step in village
selection and ranking would be based solely on the economic merits of each case. Thus, when departures
from this ranking are discussed during the second stage of consultations to consider other developmental
priorities such as geographical balance, decisions can be made on an informed basis as to the opportunity
costs of such departures.
2.16        There exist substantial regional variations in the extent of electrification coverage levels
today (Annex 2.1 and 2.2). Village electrification ratios projected for year-end 1993/94 are as follows:
Java-Bali (72 percent), Sulawesi (59 percent), Sumatra (44 percent), Kalimantan (23 percent), and Eastern
Indonesia (28 percent). Within Eastern Indonesia, there is considerable variation, ranging from a high
of 100 percent in Bali to a low of 7 percent in East Timor. Household electrification ratios projected for
year-end 1993/94 are as follows: Java-Bali (38 percent), Sulawesi (28 percent), Sumatra (22 percent),
Kalimantan (24 percent), and Eastern Indonesia (16 percent). Again, within Eastern Indonesia, there is
considerable variation; ranging from a high of 60 percent in Bali to a low of 2 percent in East Timor.
These inter-regional variations in village electrification coverage reflect the Government's balancing of
two divergent pulls, balanced regional development on the one hand versus purely economic
considerations. A second balance that is implicit in GOI's strategy for extending RE is the priority given
to extensification over intensification. Given the relatively low levels of household electrification levels
today, the marginal costs of intensification-- the incremental cost of connecting one additional household
within a village that already has access to electricity -- are substantially lower than the marginal cost of
extensification, the cost of extending the 20 kV network to an un-electrified village.
2.17        Local participation.  Local participation, another important objective of the Government
and undertaken through a cooperative arrangement between MOC, MME and PLN, enhances the
developmental benefits of RE programs, by fostering a sense of ownership and control among the rural
communities involved through grassroots organizations such as Kooperasi Unit Desa (KUD), or village
cooperative unit). If properly structured, local participation also makes good economic sense for PLN and
the country; by reducing PLN's costs, human resource requirements and need for further organizational
expansion and geographical extension; while also providing the opportunity for the development of village
level managerial and technical resources and a labor pool. Four patterns/schemes ("polas") and two types



15
of management service agreements (MSAs) are possible under existing arrangements, differentiated either
by the scope of activities contracted or by the ownership and functions carried out:
(a)        Pola 1: the cooperative handles customer administration functions, such as meter reading
and bill collection, on a fee-for-service basis;
(b)        Pola II: the cooperative installs house wiring as a contractor of the customer, with a license
and fee schedule approved by PLN;
(c)        Pola III: the cooperative purchases electricity in bulk, from PLN, and takes over all
distribution functions as well as ownership of all such assets;
(d)        Pola IV:  the cooperative generates and distributes electricity, independently of PLN,
subject to MME regulation;
(e)        MSA-erid: the cooperative handles a wider range of customer and technical functions, such
as line maintenance, simple fault clearing, tree trimming, street light maintenance, based
on a management services arrangement that identifies the geographical service area of
responsibility, specific functions and work elements to be undertaken, performance
indicators, and the fee structure; and
(f)        MSA-isolated diesel: the cooperative handles the daily operation and management of a
remote (isolated) diesel power plant based generation and distribution system, including all
customer administration functions, with asset ownership still in PLN's hands.
2.18       Currently, an estimated 2,260 KUDs are under contract with PLN, operating 1,800 Pola
I schemes and 460 Pola I&II schemes; and covering about 11,000 villages out of the 29,000 villages
electrified by PLN. About 60% of these KUDs are situated Outside Java. Further, there are about 90
MSA-grid and 20 MSA-diesel agreements in operation. Field assessments of the Pola Program to date
indicate that by and large, the experience is viewed very positively by PLN as well as the KUDs. PLN
reports these activities to be cost effective and from a longer term outlook it helps mitigate the need for
PLN to expand its staff and organizational resources that are already stretched to the limit. The main
benefits to the KUDs have been: (i) increased fee income -- in 1993, through Pola I schemes alone,
KUDs collected a total of about Rp. 93 billion (US$44 million) per month in electric bills for PLN and
received fee income of about Rp. 1.4 billion (an average of US$279 monthly income per KUD) ; (ii)
increased income from membership fees as well as the provision of electricity related value-added
services, such as a convenience facility for bill payment at home; (iii) employment; and (iv) less
quantifiable but important benefits such as improved ability to serve the community and enhanced status
by association with a "high tech" and professional organization such as PLN that is generally held in high
esteem.
2.19       At present, the major constraints to expansion of the Pola/MSA Programs are the weak skills
base of KUD personnel, a fee structure that in many instances is inadequate to attract qualified personnel,
inadequate support and facilitation by MOC staff from headquarters and particularly from provincial staff
at the provincial (KANWIL) and district (KANDEP) levels, administrative procedures and management
approach at PLN that sometimes cause excessive delays in identifying, preparing the groundwork and
implementing Pola schemes, and limitations in PLN's training capacity to train the necessary numbers
of KUD personnel for the very activities they would subsequently takeover. The Pola Program component



16
of the proposed project calls for a significant expansion of the programn to be facilitated by easing the
major program constraints today, and with a longer term vision in mind as follows.
2.20       Experience with the Pola/MSA programs to date underpins the conclusion that an effective
approach for RE delivery is for PLN to devolve itself of as many labor intensive distribution system
activities in as any areas as is feasible; where the KUDs or other organizations are demonstrably able to
efficiently take over such activities and at a cost lower than PLN's cost. This positive experience also
underlies the longer term vision that opportunities can be proactively created over time, whereby an entire
geographical area serving somewhere between 20 to 50 thousand consumers and with the right
socioeconomic structure will lend itself to the creation of Pola Ills. This would be achieved by the
consolidation of the service areas of successful KUDs that are already well established in that area and
successful in running MSA-grid agreements and Pola I&II schemes. Several KUDs could band together
into a "super-KUD" or another such over-arching local electricity organization, and assume responsibility
for managing all distribution in the service area -- including ownership of assets -- and buying electricity
from PLN at a bulk supply tariff based on the economic cost of supply. Such an evolutionary strategy
for RE delivery by increasing local participation, if effectively carried out, could be expected to result
in substantially redefining PLN's role over time to that of an efficient bulk power supplier in as many
rural areas as is feasible; and perhaps providing on a fee basis, specialized manpower or technical skills
as needed to the Pola III management. Naturally, the pace of any such development in the emergence of
Pola III schemes will vary by region, depending upon the human resource potential in that area,
socioeconomic and demographic characteristics and level of development. Advance indication of progress
in this direction would become available over time, through actual experience with performance in Pola
I&II and MSA schemes.
2.21       Promotion of productive uses of electricity. A primary objective of the RE program in
Indonesia is to contribute to the development of economic activities in rural areas through the productive
uses of electricity. Productive uses are essential to establish the long-term economic viability and
sustainability of rural electrification, particularly in the Outer Islands, where costs are high. Increased
productive uses contribute both to higher benefits and lower costs. Load development increases capacity
utilization of high fixed cost investment and therefore enhances the economic rate of return, as long as
the tariff covers at least the variable cost of electricity generation.
2.22       A long recognized issue in Indonesia is that the productive use of PLN electricity in many
rural areas is below achievable potential. New uses, beyond lighting, do not appear to increase
substantially following electrification, and changeover by existing productive users of alternative energy
sources to PLN tends to be slow in coming. A key constraint to productive uses has been that insufficient
information has been provided to users and potential users. This constraint was addressed on a pilot
basis, by the Small Business Services (SBS) component under RE I, with special promotion programs in
two of PLN's regions outside Java and in one region on Java. Working in collaboration with NGOs,
PLN actively informed existing and potential small business customers of the opportunities, methods and
advantages of using electricity for productive purposes. The SBS pilot program experience to date is
positive and results indicate that such marketing efforts which provide price and technical information
are key to increasing the number of rural productive users of PLN electricity. Under the Rural Business
Services (RBS) component of the proposed project, this approach would be expanded to all 14 of PLN's
regions using a more targeted and efficient approach which draws upon the lessons learned under RE I.
Annex 4.5 contains more details about the SBS program experience to date under the RE I, and the
design of the RBS component.



17
2.23        In addition to the constraints to increase in productive uses noted above, in many instances
rural businesses face entry and expansion barriers, most notably PLN tariff code practices: (i) the lack
of a provision for the installment payment for PLN charges for new connections or for increasing
contracted power that many firms find to be too high and; (ii) PLN's fixed demand charges, and
relatively high charges for increases in contracted power (kVA), that have reduced the effectiveness of
the SBS efforts. Removal of these barriers, that are not considered to be the primary constraints to
increased productive uses at present, is being addressed within the overall context of the Bank's policy
dialogue and the Government's agenda for sector reform, whose implementation should bring about
conditions under which the Government will find it easier to undertake restructuring of these and other
inefficient features of the present tariff structure (para 5.9).
Strategy for Future
2.24        The record of past accomplishments in expanding electricity coverage is impressive.
However, the un-finished task ahead is more formidable by several orders-of-magnitude, and the
institutional, financial and technical challenges that will have to be faced along the way cut to the very
core issue of efficiency, and RE program sustainability. Significant changes from past practices,
amounting to fundamental restructuring of the institutional and other design and delivery aspects of the
RE program are unquestionably needed. Sustaining the historical pace, let alone accelerating the pace of
growth in expanding access to electricity will require increasing the efficiency of the program -- technical,
managerial and institutional -- changes that significantly reduce the future cost of the program on the one
hand and enhance the benefits to the rural population on the other.
2.25        The unftnished task ahead. In the course of the last fifteen years spanning three
REPELITAs (1978/79-1993/94), and starting almost from ground zero, electricity access has reached
about 29,000 rural villages and about 8 million rural households receive PLN supply. To put this
accomplishment in perspective, the RE program has yet to pass even the half way mark in terms of
village coverage -- with about 31,000 villages remaining to be electrified -- and has only recently crossed
the quarter-way milestone in terms of the more important statistic of household electrification, with about
20 million households still waiting, literally and figuratively speaking, in the dark. Extrapolating at the
electrification pace reached in the recent past, and even with some provision for further acceleration, this
would imply that another 12 to 15 years would be required to achieve nearly 100 village electrification.
A more sobering implication is that it will be another 30 years at the very least before nearly all rural
homes begin to experience and share in the benefits derived from electricity service; assuming a modest
population growth rate of 1.5% per year.
2.26        Cost reduction: the continuing technical challenge. Maintaining the pace of growth of
the RE program will require all the technical ingenuity and organizational commitment to aggressively
seek and implement lower cost equipment and network designs, and delivery methods. As the RE
program pushes the geographical envelope of coverage further out, the financing constraint imposed
pressures to implement low cost reticulation designs for extensification and intensification loom large.
On the extensive margin, line lengths and material transportation costs in particular, are increasing at a
rapid rate, in order to gain access to more remote villages that require traversal over increasingly rugged
terrain. A similar challenge of high marginal costs must be confronted on the "intensive margin"
(intensification). This is best illustrated with an example, as in the case of the island of Bali where " 100
percent" electrification was achieved last year. Yet, about 40 percent of households in Bali will still not
have electricity and many are unlikely to receive PLN supply for a long time. Typically, these households
are clustered in hamlets (kampungs) of 30 to 50+ households, situated at distances ranging from 5 to



18
15 + kilometers from the nearest connecting point on the 20 kV grid. The capital costs of conventional
schemes for extending the grid to such individual clusters are extremely high; when coupled with low
consumer and load densities that result in low capacity utilization, most such situations pose an
uneconomic proposition using conventional approaches.
2.27       Grid extensification and intensification based on conventional network designs, equipment
standards, and implementation practices that can be justified in urban areas -- such as three phase MV
line extensions with 100% strength spun and pre-stressed concrete poles, and elaborate protection, and
sectionalizing equipment at substations -- are prohibitively expensive in the RE context. The proposed
project design incorporates several equipment and network design changes from present PLN practices
that will generate significant cost reductions. The proposed project also incorporates five pilot projects
to test the adaptability of "low tech" low cost network designs and equipment for RE extensification and
intensification, and construction management and procurement practices that have been successfully used
elsewhere, to reduce the costs of rural electrification; and if proven to be successful in Indonesia, these
designs will be incorporated as standard practice in the future RE program. Annex 4.2 provides a brief
description of the equipment and network design changes to be introduce in the proposed project, as well
as brief descriptions of the pilot project components.
2.28       RE-specific institutional restructuring. The single most important change that has the
potential for increasing the efficiency of the RE program by a significant quantum is in the manner in
which PLN is presently organized and manages RE.  Realizing cost reductions through technical
modifications (paras 2.26 and 2.27) will only go so far. Moreover, under the present organizational setup
and management model, incentives for pursuing technical cost reductions are weak. With changed
incentives, the RE managers themselves will become more creative in seeking solutions that reduce the
unit cost of RE delivery.
2.29       The commingling of management responsibilities for two distinct missions,i.e., RE and non-
RE operations, that require different technical solutions and management approach and culture, and with
a vastly divergent financial incentives, compromises the performance of PLN's RE as well as its urban-
industrial operations. RE operations by their very nature -- smaller and dispersed loads, more labor
intensive, low capacity utilization and relatively speaking representing the "low tech" end of the electricity
supply business -- impose a significantly higher investment and operating cost burden, and hence require
a subsidy. By contrast, PLN's urban-industrial electricity operations are largely commercial in nature
and profitable. As rural electrification coverage expands and with each new household connection, the
financial pressures on PLN increase, creating direct conflicts in objectives. With respect to its urban
operations, the strategic issues for management center around market share, profitability, customer
satisfaction, service unbundling, among others. By contrast, in the case of RE, the strategic issues
concern obligation to serve, cost minimization, and program sustainability. Under the present mixed
management model and an environment marked by conflicting priorities, rural electrification inevitably
takes a back seat. With the span of control of each top manager spread between RE and non-RE, the span
of attention given to RE is limited. Although government mandated physical targets for RE have been
met, efficiency is compromised. Very importantly, the long-term sustainability of the RE program cannot
be taken for granted by simply continuing to follow the "top-down" and mixed responsibility model for
designing, implementing and managing RE; a model that has stretched well beyond its limits of
effectiveness.
2.30       A range of institutional restructuring options are available for undertaking RE. Taking into
account the specific circumstances of PLN and drawing upon the experience with organizational and



19
management models and practices in countries that have rapidly and efficiently carried out RE -- such
as Thailand and Malaysia -- would point to an organizational and management model for PLN wherein:
(i) at the headquarters, consolidation of the responsibility for the full range of functions necessary to
undertake RE effectively within a single management unit and single point of responsibility, with
associated authority and accountability established at the highest levels and; (ii) in each of the eleven
operating regions and the four distribution units, establishing distinct and stand alone operating units, each
with a sharply defined separation -- management, operational and financial and accounting -- between the
RE and non-RE missions, with appropriate transfer prices set for any inter-unit transactions. Under such
a model, the RE units would report to the consolidated RE management unit at the headquarters. The
creation of such a distinct institutional and management sub-structure within PLN, accountable solely for
