carrying out RE, and with direct and transparent access to the resources required to carry out its mission
in an efficient manner (para 1.28), will spawn a management and staff sub-culture that is driven by a
singular and well defined sense of mission. Such an organizational unit would feel empowered to take
the initiative in implementing the policies and actions necessary to accelerate the pace and enhance the
efficiency of the RE expansion program to their maximum potential. Details of the RE-specific
reorganization and management model are being presently worked out under PLN Restructuring Plan
effort financed by a Bank TA (para 1.27). The proposed project provides for TA to implement a time
bound action plan for RE-specific reorganization to be agreed to between the Bank and PLN, taking into
consideration the recommendations to be finalized in the context of the ongoing PLN Restructuring Plan.
2.31        Increasing private sector participation - published power purchase tariff. A key
efficiency objective of GOI and PLN for the power sector is to ensure that the generation supply mix in
PLN's various grids provide electricity at least cost. PLN cannot afford to neglect the potential for cost
savings -- fuel and capital -- resulting from purchases of electricity at a price equal to or lower than the
cost of PLN supply at the margin, i.e., not exceeding PLN's "avoided cost". The potential for such
efficiency gains arise because of the unique circumstances favoring the potential suppliers, such as: access
to a specific low cost power project, access to a low cost fuel resource, or where excess electricity can
be produced as a by-product (e.g., mini-hydro, mini-geothermal, biomass, cogeneration, excess captive
generation).
2.32        In keeping with the Government's stated goal of substantially increasing private sector
participation in the power sector (para 1.23) -- which the Bank fully supports -- a key policy component
of the proposed project is the creation of a sustainable and efficient small power market and to maximize
the volume and number of such transactions (contracts) in this market. Central to the realization of such
a market is the implementation of the small power purchase tariff derived from the following principles:
(i) published purchase price not to exceed PLN's avoided cost; and (ii) a standard simple power purchase
contract to apply for all transactions. Immediate corollaries of these principles are, respectively; (i) the
published tariff to apply to all small power purchases by PLN (i.e., the power purchase tariffs would not
be differentiated based on the technology or fuel source employed by the seller, whether it is captive
power, cogenerated power, "excess" power, renewable power, hydro, biomass etc., and the sellers cost
structure or "profit potential" would not be a consideration in the transaction); and (ii) there are to be no
negotiations (i.e., neither the tariff nor the contract clauses are negotiable). Negotiations in the case of
small power contracts typically: (a) result in a protracted contracting process, (b) inherently impose a
higher and crushing burden on the small developer because of the asymmetry in  information and
expertise available to the two parties, and (c) introduce the risk of manipulation by both sides.
Furthermore, as long as the purchase price does not exceed PLN's avoided cost, the resultant outcome
is to the advantage of all parties concerned and in the nation's interest. During negotiations for the
proposed project, agreement was reached that PLN shall, by June 30 (or such other date as may be



20
agreed by the Bank), every year commencing in 1995, revise and publish the small power purchase tariff
schedule including the form of the standardpower purchase contract in accordance with the methodology
agreed to by the Bank.
2.33       Understanding has also been reached with PLN as to the necessity of designing and
implementing a monitoring program that will enable periodic evaluation of the experience of the published
small power purchase tariff following implementation of the tariff. Such a data base will include, inter
alia, "expressions of interest" and contract signing under the program and essential information such as,
name and address of the owner, initial date of contact, technical and operating parameters of the project,
type of contract sought or signed, date of signing, date of first power delivery, etc.. The database and
monitoring system would provide a basis for evaluation of the resultant power transactions that take place
-- and those that did not -- which would point the way to desirable revisions and refinements to the tariff
and power purchase contract in order to enhance the efficiency, magnitude and scope of the small power
purchase market transactions.
2.34       Bulk supply tariff. A necessary condition for the entry of cooperatives and other private
sector operators in electricity distribution and retailing -- through Pola III schemes -- is the introduction
of a transfer price/bulk supply tariff at which the distributor would buy from PLN. Such a tariff would
be based on PLN's economic cost of supply, and the specific regulatory provisions governing its approval
and implementation would be under the jurisdiction of the DGEED which also has the responsibility for
approving retail tariffs of cooperatives. Agreement was reached during negotiations for the proposed
project that PLN shall, by June 30 (or such other date as may be agreed by the Bank), every year
commencing in 1995, revise and publish the bulk supply tariff schedule in accordance with the
methodology agreed to by the Bank.
2.35       Simply implementing a bulk supply tariff, however, will not trigger private sector
participation since the government controls retail tariffs as well, and under present conditions, all such
distributors would be caught in a "price squeeze" that make electricity distribution a financially non-viable
proposition. Therefore, it is essential for the government to clearly articulate policies and supporting
regulations on the handling of situations and conditions under which subsidies would be granted. The
source and mechanics of such a subsidy transfer is being worked out by DGEED in consultation with the
Government, bearing in mind that transparency is important in the provision of any such subsidies. The
DGEED will also establish a clear policy, following close consultation with the Governrment, whether the
subsidy would be directed to PLN -- in effect subsidizing the BST for a given retailer -- or given to the
distributor directly; and in which event the method for calculating the magnitude of such a subsidy.
Development of the policy framework and regulatory approach for handling this specific issue of
implementation of the bulk supply tariff and the design and implementation of detailed regulations for
regulatory oversight of both, the bulk supply tariff and the published small power purchase tariff, will
be undertaken within the broader "Regulatory Reform Study" (para 1.30). During negotiations for the
proposed project, it was agreed that the Government shall: (a) prepare and, by June 30, 1995, adopt
a policy regarding implementation of the power purchase tariff and bulk supply tariff satisfactory to the
Bank including policies governing the granting of any subsidies related to implementation of the bulk
supply tariff, rules and procedures for determining such subsidies and how such subsidies are to be
financed and directed in a transparent manner; and (b) draft, and by December 31, 1995, promulgate
rules and regulations concerning oversight of the power purchase tariff and bulk supply tariff
implementation, in form acceptable to the Bank.



21
2.36       Renewable energy development. PLN owns about 2,000 MW of diesel generating capacity
scattered through Indonesia, designed as a primary means to sustain RE growth in the Outer Islands.
Much of the diesel plant is under-utilized, with capacity factors averaging less than 30%. Following the
Government's decision to discontinue subsidies for most petroleum products, earlier this year, the price
of high speed diesel fuel (HSD) was raised on January 7, 1993 to Rp. 360 liter, in approximately parity
to economic/border prices; thereby raising the costs of diesel generation substantially. Under these
conditions, it has become increasingly important to consider alternative ways of optimizing electricity
supply sources, by utilizing cost effective renewable resources that are environmentally superior to fossil
fuels based generation.
2.37       Indonesia's renewable energy experience has generally been very positive, although in terms
of decentralized and small-scale applications, it has remained mainly a demonstration effort. Over 10,000
solar home systems (SHS) have been installed since 1988; as a strategy of electrification while waiting
access to grid connection. Numerous studies by DJLEB, PLN, BPPT, demonstrate the significant
potential of renewable energy. However, renewable energy projects need to be developed within the
framework of a least-cost rural electrification strategy, rather than as a series of individual demonstration
projects. A cohesive renewable energy development strategy to set goals, plans and policies is also
needed to guide donor funds towards priority investments. The RE Master Plan being prepared under
the Bank-funded TA component of the ongoing RE I project will establish for each region, the
economically optimal village electrification targets to be ultimately achieved in each region by grid
reticulation, as well as the corresponding year-to-year electrification targets for each region. Areas not
planned to be covered by grid extensions for at least ten years will be identified as well in this process,
and would be potential candidates for employing cost effective and decentralized generation alternatives
especially renewable based generation. A technical assistance component of the proposed project will
facilitate the preparation of a Renewable Energy Development Plan (para 4.6).
2.38       Most immediately, PLN can take advantage of the considerable design and development
work done so far in the areas of mini-hydro and mini-geothermal project development; projects that can
supply power to rural areas at economic costs that are lower than the cost of diesel-based power supply.
The proposed project includes construction of five mini-hydropower plants in Sulawesi and Eastern
Indonesia, and well test drilling for two mini-geothermal sites. If the test well drilling proves to be
successful as per expectations, financing for the mini-geothermal powerplants at those sites could be
considered for provision in a subsequent lending operation by the Bank.
Bank's Past Experience
2.39       Overview. The Bank group has played a very active role in the development of the power
sector in Indonesia. Since 1969, it has provided about $4.1 billion for the power sector, through three
IDA Credits, 18 loans and one supplemental loan. A list of past lending operations is provided in Annex
2.3. Project Performance Audit Reports (PPARs) and Project Completion Reports (PCRs) on the first
14 projects have concluded that the Bank has made positive contributions towards the development of the
Indonesian power sector over the past 20 years by improving PLN's project implementation, operational
and managerial capabilities and helping Indonesia meet critical demands for electricity. These reports
attribute the success of power operations in Indonesia to the facts that: (i) there was a long-term vision
for the development of the sector and this vision was shared by the Bank, Government and PLN; (ii) the
vision was translated into a long-term strategy which was resolutely pursued; and (iii) a reasonable
balance was struck between the physical and the institutional development components of the projects.
Taken altogether, these projects have assisted in the expansion of generation, transmission and distribution



22
facilities and rural electrification and institutional development of PLN. The technical assistance provided
by the Bank has also strengthened PLN's technical and management capabilities in engineering, project
management capabilities, distribution and rural electrification planning and management, and corporate
and financial planning. As a result of these efforts, PLN's implementation capacity and operational
efficiency have increased significantly, whereby it now handles an investment program of about US$ 3.5
billion per year, and connects over one million new customers annually.
2.40       Experience with rural electrification. The Bank's first and ongoing lending operation for
rural electrification (RE I, Loan 3180-IND), provides US$329 million to finance the bulk of the foreign
exchange costs of a time slice of the RE development plan through FY 94/95, and is scheduled to close
in June 1995. The Bank's objectives in the RE I project derive directly from the findings of the Rural
Electrification Review (Report 6144-IND, 1986). In addition to helping finance implementation of the
physical program elements, the key objectives of RE I are centrally aimed at: (i) development of a RE
Master Plan and upgrading of PLN's planning, technical and manpower capability in this area; (ii)
reducing the costs of future RE development by, (a) promoting the development of appropriate technical
standards for equipment performance and network design and construction practices, (b) reducing PLN's
costs and provide an opportunity for the development of village-based local participation in the form of
managerial, technical and labor resources (i.e., "Pola I & II" and "MSA" sub-contracting arrangements);
(iii) increasing the benefits of future RE development by development and demonstration of a pilot
program for increasing the productive uses of electricity by small businesses; and(iv) technical assistance
to develop methods to establish financial transparency in PLN's accounting of the costs of rural
electrification, as well as assistance for training personnel from PLN and the Ministry of Cooperatives
(MOC) to support the RE program.
2.41       By and large, the RE I project's scorecard vis-a-vis its objectives is favorable.  The
problems encountered in earlier power projects relating to the need to reduce implementation delays by
the timely appointment of consultants, and speedier procurement, still persist, though to a greatly reduced
extent. The Master Plan work-in-progress represents a major step forward in rationalizing RE planning
in Indonesia, and is directly helping rationalize the future sequencing of network (grid) reticulation;
including the allocation of national electrification target by region, extensification and intensification
within each region, and village selection in the proposed project. Assessing the performance of the RE
I project has to be seen in the context of a gradual and continuous learning process. Thus, changes in
equipment standards and performance specifications and in network design, facilitated by the RE I
project, provide the basis for significant cost reductions in the equipment and material costs of the
physical program in the next stage of rural electrification under the proposed project and advanced
knowledge coupled with PLN's evolved state of technical readiness set the stage for testing pilot
distribution projects in the proposed project that would form the basis for further cost reduction in RE
III, possibly with Bank financing. Experience gained with local participation under RE I -- in the form
of the Pola program -- has been generally quite positive, and provides the basis for seeking a more
ambitious and larger scale promotion of this concept. This would include introduction of a bulk supply
tariff for the sale of electricity by PLN to third parties engaged in electricity distribution (Pola III). In
addition, experience under RE I with the high cost diesel operations of PLN points the way for
aggressively seeking lower cost sources of generation including from private sources; a necessary
condition for which is the introduction of a published small power purchase tariff based on PLN's
"avoided cost" of supply and a standardized power purchase contract to apply for such purchases.



23
3
THE BORROWER AND THE IMPLEMENTING AGENCIES
3.1       The proposed loan would be made to the Government of Indonesia (GOI). The
proceeds of the proposed loan are to be made available to the State Electricity Corporation
(PLN), through an onlending arrangement, and to MOC; which are responsible for the
implementation of the respective components of the proposed project. PLN, the sole government-
owned national power utility, has been the beneficiary of Bank Group lending to the power
sector in Indonesia since 1969, including the first and ongoing rural electrification project (RE
I, Loan 3180-IND, paras 2.40 and 2.41). MOC, also a beneficiary of the RE I project, will
implement its project component through the Directorate of Industry and Electric Power
Promotion of its Directorate General of Urban Cooperatives Development.
PLN's Organization
3.2       PLN was established as a public corporation (Perum) under a Presidential Decree
of 1972, that confers upon it the responsibility and obligation to manage Indonesia's power
system, including the generation, transmission and distribution of electricity, and the planning,
construction and operation of electricity supply facilities (para 1. 8). At present, PLN is managed
in a centralized command and control manner, by a Board of Directors headed by a President
Director who is appointed by the President of the Republic, and is accountable to MME. The
Board of Directors have, both, policy setting and supervisory responsibilities.  PLN's
organization chart is shown in Annex 3.1.
3.3        For the Java-Bali system, the operational responsibility rests with the managers of
two generation and transmission units, the Java-Bali load dispatch center, and four distribution
units. For the Outer Islands, operational responsibility rests with the managers of PLN's eleven
operating regions and the special region of Batam island. Under the Regional Manager, there
are four primary departments with Deputy Managers in charge of Operations, Construction,
Finance, and Administration. Each Region has a number of branches and sub-branches. The
branch manager is responsible for service at the customer level, and reports directly to the
Regional Manager. The Construction Divisions in the Regions have responsibility for distribution
lines and small generation facilities (under 1 MW), with major construction and procurement
decisions being the responsibility of a number of project managers who report directly to the
Board. There is no real separation of management responsibility for RE and non-RE operations
and functions in the regions, the distribution units or at the Board level.
Institutional Change
3.4       PLN's managing and operating capabilities have increased along with energy sales;
it has effectively managed a large investment program, and constructed and operated large coal-
fired plants in Java-Bali. Annex 3.2 shows some of the performance indicators for PLN for the



24
years 1981/82 to 1992/93; they indicate significant achievements in several areas. However,
when compared with best utility practices, there is considerable scope for improvement. PLN
has reached the limits of its effectiveness under the present organizational structure and
management systems, and a detailed plan for restructuring PLN is now underway and will be
finalized by December 1994 (paras 1.27 and 1.28).
3.5        With centralized control and most decisions requiring Board approval, the Managers
of PLN's Regions in the Outer Islands and the four distribution units in Java-Bali have limited
planning and operational flexibility and discretion; correspondingly, their accountability for
operational efficiency is also limited. As a result, the Managers have few opportunities or
incentives to initiate steps to improve the quality and level of service and/or reduce costs by
introducing efficiency measures. These systemic problems are further compounded by the lack
of a clear separation of responsibility and accountability -- management, technical performance,
financial -- at headquarters and in the regions, between PLN's RE and non-RE operations;
despite the fact that these two missions are quite distinct in terms of the technical, commercial,
and customer administration challenges. With decisions on standards, planning, on-ground
implementation, procurement, monitoring, and responsibility for overseeing construction in-field
residing in so many different functions and operational units, global cost and efficiency of the
RE program are compromised; since the outcome is based on a management system of
coordinated and collective responsibility and conflicting objectives inherent between RE and non-
RE operations under the present organizational and management structure (paras 2.28 through
2.30).
3.6        During negotiations for the proposed project, agreement was reached with the
Government and PLN that they shall (a) furnish to the Bank, (i) such periodic reports concerning
the reorganization of PLN as reasonably requested by the Bank, and (ii) a draft time bound
corporate reorganization and restructuring action plan, based on terms of reference agreed with
the Bank, for PLN to operate according to best utility practices (including an action plan for
restructuring specific to rural electrification); (b) discuss the said report(s) and draft action plan
with the Bank; (c) finalize the said action plan taking into account the comments, if any, thereon
by the Bank, and (d) by June 30, 1995, take appropriate steps to implement the said action plan
taking into consideration the comments, if any, thereon by the Bank.
Staff Development and Training
3.7        As of March 31, 1993, PLN had nearly 56,000 employees, of whom about 46,000
are permanent staff. PLN has no difficulty in recruiting staff educated at levels up to technical
high school; however, less than 5% of the staff have university degrees. PLN's Center for
Education and Training (PUSDIKLAT), plays an important role in its efforts to upgrade the
skills of its technical and administrative staff, and manages ten training centers with a staff of
over 500, including over 200 trained instructors. These efforts have been largely successful. In
1992/93, about 8,000 staff received training of various durations, including about 200 staff
trained abroad. PLN also expends considerable effort in training personnel from rural areas in
labor intensive functions such as meter reading and bill collection, house wiring, and more
technical functions such as low voltage line maintenance, and diesel power plant operations.
Once trained, these individuals are sub-contracted through the various Pola arrangements and
Management Service Agreements (MSAs) between PLN and KUDs, to undertake the



25
corresponding functions on a fee basis, that would otherwise require the hiring of full time PLN
staff and at a higher cost (paras 2.17 and 2.18).
3.8        The Bank has supported PLN's training activities and human resources development
efforts, including the establishment of several training centers. Current efforts being supported
by the Bank include training in the areas of diesel power plant efficiency improvements,
distribution management, corporate and financial planning, and general accounting and cash
management. Additional training activities related to rural electrification include RE planning and
best RE practices in other countries.
Accounting and Budgeting
3.9        PLN's accounting system, installed in the early 1970s, follows generally accepted
accounting principles, and normal utility practices, but it needs upgrading to keep pace with the
growing complexity and volume of accounting transactions. The accounting system needs to
produce more accurate information in a timely manner for decision-making purposes. Also,
PLN's internal procedures for preparing budgets and monitoring actual expenditures need to be
strengthened through better coordination among and participation by the various functional
directorates and regional offices in the process, to enhance the budget's value as a management
tool. Since 1987, PLN has been implementing an action plan to improve its accounting practices
and functions. PLN is also attempting to improve coordination between its departments and its
regional offices in the areas of budget preparation, and monitoring and evaluation of
performance. Technical assistance is provided under the Power Sector Efficiency Project (Loan
3097-IND) to improve the accounting system. The Rural Electrification Project (Loan 3180-
IND) provides technical assistance to continue to enhance the system for the segregation of
PLN's accounting of its RE operations.
Billing and Collections
3.10       PLN's bills are computerized and prepared monthly at the Jakarta headquarters; for
the Outer Islands, the bills are prepared at the branch level, and printed by the Region's Finance
Division. Overall collection performance is generally satisfactory. The receivables position of
general consumer accounts has been good, as it has been kept below one and one-half months
of the yearly billing. The overdues from government users (including the Armed Forces and
local governments), which account for about 10 percent of PLN's total sales, have also been
maintained to under two months' billing.
Audit and Internal Control
3.11       PLN's accounts are required by its charter to be audited by Government auditors.
The audits are carried out following generally accepted accounting practices and standards. In
the audit reports for 1989 through 1993 there were no qualifications. As a result of joint efforts
by PLN and its auditors, the time required for completing PLN's audits has been reduced
significantly over the last three years. In recent years, the accounts have generally been finalized
within three months after the closing of the fiscal year and the audited accounts were available
within four months of the close of the fiscal year. The audit covenant under Loan 3602-IND,
that requires PLN to submit to the Bank its audited financial statements and the report of such
audits no later than six months after the end of each fiscal year, was also agreed to under the



26
proposed project. In addition, PLN would furnish to the Bank (i) a copy of its unaudited annual
accounts whenever they are sent to the auditors; and (ii) its segregated financial and operating
information on rural activities within six months after the end of each fiscal year.
3.12       PLN has an effective internal audit group which reports to the President Director
for various special investigations, in addition to keeping general and project accounting matters
under review. The Bank is discussing with Government and PLN the desirability of having
PLN's accounts audited by internationally reputed commercial accounting firms, independently
of Government auditors. Such commercial audits would not only ensure timely availability of
PLN's audited accounts, but would also provide greater comfort to the capital markets, which
PLN would be accessing increasingly following its recent conversion to a Persero, for funds to
finance its capital expenditures.
Insurance
3.13       PLN currently carries transit and marine insurance on equipment and materials in
transit. Projects under construction are covered by the contractor's all risk and erection
insurance. Fire and other hazards on most of the assets in operation are self-insured. In view
of the geographical spread of PLN's assets, any single loss would be relatively small by
comparison with total assets and operations, and this policy has been considered satisfactory by
the Bank. However, with some very large projects having been completed in recent years and
others now under implementation, there would be a greater concentration of assets in certain
locations than in the past. An understanding was reached under Loans 3097-IND and 3098-IND
that major works, plant and equipment at large power projects would be insured with outside
agencies. Accordingly, PLN is currently carrying commercial insurance for major operating
plants and equipment at several large power projects totalling about 4,900 MW, in and outside
Java. PLN is also considering proposals for creating a fund for self-insurance of the remaining
assets.
The Ministry of Cooperatives and Small Enterprises Development (MOC)
3.14       MOCs involvement in rural electrification began in 1978, when it served as the
implementing agency for a USAID-financed project to set up demonstration electric cooperatives
in three rural areas in Lampung (South Sumatra), Lombok (West Nusa Tenggara) and Luwu
(South Sulawesi), which are the three major Pola IV schemes (paras 2.11 and 2.12). MOC's
involvement in rural electrification is currently handled through its Directorate General of Urban
Cooperatives Development (Direktorat Pembangunan Koperasi Perkotaan (DJPKP), that among
other things, took over the functions of the former Directorate for the Promotion of Electric
Cooperatives and Project Development office. Through its Directorate for Industry and Electric
Power promotion, MOC also collaborates with PLN for the implementation of the joint MOC-
MME program to promote the role of village cooperatives in rural electrification. MOC is also
a member of the RE Steering Committee chaired by the DGEED to ensure inter-agency
coordination and cooperation in matters related to the Government's rural electrification
program. An organization chart of MOC is shown in Annex 3.3.
3.15       MOC's primary role in RE is to promote and facilitate local participation by village
cooperative organizations (KUDs), in Pola I-IV and MSA contracts with PLN. MOC's field staff
at the provincial (KANDEP) and district (KANWIL) levels are key to carrying out this mission.



27
These staff typically: (i) help monitor progress and facilitate resolution of any problems with the
ongoing Pola and MSA contracts between PLN and KUDs by bringing both parties together; (ii)
identify prospective new KUDs interested in entering into such contracts with PLN; and (iii)
facilitate the required follow-up steps in such instances, including discussions with the PLN
regional managers, and facilitating the necessary training of KUD personnel at PLN's regional
training centers (UDIKLAT). However, their ability to carry out this role effectively is hampered
by the insufficient knowledge about the various facets of the electricity supply. A TA on human
resource development, financed under the ongoing RE I project is providing training for MOC's
headquarters and KANWIL and KANDEP staff on the basics of electricity supply; technical
aspects, safety, financial and accounting systems, customer administration, and management.
This basic grounding will strengthen the institutional capacity of MOC to facilitate expansion of
the Pola Program component of the proposed project.
3.16       Technical assistance would be provided to MOC under the proposed project for:
(i) implementation support for the Pola Program expansion to be carried out; and (ii) policy
support to undertake the detailed feasibility studies and advanced preparation required for
potentially setting up Pola III schemes in up to two promising areas, Central Java and South
Sulawesi.



28
4
THE PROJECT
RE Development Plan.
4.1        PLN has prepared year-by-year RE plans for REPELITA VI (1994/95-1998/99) targets for
rural electrification, that call for the connection of about 6.5 million new consumers and 18,600 villages
over the five year period. The physical program underlying this expansion involves the installation of
about 62,000 kmc (circuit-kIn) of MV lines, 105,000 kcm of LV power lines, 3000 MVA of transformer
capacity, and 40 MW of additional diesel capacity, requiring capital expenditures of about US$1.7 billion
(in 1993 prices) over the five year period. Details of this plan are shown in Annex 4.1. The physical
program requirements and estimated expenditures are to be finalized by June 1995, under the ongoing
Bank-financed technical assistance for development of the overall RE Master Plan for Indonesia, under
the RE I project. The RE Master Plan will establish for each region, based upon a computerized RE
Planning System (REPS), the year-by-year village electrification levels to be achieved, extensions of the
various regional grids and in the process will also determine an economic rank order of priority for
electrifying the remaining 30,000 villages; including those villages where the grid extension is not
economically feasible for the foreseeable future and alternate decentralized means would be necessary for
electrification.
Project Objectives and Scope
4.2        The specific objectives of the proposed RE II project, besides continuing Bank support for
the broader reform agenda for the power sector initiated under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power
Project, are to:
(a)   advance the efforts initiated under RE I project to place the rural electrification
program in Indonesia on an efficient and sustainable footing by: (i) reducing the unit
cost burden of RE through least-cost planning, lowering equipment and construction
costs, and by restructuring PLN's organizational structure and management processes
specific to its RE mission; (ii) enhancing the efficiency of RE delivery by increasing
and strengthening local participation; and (iii) maximizing the economic benefits of
RE by promoting productive uses of electricity in rural areas;
(b)   expand the coverage and supply of electricity to rural areas through a time slice
investment program;
(c)   establish incentives for private sector and local cooperatives to take an increasingly
larger share of RE distribution and alternative energy development within the
framework of a least-cost RE Master Plan, specifically by implementing: (i) a
published power purchase tariff based on PLN's "avoided cost of supply" and
standard contract for purchases by PLN from small power producers; (ii) a bulk



29
supply tariff for wholesale sales of power by PLN to potential retail distributors of
electricity based on PLN's economic cost of supply;
(d)   continue PLN's  institutional development to  undertake  RE  planning  and
implementation, especially at the regional level; and
(e)   undertake pilot distribution programs that have the potential for further reducing the
costs of RE extensification and intensification, with a view to introducing them as
regular components in the Government's rural electrification program, possibly
supported by future Bank lending.
Project Description
4.3        The principal components of the proposed project are as follows:
(a)   electrification of 7000 rural villages in the second and third years of Repelita VI
(95/96-96/97); comprising about 2.1 million new consumers -- 1.8 million from
extensification and 350,000 from intensification -- through the construction of an
estimated 28,000 kmc of MV lines and 35,000 kmc of LV lines, 1.3 million poles
and 833 MVA of distribution transformer capacity;
(b)  implementation of economic sub-transmission schemes aimed at promoting private
sector participation in small-scale generation by interconnecting captive power plants,
cogeneration plants and other small power producers that are prepared to sell power
to a PLN regional or mini-grid serving a rural area. For such transactions, a
published power purchase tariff will be offered by PLN; derived in a transparent
manner from PLN's "avoided cost of supply" in different regions, and which is
available to all such potential sellers on a non-discriminatory and non-negotiated
basis as per the terms of a simple and standard power purchase contract;
(c)   development by PLN of five mini-hydro projects with an aggregate capacity of 7.45
MW, and field test drilling of two mini-geothermal fields in Lombok and Ambon for
which power plant construction would be considered for financing under a proposed
follow-on Renewable Energy Development project;
(d)   pilot projects to test the adaptability of low cost "low tech" equipment designs, low
cost network reticulation designs and more efficient construction management
methods for full scale implementation in RE III;
(e)   expansion of the ongoing rural small business services component to promote
productive uses of electricity by businesses in rural areas, including participation by
NGOs;
(f)  implementation support for expansion of the Pola I, II and MSA schemes for local
participation by village level cooperatives and organizations in power distribution
related activities; and preparation of up to two geographic areas for possible
conversion to Pola III; and



30
(g)  technical assistance for: (i) enhancing the RE planning systems capability within PLN
and transferring it to PLN Regions and branches; (ii) preparation of a Renewable
Energy Master Plan for rural electrification in areas not likely to be covered by
PLN's major regional grids within the next ten years; and (iii) implementing the RE-
specific institutional changes required to enhance the organizational and management
efficiency of undertaking RE within PLN, that is consistent with the ongoing
restructuring of PLN and its conversion to Persero status.
4.4        The investment component of the proposed project has been developed on the basis of
advance results available from the RE Master Plan effort. Since the proposed project would cover a two-
year time slice of the RE Development Plan, and further to the agreement reached to review with the
Bank annually, PLN's development program and investment plan (para 1.32), agreemenL was reached
during negotiations for the proposed project that PLN shall: (a) by October 31 of each year commencing
in 1995, until completion of the project, furnish to the Bank for its review and comments, PLN's draft
annual electrification development and investment plans; and (b) thereafterfinalize the said development
plans taking into account the comments, if any, thereon by the Bank.
4.5         System cost and developmental impacts The distribution investment component of the
proposed project incorporates several modifications in equipment specifications and network designs that
would provide significant cost reductions from past PLN practices. These changes in equipment technical
standards and performance specifications, and in network design and construction practices, are the
outcome of TA efforts financed under the Bank's RE I project and as a result of PLN's evolved state of
technical readiness in RE. Since design improvements are the outcome of a continuous process, the
proposed project includes pilot projects that if proven successful, will set the stage for realizing further
cost reductions in subsequent RE programs. Annex 4.2 provides further information about the key design
changes as well as brief descriptions of the pilot project components and the potential for further cost
reductions. Besides targeting and monitoring efficiency improvements in service delivery, the project also
contains elements that will help better assess developmental impacts than hitherto. A TA component on
enhancing RE Planning (para 4.6) will facilitate monitoring of household connection rates over time,
following village electrification as well as household consumption rates and patterns over time. The Rural
Business Services component (para 4.7) incorporates a monitoring system that will help assess the
project's impact on increasing productive activity in rural areas.
4.6        Technical Assistance. The technical assistance components of the project are individually
identified by category and value, in Table 4.1. Out of a total value of about US$29 million, nearly 70%
is for engineering and implementation support of the project components identified. About US$8. 1 million
of TA is for institutional development to: (i) enhance the RE Planning System (REPS) being developed
under the ongoing RE Master Plan TA, and to implement and operationalize the systems and planning
capabilities in PLN's regional offices; and (ii) implement the RE-specific organizational restructuring of
PLN's RE Management and Operational Units and supporting management and financial and other
systems necessary to operationalize such changes. In addition, about US$1.3 million is for policy support
for: (i) preparation of the Renewable Energy Development Plan (paras 2.37 and 2.38); and (ii)
preparation of up to two rural areas where responsibility for electricity distribution would be devolved
from PLN and taken over by a local organization under a Pola III scheme (para 3.16). Terms of reference
(TOR), for all but one of the TA components have been prepared and agreed to with PLN and MOC,
and are contained in the Project Management and Implementation Plan (PMIP) report (para 4.26). The
TOR for the RE-specific reorganization restructuring support for PLN are to be prepared based once the
findings and recommendations of the management consultants that are presently supporting PLN in the

involve about ten foreign consultant contracts. During negotiations for the proposed project, agreement
was reached with PLN that the consultants for the various TA components, identified in Table 4.1, will
be appointed under terms-of-reference and conditions satisfactory to the Bank, and by no later than the
corresponding dates identified in the Project Management and Implementation Plan (PMIP) report (para
4.26).
4.7        Productive uses - Rural Business Services (RBS). The proposed RBS component for
promoting productive uses of electricity draws from the lessons learnt with the small business services
(SBS) pilot component under the Bank's ongoing RE I project; and addresses the key productive use
constraints, on both the demand (business) and supply (PLN) sides, which at present continue to limit the
financial and economic benefits of rural electrification: (a) demand-side information constraints to
increased productive uses remain important. SBS demonstrates that these can be addressed effectively
through improved PLN marketing efforts. However, the high unit costs experienced, as anticipated,
under SBS would be reduced under RBS by using better targeting and more cost effective promotional
campaigns to be carried out by non-governmental organizations (NGOs); and (b) weaknesses in PLN's
corporate capabilities for SBS due to insufficient analysis, and gaps in the collection of financial and
economic data on productive uses, the newness of small business services in PLN, insufficient staffing
of the central SBS unit, and incomplete coordination between the provision of PLN distribution materials
and equipment and SBS marketing efforts have limited PLN's success in providing services to business
in rural areas. These weaknesses would be directly addressed within RBS, by further strengthening of
the present SBS unit within PLN, by the provision of a full-time RBS advisor, and by adjustments to and
further sharpening of the SBS program strategy, design and management. Annex 4.5 provides further
elaboration of the RBS program strategy and design, including a review of the experience to date with
the SBS program under the RE I project.
4.8        Pola program. The primary objective of the Pola Program component in the proposed
project is to enhance the efficiency of RE delivery and increase local participation, by substantially
expanding sub-contracting to KUDs and other local organizations, or by PLN out-sourcing or otherwise
devolving itself of as many distribution, operational and management functions that can be more
efficiently and cost effectively carried out by other providers. This component is designed to improve
upon and greatly multiply the benefits that are already being experienced under the ongoing Pola program
in the RE I project (paras 2.17 and 2.18). A key secondary objective is to select and prepare up to two
geographical areas for possible conversion to Pola IIls in RE III; possibly one in central Java, and another
in South Sulawesi (paras 2.20 and 3.16).
4.9        Details of the Pola Program design are elaborated in the PMIP report (para 4.26). Briefly,
the Pola Program targets call for a near doubling of the total number of Pola I&II and MSA schemes
(4400 by the end of FY 1997/98); with a five-fold increase in the MSA grid contracts from about 90
today to about 600 by the end of FY 1997/98. The major elements of the Pola Program design are aimed
at: (i) strengthening the weak skills base of KUD personnel through a nation-wide Pola I, II and MSA
training program to be facilitated by enhancing PLN's capacity to implement a training program
consistent with the target penetration levels; (ii) reducing the excessive delays in implementing Pola/MSA
schemes caused by administrative procedures at PLN, by incorporating more effective trouble-shooting
capability in the program management structure; (iii) enhancing MOC's ability to support KUDs by
providing program promotion orientation and management training at the Pusat, KANWIL and KANDEP
levels; (iv) implementing a revised and more flexible fee structure for Pola I, II and salary formula for
MSA activities in order to attract better qualified KUD personnel while simultaneously ensuring cost



33
effectiveness from PLN's perspective, with regional managers to have some flexibility in negotiating fees
appropriate to regional cost structures; and (v) transferring additional utility functions through a greater
emphasizes on MSA-grid arrangements in particular, including introduction of the bulk supply tariff to
facilitate Pola III conversions (paras 2.19 and 2.20).
4.10        Mini-hydro. The mini-hydro component of the project will support installations at five sites
in Sulawesi and Eastern Indonesia, with an aggregate generating capacity of 7.5 MW. All projects would
be connected to existing mini-diesel grids, thereby displacing diesel fuel. Individual projects range in size
from 0.86 MW to 2.6 MW, use a run-of-river design and require minimal pondage. Favorable site
conditions for installation, operation, and maintenance have been confirmed at each site. These include
good harbor access, adequate roads, and availability of local labor and supplies. A standard horizontal
Francis turbine-generator design would be used at four of the five sites resulting in considerable savings
from standardization in procurement, installation, training, operation and maintenance and spare parts
inventory management. Annex 4.10 provides more detailed data on the technical characteristics of the
five mini-hydro project sites.
4.11        Mini-geothermal test wells. The mini-geothermal component provides for well test drilling
at two sites in Eastern Indonesia: at Semablun in Lombok, and Tulehu in Ambon. The potential
generating capability at each site is estimated to be 10 MW; and in both instances the displaced fuel will
be diesel fuel. The objective of the mini-geothermal component is to: (i) confirm the power generating
capability of each field (MW, and life); (ii) estimate the cost of tapping such power and verify whether
it would be economic against alternative options; (iii) ascertain whether the power can be produced in
an environmentally sound manner; and (iv) prepare the field and power development programs. In order
to meet these objectives, it is proposed to drill two test holes in each field and monitor the steam and
other effluent for a period of about ten months (flow rates, temperatures, and fluid and gas composition);
on the basis of which the technical, economic and environmental feasibility of a mini-geothermal power
plant will be established for each site. If results are positive, this testing phase will also provide the
necessary data to develop detailed designs and cost estimates and bidding documents for the power plant
to be constructed as well as detail designs for environmental mitigation that is found to be necessary in
the specific context of each site.
4.12        Specific activities to be financed under the proposed project are: (i) preparation of detailed
engineering design and approach for test well drilling and monitoring, including the engineering design
of measures for monitoring, collecting and disposal of effluent in an environmentally acceptable manner;
(ii) test well drilling; (iii) well monitoring and testing; (iv) technical, economic and environmental
analysis of data collected from the monitoring phase; and (v) preparation of detailed cost estimates and
designs for field development and power plant construction and corresponding environmental and any
resettlement plans as appropriate, for implementation and possible financing in a follow-on Bank loan.
4.13        Interconnection  of captive power plants. This component provides for financing
interconnections with existing captive power plants and other small power producers that find it
financially attractive to sell any excess capacity and energy to a PLN regional grid supplying power to
rural areas. The pricing basis for such transactions would be the published small power purchase tariff
and standard contract that would be agreed to with PLN under the policy component of the proposed
project (paras 2.31 and 2.32).



34
Project Cost
4.14       The estimated project cost, inclusive of duties and taxes and physical and price
contingencies, is US$ 743 million. The total financing requirement, including interest during construction
(IDC) and financing charges on the Bank loan, amounts to about US$ 841 million; with US$ 465 million
in foreign exchange and US$ 376 million in local funds and assuming that PLN self-finances all local
currency costs instead of borrowing. Cost estimates are in December 1994 prices, and utilizing an
exchange rate of 2193. Price contingencies for foreign costs are assumed at 2.2 percent per year during
the project implementation period, and for local costs at 5.0 percent per year. The cost estimates, by
major program components are summarized in Table 4.2.
Financing Plan
4.15        The financing plan is summarized in Table 4.3. A proposed Bank loan of US$ 398.0
million equivalent to the Republic of Indonesia would finance 100 percent of the foreign exchange cost
exclusive of interest during construction and taxes, and about 47 percent of the total financing required.
Of this loan amount, about US$ 2.2 million equivalent would be made available to MOC, and the balance
of US$ 395.8 million equivalent would be on lent to PLN. Agreement was reached with GOI during
negotiations for the proposed project that the GOI will on lend the proceeds of the proposed Bank loan,
except for the $2.2 million for technical assistance to the Ministry of Cooperatives, to PLN under a
subsidiary loan agreement with a 20 year term includingfive years grace period. The subsidiary loan will
be denominated in foreign currency withdrawn and the onlending interest rate would be equal to the
Bank's standard variable interest rate plus 0.5 percent per annum. In addition, PLN will pay to GOI a
commitment charge of 0. 75 percent per annum or at such other percentage rate as the Government shall
pay to the Bank on the undisbursed amount of the Subsidiary Loan from time to time. The foreign
exchange risk will be borne by PLN. Execution of a subsidiary loan agreement between GOI and PLN,
satisfactory to the Bank, will be a condition of effectiveness of the loan. Agreement was also reached that
the Government would provide the necessary local funds to PLN to complete the project.

4.16       Financing for expansion of the productive uses program -- the Rural Businesses Services
component to be undertaken by PLN -- as well as financing for the Pola Program component of PLN -
is to be sourced from PLN's annual contribution to the Government's "PPELK fund" (Pembinaan
Pengusahaan Ekonomi Lemah dan Kooperasi); that is intended for the development of economically weak
enterprises and cooperatives. PLN's contributions to this fund are transferred from MOF to an account
in the name of MME; and disbursements are made by MME for pre-approved activities carried out by
PLN and that are consistent with the objectives of the PPELK fund. During negotiations for the proposed
project, agreement was reached with GOI thatfinancing for the Rural Business Services (RBS) program
as well as financing for the local costs of the Pola program component of PLN will be providedfrom the
PPELK fund; and to the extent that such funds turn out to be less than the required amount, the
Government would make up the shortfall.

and connecting new consumers; (b) concrete power line poles; (c) equipment for mini-hydro, mini-
geothermal and pilot project schemes; (d) drilling services for the mini-geothermal component; and (e)
consultancy services. All goods and house connection items to be financed under the Bank loan will be
procured through international competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance with the Bank's procurement
guidelines and utilizing the Bank's Standard Bidding Documents (SBD) in their organization, format and

38
language; with the following exceptions. Concrete power line poles would be procured through local
competitive bidding (LCB) procedures in accordance with GOI guidelines satisfactory to the Bank.
Because of the large quantities and weight involved in the transportation of raw materials and finished
product, foreign suppliers are not expected to be interested in supplying concrete power line poles;
however, under LCB procedures they would be allowed to bid if they are interested. Consulting services
for technical assistance (TA), will be procured in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for the Use of
Consultants. Table 4.1 identifies the major consultancy components under the TA component. Eligible
local suppliers competing for the supply of goods under ICB procedures would be eligible for a
preference in bid evaluation of 15 percent or the import duty, whichever is lower. All bid packages for
goods as well as drilling services to be financed by the Bank loan estimated to cost over US$ 200,000
equivalent, which would cover over 95 percent of the procurement volume, would be subject to the
Bank's prior review. All consulting services above US$ 100,000 equivalent provided by firms, and above
US$ 50,000 equivalent to be provided by individuals would be subject to the Bank's prior review.
4.18       With respect to the procurement of concrete power line poles, understanding has been
reached with PLN, that the specifications in the tender documents will be sufficiently general so as to
include rectangular and/or verendiel concrete poles or other types of concrete poles that meet performance
standards, and this inclusion will be explicitly stated in the tender document. Further understanding was
reached with PLN that, in order to contain increases in the transportation costs of concrete poles and
increase more competition, the tenders for concrete power line poles would require the bidders to quote
two separate price components: (i) the ex-factory price of the pole; and (ii) transportation costs. During
the implementation stage, construction contractors will be advised of the pole suppliers ex-factory price
as well as the delivered price to site; and the construction contractors would be able to exercise the choice
of picking up the poles at the factory gate and transport the poles themselves, or have the poles delivered
at site.
4.19       Equipment for the mini-hydro projects would be procured through limited international
bidding procedures (LIB), and equipment for pilot projects would be procured through shopping
procedures permissible under Bank procurement guidelines; because of the specialized nature of some
equipment and the need for customization in certain cases. In the mini-hydro component, understanding
has been reached with PLN that procurement of the five standardized turbine/generators for Ulung
Peliang, Bambalo (2 sets), Kolondom and Werba would be done under a single equipment contract; with
a separate tender to be issued for the non-standard equipment at Lokomboro. Miscellaneous purchases
involving small lot sizes of materials and equipment, such as isolating transformers and wood moulds for
reinforced concrete power line poles, and not exceeding US$ 2,400,000 in aggregate, will be procured
through shopping procedures permissible under Bank procurement guidelines; with individual contracts
not exceeding US$ 200,000 in the case of international shopping and not exceeding US$ 50,000 in the
case of local shopping.
4.20       In the mini-geothermal component, understanding has been reached with PLN that an
integrated drilling services contract would be used at each site in place of PLN's conventional three
contract approach. This innovative approach would reduce development costs by consolidating
responsibility and control in a single head contractor with incentives for cost containment and adherence
to schedules. Because of the relative scarcity of geothermal drilling expertise, procurement for each of
the two integrated drilling services contract, one each for the two proposed sites, would be through
limited international bidding procedure permissible under Bank procurement guidelines.



39
Disbursement
4.21       The proposed Bank loan would be disbursed against approved contracts and is estimated to
cover: (a) 100% of cif (for imported), or ex-factory (for locally assembled) equipment and materials by
ICB; (b) 80% of ex-factory expenditures for concrete poles, which represents the estimated foreign
content; (c) 100% of expenditures for consultancy services and training under the technical assistance
component; (d) 65% of local expenditures for other items procured locally; and (e) 100% of the drilling
services in the mini-geothermal component. Table 4.5 shows the allocation of loan proceeds to each
Category and the percentage of expenditures to be financed in each Category. Withdrawl applications
would be fully documented for all expenditures against which loan disbursements would be made, except
for goods and drilling services contracts valued at less than US$ 200,000 and individual contracts valued
at less than US$ 50,000 and consulting firm contracts valued at less than US$ 100,000 which would be
disbursed on the basis of statements of expenditure (SOEs). Documents supporting the SOEs would be
retained by respective project implementing units and made available for review as requested by Bank
missions.
4.22       In order to facilitate disbursements, a Special Account in an amount of US$ 20 million was
proposed, this amount to be deposited in a bank on terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank. This
Special Account would be held in the name of Director General of Budget, Ministry of Finance,
following established procedures. The Special Account would be used for all eligible foreign and local
expenditures. Replenishment to the Special Account would be made on a monthly basis or when 20
percent of the initial deposit has been used, whichever occurs first.
4.23       The estimated disbursement schedule for the proposed Bank loan is shown in Annex 4.3.
Disbursements against the proposed Bank loan are expected to be faster than the Bank profile for power
projects because of the high proportion of goods readily available in the market, the relatively short two-
year time-slice coverage of the project, and the fact that the loan would be disbursed mainly against
procurement of goods. Construction of civil works involved in the proposed project would be minimal.
Moreover, problems with slow disbursements encountered in the ongoing rural electrification project are
expected to be reduced significantly in the proposed project, because of the creation of a single point of
responsibility for the project in PLN -- the Project Management Unit (PMU) -- and a detailed Project
Management and Implementation Plan (PMIP) for closely monitoring the status of procurement and
disbursement on a full time basis and to take the necessary corrective actions (paras 4.26 and 4.27).
Implementation
4.24       PLN and MOC would be responsible for the implementation of their respective components.
After the completion of general plans for RE by PLN Headquarters, for each year and for each province,
the villages to be electrified will be finalized on an annual basis as a consequence of consultations
between PLN's regional offices in conjunction with local and central governments. The starting point for
the selection of villages would be the annual village electrification targets by region developed by the
ongoing RE Master Plan study that would be completed by June 1995. Next, villages within each region
would be ranked in accordance with a priority order established on the basis of economic criteria and
evaluation method acceptable to the Bank. The final list of villages to be electrified in a given year would
be drawn up from this rank ordered list, taking into account other developmental objectives; but with the
primary emphasis placed being on economic viability. During project preparation, an understanding was

reached with PLN on the general thrust of such revisions to be undertaken to the village selection criteria
presently in use (paras 2.9 and 2.15). Agreement was reached during negotiations for the proposed
project that PLN shall select villages to be electrified each year under the project in accordance with
criteria and procedures agreed with the Bank. After the village list is finalized, PLN's regional offices
will prepare designs for each of the sub-projects within their jurisdictions, and prepare bills-of-quantities
of materials and equipment required for construction. This would be followed at the Headquarters by the
preparation of standard bid documents with the technical specifications for the items to be procured and
subsequently with the tendering process.
4.25       PLN would be responsible for project engineering and would construct RE sub-projects
financed under the proposed project through contractors. PLN would be responsible for construction
supervision carried out through its regional offices. Since construction of RE facilities by PLN is a
continuous process, PLN would start construction of the project as a continuation of its ongoing RE
construction, using materials and equipment available in its regional warehouses, should any delays occur
in delivery of Bank financed goods at the site. Due to the nature of the construction, some replenishment
of PLN's provincial warehouses is possible and would be acceptable to the Bank. A project
implementation schedule is contained in Annex 4.4; with detailed schedules for each component provided
in the PMIP report (para 4.26). To ensure timely implementation of the physical program, preparation
of activities related to tendering and procurement are already underway in advance of the expected date
of loan effectiveness in June 1995. In the case of pilot projects, TAs and the mini-geothermal and mini-
hydro and mini-geothermal components, detailed TORs have been finalized to enable contractor/consultant
selection process well in advance of the expected date of loan effectiveness.
Project Management and Monitoring
4.26       The project management and monitoring arrangements are detailed in a separate and
substantial report titled "RE II Project Management and Implementation Plan", February 1994 (PMIP).
This report is a part of the project file and is referenced in the loan documents. For each component of
the project, there is a chapter in the PMIP report which addresses the following principal elements: scope
and objectives, critical success factors, strategy and implementation plan, project management structure,
milestones and performance indicators, monitoring and reporting requirements, implementation schedule
and disbursement schedule. The report will serve as the key reference document for the line managers
in-charge of executing each component; identifying the basis on which the progress and success of each
component will be measured and for which they are responsible. The PMIP will also provide the
reference benchmarks for Bank supervision of the project components.
4.27       Project Management Unit (PMU) and Project Coordinator. A central feature of the
project implementation arrangements is the RE II Project Management Unit, located in PLN's RE
Division -- DIVDES. A detailed description of the PMU, its organization and staffing, and its role,
functions and responsibilities is contained in Chapter 2 of the PMIP. The PMU is headed by the RE II
Project Coordinator -- a senior and seasoned PLN manager -- who will act as the focal person
responsible for ensuring implementation of all components and sub-components of the project in
accordance with the project design and objectives and implementation plan and monitoring and evaluation
arrangements as elaborated in the PMIP report agreed to between PLN and the Bank. The Project
Coordinator and the PMU will be responsible for the project's progress and implementation from its
inception to its successful completion; and will be directly accountable to the Board of Directors and the
President Director. All concerned Division Heads, Wilayah Managers, and Heads of Districts and
Distribusi, and those responsible for conducting or supporting rural electrification, would be obliged --



42
under a official circular already issued by PLN's president Director -- to provide full administrative and
logistical support to the Project Coordinator and the PMU and in a responsive manner. As the focal
person, the Project Coordinator will oversee, monitor, and coordinate the activities of all PLN
Divisions/Departments directly and indirectly involved in implementation of the RE II project; and will
have the authority to recommend, initiate or otherwise facilitate and ensure that the relevant Division or
operating unit most directly involved in implementation of a given project component will undertake on
a timely basis the required corrective actions that may become necessary from time to time for ensuring
effective implementation of the project. The Project Coordinator will also be responsible for ensuring that
project implementation issues involving all external agencies such as DGEED, BAPPENAS, MOC, and
by contractors are addressed in a timely manner and are complied with as per the PMIP report. During
negotiations for the proposed project, agreement was reached with PLN that it shall maintain the project
Management Unit within its Rural Electrification Division with functions, powers, staff, and funds
necessary to coordinate and supervise and to ensure the implementation of the project.
4.28       Technical audit  The Bank would closely supervise project implementation status by
undertaking field missions. A tentative schedule for supervision activities and missions is summarized in
Annex 4.6. It would also be necessary to conduct a technical audit in order to verify on a sampling basis,
that the resources spent under the proposed loan are used efficiently and for their intended developmental
purposes. Inter alia, this technical audit would focus ex-post on quality assurance and efficiency as it
relates to program aspects such as hardware utilized, erection, inventory management, speed of customer
connections following village access to electricity, operations and maintenance procedures and actions to
ensure that the equipment performs to standards throughout its intended life, and report on the
performance of the environment and resettlement aspects where applicable. During negotiations for the
proposed project, agreement was reached with PLN that it shall: (a) by October 31, 1995, establish
principles and procedures for technical audit of Subprojects in consultation with the Bank: (b)
commencing in 1996, until completion of the Project, carry out an annual technical audit of the completed
parts of the project, through independent technical auditors acceptable to the Bank in accordance with
the principles and procedures acceptable to the Bank, focussing on the quality and standards of the
goods, works, and services financed out of the proceeds of the Loan including effective utilization thereof,
operation and maintenance of the equipment, and the environmental and resettlement aspects of the
Subprojects; and (c) furnish to the Bank as soon as available, but in case not later than September 30
of each such year, the report of such technical audit by such auditors and such other information relating
thereto in such detail as the Bank may reasonably request.
Environment and Resettlement
4.29       The proposed project comprises of three physical components: (i) implementation of
distribution network schemes consisting of numerous very small sub-projects; (ii) construction of five
mini-hydro projects (about 7.25 MW) of generating capacity; and (iii) test-well drilling at two mini-
geothermal fields at Tulehu, Ambon and at Sembalun, Lombok.
4.30       The distribution component involves erection of about 28,000 kmc of MV (medium voltage-
20 kV) lines, 35,000 kmc of LV (low voltage-220/440 volts) lines, 1.3 million poles and 833 MVA of
secondary distribution transformer capacity. This component consists of several hundreds of very small
sub-projects, each involving MV line extensions to a few villages and LV lines connecting a few
consumers. The individual sub-project locations and designs will be developed subsequently and closer
to the time of implementation. Erection of these lines would involve staking power line poles, mostly



43
following existing infrastructure and in thinly populated areas, with the transformers mounted on pole
tops.
4.31       The distribution sub-projects are not expected to pose major or significant environmental
and resettlement (E&R) problems; while having the potential to cause minor and localized impact, these
would not be large enough to warrant major environmental concern. During negotiations for the proposed
project, agreement was reached with PLN: (i) the environmental and resettlement impacts of the
distribution sub-projects shall be established and documented in the Environment chapter of the design
reports for the sub-projects as per guidelines satisfactory to the Bank (Annex 4. 7); (ii) people affected by
any of the sub-projects will be compensated and resettled on the basis of the agreed resettlement policy
(Annex 4.8); and (iii) PLN shall maintain data and progress reports on implementation of land acquisition
and resettlement measures, and a summary report will be sent to the Bank annually for review and
comment.
4.32       The mini-hydro component consists of five sub-projects with individual projects ranging in
capacity from 900 kW to 2.6 MW. Favorable site conditions have been confirmed at each site. The
environmental impacts -- primarily land acquisition and physical resettlement -- are well defined and
understood and minor in each case. During negotiations for the proposed project, agreement was reached
that PLN shall complete, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank (Annex 4.9), the environmental screening
for the mini-hydro plants and incorporate the mitigation measures for the plants design and/or
environmental management plan, before any disbursements would be approvedfor that component of the
project. These sub-projects will involve acquisition of an estimated 408,175 square meters of land, that
will require compensation and/or resettlement of about 60 families, of which 57 will be partially affected
and three families will be displaced. Annex 4.10 provides some data on the technical and physical
parameters for each sub-project; as well as the main parameters for land acquisition areas, and relocation
and compensation. PLN is capable of resolving all such issues in a manner satisfactory to the Bank.
During negotiations, agreement was reached with PLN that: (i) it will prepare and submit a report
summarizing, (a) the compensation rates offered by PLN and how these rates compare with the existing
NJOP (land tax) and market values in a given area, (b) data by household on the extent of impact -- the
amount and proportion of land and/or house affected -- and total compensation offered to affected
households, and (c) any resettlement measures developedfor affected persons by PLN. Clearance of this
report by the Bank will be a condition of disbursement on this component of the project; and (iii) all
affected households will be compensated at least two months prior to commencement of implementation
in accordance with the agreed resettlement policy (Annex 4.8).
4.33       In connection with the proposed project component which involves test-well drilling of four
geothermal test wells, during negotiations agreement was reached with PLN: (i) environmental screening
to identify all possible environmental impacts during drilling and testing operations will be conducted by
qualified consultants, acceptable to the Bank; (ii) following environmental screening, conceptual
engineering design documents, including all the required environmental mitigation measures will be
submittedfor Bank's approval; and (iii) completion of the preceding conditions, satisfactory to the Bank,
will be a condition for disbursement on this component.



44
5
FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
Past Operating Results and Financial Position
5.1         PLN's operating results and financial position for 1985/86 to 1992/93 are shown
in the financial statements in Annex 5.1. PLN's overall profitability as reflected in the rate-of-
return (ROR), has improved in recent years; as a consequence of timely tariff increases,
beginning with an average increase of about 25 percent in the electricity rates approved by the
Government, effective April 1, 1989, and subsequent increases in the rates by about 20 percent
effective August 1, 1991, about 13 percent effective February 1, 1993, and about 8 percent
effective November 1, 1994.
5.2         PLN's operating performance varies considerably at regional levels. PLN's Java
operations, which supply electricity through an interconnected system with power generated
by large and efficient power plants, are largely commercially-oriented and profitable. PLN's
outside-Java operations, which are mainly small-scale and dispersed over a large number of
islands and where power generation is largely oil-based and diesel power plants are commonly
employed, are mostly unprofitable, and have a negative impact on PLN's overall finances.
This situation in the case of the Outer Islands is further exacerbated by the relatively high share
of expenditure related to expansion of rural electrification coverage.
Financial Objectives
5.3         During the negotiations of the loan for the proposed project, it was agreed that
PLIN shall, from time to time, take all measures, satisfactory to the Bank, as shall be required
for it to realize, for all its operations, forfiscal year 1995 and in each fiscal year thereafter,
an annual rate of return of not less than eight percent of the average net revalued fixed assets
in operation. The principal financial objectives underlying this revenue covenant are that PLN
should realize positive annual rates of return, meet its debt service requirements, maintain
adequate liquidity, self-finance over 25-30 percent of its annual capital expenditures, and rely
mostly on borrowings to finance the balance, confining the Government's contributions to
programs that involve the socially directed activities such as rural electrification, mainly
outside-Java.
Present and Future Performance
5.4         PLN's financial performance for 1993/94, the estimate for 1994/95 and
projections through 1999/2000 and related income statements for its Java and outside-Java
operations are summarized in Table 5.1. (Details are given in Annex 5.1; notes and
