assumptions underlying the projections are given in Annex 5.2). In the financial projections,
for the Java and Outside Java operations, sales growth is based on PLN's demand projections
and generation expansion plans, and the capital expenditures are based on the proposed
investment program to be implemented by PLN, Rp 68,600 billion in current prices (Annexes
1.6 and 1.7), and exclude capital expenditures for generation projects proposed to be
implemented by private investors (paras. 1. 13 and 1. 14).
5.5         The financial projections are based on compliance with the proposed revenue
covenant in 1994/95 (para. 5.3). A tariff increase of about 7.7 percent, effective November
1, 1994, was approved by the Government. This increase would be sufficient to meet the 8%
ROR covenant, predicated on realization of forecast of total energy sales for the period April
1994 - March 31, 1995. Trends based wholly on actual 12 monthly energy sales -- over
period July 1993 through August 1994 -- show an average growth rate of 1.2% per month,
which, if continued, indicate that by March 31, 1995 PLN's energy sales would fall short of
projections by perhaps 6 percent. However, PLN's monthly sales for the five remaining
months of the above mentioned period are expected to grow at a higher rate than the 12-month
historical trend would suggest. Furthermore, under the recently implemented tariff structure,
the peak period tariff relative to off-peak period tariff has been reduced thereby greatly
reducing incentives for peak shaving by industrial customers. In addition, PLN is engaged in
load promotion activities, and the Bank was informed that recent sales data on daily sales for
Java-Bali indeed support this expectation. To maintain the annual rates of return in subsequent
years at the 8 percent level, annual tariff increases would be required mainly to compensate
for the variation in the input costs, (e.g., fuel and inflation), except in 1998 where a tariff
increase of about 14 percent would be required to compensate for substantial increases in the
cost of power purchases from private producers (para. 1.13). PLN's annual self-financing
ratios during 1994/95-1995/96 would be slightly over 25 percent, increasing to a 30-40 percent
range on a three-year average basis during the balance of the forecast period; because of
reduced PLN investment in later years in light of the expected increase in private investment
in generation capacity to supply PLN (paras. 1.13-1.14). The capital structure would remain
satisfactory; the debt as a percentage of debt plus equity (excluding revaluation surplus) would
rise from about 36 percent in 1992 to about 53 percent in 1996, and thereafter remain at that
level. PLN's debt service coverage ratio would be in excess of 3.0 throughout the projection
period. An existing covenant under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project (Loan 3761-
IND), requires that PLN may not incur any additional debt unless a reasonable forecast of its
net revenues after expenditures for each year during the term of the debt to be incurred shall
be at least 1.5 times its projected debt service requirements. This covenant was also agreed
under the proposed project. PLN's current ratio is expected to be in the range of 1.3 to 1.5
through most of the projection period; this would provide adequate liquidity.
5.6         The financing plans assumed in the financial projections for the REPELITA V
(1989-1993) and REPELITA VI (1994-1998) are presented and compared to that for the
REPELITA IV (1984-1988) in Table 5.2. Details are given in Annex 5.1. In contrast to
REPELITA V, the financing plan of REPELITA VI assumes higher contributions from internal
sources (about 33 percent versus 19 percent) and borrowings by PLN (about 51 percent versus
42 percent), and reduced Government contribution (about 17 percent versus 39 percent). The
self-financing levels projected for REPELITA VI are higher than the levels PLN has achieved
historically and largely reflect the fact that private power projects account for substantial
investment during this period that would otherwise have been carried out by PLN (para. 1.13).
If PLN were to carry out all investment during the REPELITA VI period, its contribution to
investment would be about 27 percent, or about 6 percentage points lower for that period.

5.7         To ensure PLN's financial soundness and to facilitate private power entry into
the sector, the Government approved, together with the retail tariff increase in November
1994, a Periodic Tariff Adjustment Mechanism. This formula-based tariff adjustment only
passes through costs which are outside the control of PLN such as: changes in fuel prices,
power purchase prices, the local inflation rate, and the US dollar currency exchange rate
towards Indonesian Rupiah. Any changes needed to the base tariffs, would be reviewed by
the Government during updates of the base tariffs, at which time efficiency issues would be
addressed.
Tariffs
5.8         PLN's tariff schedules, which became effective November 1, 1994, comprise
24 consumer categories. For medium and large industrial consumers, large commercial
consumers, large social institutions, and for large office buildings, time-of-day pricing is in
effect. The 1993-94 tariff schedules are shown in Annex 5.3.
5.9         The Bank has reviewed PLN's LRMC study for Java-Bali that accounts for
about 80 percent of PLN sales. The study indicates that PLN's average revenue would be
about Rp 163/kWh in Indonesia as a whole, and Rp 154/kWh in Java, if the tariff to each
consumer category was set at the estimated LRMC of supply in Java. PLN's average revenue
of Rp 162/kWh in Java, based on the current tariffs (effective November 1, 1994) and not
including connection fees paid by consumers, is close to the average LRMC of supply in Java.
However, cross-subsidies exist between different consumer groups. To compensate for
subsidies to small consumers, electricity prices to large consumers in Java, (industrial,
commercial and residential), are higher than the economic cost of supply. Despite these
subsidies, the household electrification ratio remains low at about 35 percent. High lump sum
payments for the initial installation charge coupled with fixed monthly demand charges serve
as barriers to many new consumers and to increase in consumption by existing consumers.
Implementation of key elements of the sector policy and agenda - commercialization of PLN
operations, implementation of published bulk power purchase and supply tariffs, increased



48
participation of the private sector in electricity supply and distribution, and instituting a
uniform regulatory framework for PLN and the private sector - will ultimately bring about
conditions under which the need for substantially reducing tariff distortions will become
unavoidable; conditions under which the Government will also find it politically easier to allow
such changes.
5.10        During negotiations for the proposed project, it was agreed that PLN shall: (a)
by October 31 every year commencing in 1995 review its long run marginal costs with a view
to formulating its tariff structure; and (b) discuss the results of such review with the Borrower
and the Bank; and (c) thereafter, take appropriate steps to revise the tariffstructure taking into
account the views, if any, thereon of the Borrower and the Bank.
Overall Development Fund and Dividend Policy
5.11        PLN's corporate status changed from "Perum" to "Persero" effective August 1,
1994 under Government Regulation Number 23 of 1994. The dividend policy applicable to
PLN is currently under consideration by MOF but it is estimated that the decision would be
made by year-end 1994. A covenant was agreed with the executing agency, PLN, that would
limit PLN from declaring any dividend or making any other distribution with respect to its
share capital, unless, after such dividend has been paid or other distribution has been made,
the current assets of the Borrower would equal or exceed 1.0 times the current liabilities of the
Borrower. The dividends will be paid on a net profit after interest and corporate tax basis to
the stockholder (currently MOF/State Treasury). Under Perum status PLN was obliged to set
aside 55 percent of its annual net profit after corporate income tax for "Dana Pembangunan
Semesta" (DPS), or the Overall Development Fund, payable in five annual installments
commencing two years after the year in which the DPS obligation was incurred. PLN's
conversion to a Persero will not affect the remaining DPS installments due the State Treasury.
Under this project, a covenant was agreed that GOI will take all such actions as shall be
necessary to ensure that any payments from PLN to the DPS shall not prevent PLN from
inplementing its agreed investment program.
Financing Policy
5.12        PLN formulates its long-term investment program spanning a ten-year-horizon,
which is updated periodically. The plan is reviewed by the GOI and a five-year time-slice of
it is incorporated in the country's development plan, the next of which (REPELITA VI) is to
commence on April 1, 1994. Based on the approved framework of the five-year plan, PLN
prepares an annual plan and a budget, which is approved by the Government. In recent years,
the Government has shown a strong financial commitment towards these plans and has taken
steps to enable PLN to implement them (para. 5.6).
5.13        An existing covenant (Loan 3761-IND) relating to the long-term aspects of PLN's
financial planning was also agreed under the proposed project. This covenant requires, inter
alia, that, as part of its ongoing annual planning activities, PLN should prepare a rolling ten-
year financial forecast based on its proposed development plan, and also prepare for the first
five years of the forecast period a financing plan that should indicate the amount of external
financing and the appropriate mix of equity and debt, and if possible the potential sources of
financing. Technical assistance is being provided under the Power Sector Efficiency Project
(Loan 3097-IND) is helping to strengthen the financial planning capability in PLN.



49
5.14        PLN is authorized by its charter to borrow from local and foreign lenders and
to issue its own obligations. Until recently, PLN's local borrowings had been only from the
Government-owned Banks. By Government Regulation No. 60/192 dated September 19, 1992,
PLN has been authorized to issue bonds in stages up to a maximum amount totalling Rp 1.2
trillion, with the approval of the Minister of Finance. In September 1992, PLN issued in the
domestic capital market, the first annual tranche of such bonds amounting to Rp 300 billion,
which was oversubscribed. PLN raised a further Rp 600 billion through bond issuance in
September 1993. PLN would require extension of authority to issue bonds beyond the current
ceiling of Rp 1.2 trillion, since the practice of issuing bonds is expected to continue in later
years. To support the bond issue, PLN has strengthened its treasury functions and reduced
the time required to finalize the annual financial accounts and complete their audit (para. 3.12).
5.15        Under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project (Loan 3761-IND), the Bank
proposed and the Government agreed that any proceeds of the proposed Bank loan be onlent
to PLN under a subsidiary loan agreement, be disbursed in the same currencies as those
disbursed from the Bank loan account, and their aggregate value denominated in US dollars,
with each withdrawal valued in the same manner as valued by the Bank. Repayment of the
subsidiary loan would be on the same terms and conditions as those for the repayment of the
Bank loan by the Government. The onlending interest rate would be the Bank's standard
variable interest rate plus 0.5 percent per annum. The same on-lending arrangements agreed
to under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project was also agreed to under the proposed
project (para. 4.15).
Asset Revaluation
5.16        PLN's assets have been revalued periodically. The last detailed revaluation was
done in 1979 as required of all public corporations under instructions of the Ministry of
Finance. The revaluation was recorded in the books and was accepted by the Bank. Fixed
assets acquired after March 31, 1979 are appraised at cost.  Following the currency
devaluation in September 1986, the Government issued Regulation No. 45/1986 dated
October 2, 1986, providing for the revaluation of assets of businesses and public corporations,
acquired through September 12, 1986. The Regulation specifies the annual revaluation factors
for the years 1970 to 1986 for adjusting the asset values as of January 1, 1987. For purposes
of monitoring the proposed revenue covenant (para. 5.3) PLN has prepared a revaluation of
its assets for 1979/80-1986/87 based on the Regulation. The methodology used by PLN for
asset revaluation is satisfactory to the Bank. A covenant, under Loan 3761-IND, that requires
PLN to revalue annually its fixed assets (including work in progress and consumers'
contributions) and accumulated depreciation for the purposes of the rate of return calculations
under the revenue covenant was also agreed under the proposed project.

6
PROJECT JUSTIFCATION
Project Benefits
6.1        Support power sector reform agenda. The proposed project would support implementation
of the broader reforms for the power sector initiated under the Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project
(paras 1.21 through 1.34).
6.2        RE-specfic institutional change.  The proposed project would support the continued
development and implementation of an efficient RE program in Indonesia. The project will help to
address a primary constraint to further enhancing the efficiency of RE delivery as well as the long term
sustainability of the RE program; that is related centrally to the need for significant changes to PLN's
present organizational structure and management processes for the RE program. Such changes will help
to establish a mission-oriented institutional environment. With changed incentives, the RE line managers
would themselves become agents of change by being more imaginative in finding ways to reduce the unit
cost of RE delivery, aggressively seeking to reduce power generation costs by buying power from private
generators when it is cost effective to do so, vigorously promoting productive uses of electricity, and
proactively promoting local participation through Pola and MSA programs. The potential efficiency gains
from such a change will lead to cost reductions that will go beyond those reached from technical
modifications alone (paras 2.28 through 2.30 and para 3.5).
6.3        Meet demand, enhance the quality of life and increase productive uses. In addition, the
proposed project would help to extend the supply of electricity in rural areas in Indonesia during 1995-
1997; thereby contributing to a significant improvement in the quality of life of 2.1 million rural
households - with women and children being primary beneficiaries -- and more broadly contributing to
the development of rural areas, by facilitating economic opportunity through productive uses of electricity
and by making it feasible to start the process of technology change over a period of time (para 2.3).
6.4       Increase local and private participation. The proposed project builds upon the positive
experience of the ongoing rural electrification (RE I) project, the Bank's first lending operation in this
sub-sector, and aims to enhance the efficiency and benefits while reducing the cost of RE delivery by
greatly expanding the extent and scope of local and private sector participation in electricity delivery and
supply functions; by village cooperative organizations participating in the various Pola schemes and MSA
arrangernents (paras 2.17 through 2.19), and by private entrepreneurs (paras 2.31 through 2.35); as well
as by aggressively seeking and cost effectively promoting on a wide scale, with the help of NGOs,
productive uses of electricity by rural businesses (paras 2.21 through 2.23).
6.5        Cost reduction - present and future. The proposed project would reduce the unit costs of
electricity distribution through the revision and adoption of technical designs and standards for equipment
and construction that are appropriate in the RE context. The project would also set the stage -- through
the pilot project sub-components -- for significant cost reductions in the equipment and material costs as



51
well as reductions in construction management costs associated with implementation of the physical
program in the next stage or rural electrification.
6.6        Renewable energy development.  The project will promote the development of cost
effective indigenous and decentralized renewable energy resources and in an environmentally sound
manner; and simultaneously strengthen PLN's capability to plan, design and implement a longer-term
mini-hydro and mini-geothermal power development program that is integrated into RE planning. The
experience gained during the course of the proposed project will also pave the way for realizing cost
reductions that can be gained by standardization of equipment designs and power plant configurations and
by more streamlined contract management and supervision approaches.
Economic Rate of Return
6.7        Distribution component. The proposed two-year time slice RE investment would electrify
approximately 7,000 rural villages and connect about 2.1 million new consumers. Following completion
of this program in 1996/97, the household electrification ratio would stand at about 35%, well below the
two-thirds of the population that is estimated to be able to afford electricity at the economic cost of
supply; with the percentage of households that can afford to pay for electricity consumption for meeting
just their basic needs -- lighting -- being a much higher percentage yet. In the case of Indonesia, there
is no dearth of evidence to indicate that there is considerable amount of suppressed demand and backed
up by willingness to pay.
6.8        The two-year time slice distribution investment component of the proposed project is a
component of PLN's overall core development and investment program. The RE development plan --
number of villages and consumers to be electrified, unit material and equipment requirements and costs -
- is based upon the advance results available from the RE Master Plan development efforts. Economic
viability has been established by calculating the Internal Economic Rate of Return (IERR), on the basis
of the incremental cost and benefit streams associated with the 1995/96-1996/97 time slice investment for
RE.
6.9        Capital costs for the economic analysis include the initial investments in the time slice for
distribution network, MV and LV, extensions and house connections; as well as corresponding investment
in later years for intensification for which the MV grid would already be in-place as a consequence of
the two year time-slice investment. These costs are exclusive of taxes and duties. Power supply costs are
based on the long run marginal costs of generation (LRMC) of bulk supply delivered to 20kV.
6.10       Benefits of the proposed distribution program have been estimated in terms of consumers'
willingness-to-pay for electricity estimated by the tariff plus consumer surplus. For non-residential
consumers, their willingness to pay is estimated by the cost of self generation from a captive diesel plant.
For residential consumers, their consumer surplus is estimated by two components: (a) consumer surplus
in the diverted market i.e, cost savings from the substitution of kerosene by electricity for lighting, and
(b) the incremental consumer surplus in the new market i.e., the benefit of increased consumption because
of the availability of lower cost electricity.
6.11       The IERR for the distribution investment component of the proposed project, on the basis
of the approach described above, was calculated to be about 21 percent. Further details of this analysis,
including sensitivity to increases in capital costs and reduced demand are given in Annex 6.1.



52
6.12       Mini-hydro and mini-geothermal. A least-cost analysis was performed for each of the five
mini-hydro projects and for each of the two mini-geothermal projects, comparing costs with and without
the project. The "with project" case assumed, respectively, a hybrid mini-hydro/diesel, mini-
geothermal/diesel powered grid compared to the "without project" case of supply by a diesel power plant.
The net present value of annual costs for operation with and without the mini-hydro/mini-geothermal plant
were calculated to establish that in each case the renewable project would be the least cost option for
generating electricity. Next, an internal economic rate of return (IERR) analysis was conducted for each
project, based on the costs and benefits specific to each project. The results indicate IERRs for each
project well above 12 percent;, even when capital costs were increased by 10 percent from the base case,
and when electricity demand growth rates were reduced by 50 percent from the base case. Annexes 6.2
and 6.3, respectively, provide further details of this analysis of the mini-hydro and mini-geothermal
project components.
Project Risks
6.13       No significant technical or implementation risks are foreseen with respect to the physical
network development program, that involves routine grid extensions and consumer connections. PLN
has consistently exceeded annual rural electrification targets and implementation schedules. The risk
associated with implementation of the mini-hydro is minimized by a technical design that places maximum
emphasis on standardization, and ensuring appointment of highly qualified construction management and
supervisory contractor in the case of mini-hydro; and in the case of the mini-geothermal component by
employing an internationally reputed firm under a single integrated drilling services contract. The risk
of inadequate private sector response to the published power purchase tariff is minimized by emphasizing
that a transparent, regulatory framework and effective regulator is in place, and by preparing for close
supervision. Risks associated with implementation of the overall policy reform agenda in general and
other elements of the policy component specific to RE -- institutional restructuring, and implementation
of the bulk supply tariff -- are minimal in view of the Government's and PLN's excellent record in
implementation once agreement is reached.



53
7
AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMIENDATION
Agreements Reached with the Borrower
7.1         During the negotiations, the following agreements were reached with the Borrower:
(a)   The Borrower shall onlend the proceeds of the proposed Bank loan, except for the $2.2
million for technical assistance to the Ministry of Cooperatives, to PLN under a subsidiary loan
agreement with a 20 year term including five years grace period. The subsidiary loan will be denominated
in foreign currency withdrawn and the onlending interest rate would be equal to the Bank's standard
variable interest rate plus 0.5 percent per annum. In addition, PLN will pay to GOI a commitment charge
of 0.75 percent per annum or at such other percentage rate as the Government shall pay to the Bank on
the undisbursed amount of the Subsidiary Loan from time to time. The foreign exchange risk will be
borne by PLN. In addition, the Government would provide the necessary local funds to PLN to complete
the project (para 4.15);
(b)   The Borrower shall permit financing for the Rural Business Services (RBS) program as well
as financing for the local costs of the Pola program component of PLN to provided from the PPELK
fund; and to the extent that such funds turn out to be less than the required amount, the Government
would make up the shortfall (para 4.16);
(c)   The Borrower shall: (a) prepare and, by June 30, 1995, adopt a policy regarding
implementation of the power purchase tariff and bulk supply tariff satisfactory to the Bank including
policies governing the granting of any subsidies related to implementation of the bulk supply tariff, rules
and procedures for determining such subsidies and how such subsidies are to financed and directed in a
transparent manner; and (b) draft, and by December 31, 1995, promulgate rules and regulations
concerning oversight of the power purchase tariff and bulk supply tariff implementation, in form
acceptable to the Bank (para 2.35);
(d)   The Borrower shall: (a) by December 31 each year commencing in 1995, (i) review with
the Bank (1) its power sector development program, (2) the least cost planning analysis used to formulate
the said program, (3) the roles of PLN and the private sector in the said program, and (4) the
transparency and appropriateness of the business environment for private sector participation; (ii) review
with the Bank, PLN's development and investment programs with respect to, (1) their consistency with
the power sector development program, (2) the balance among generation, transmission and distribution
investments, and (3) the balanced development of regions; and (b) ensure that PLN has access to
sufficient funds to finance capital expenditures for its investment program pursuant to the said reviews
(para 1.32);
(e)   The Borrower shall: (a) undertake and complete a study on private power development in
accordance with terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank; (b) upon completion of the said study,
furnish the same to, and discuss the results and recommendations thereof, with the Bank; and (c) based
on the said study's results and recommendations and subsequent review, comments and discussions



54
prepare draft rules and procedures to govern private sector participation including the solicitation and
evaluation of all forms of proposals for private power supply, furnish the said draft rules and procedures
for review and comments to the Bank, and (iii) by December 31, 1995, finalize, adopt and enforce the
same taking into account the comments, if any, thereon by the Bank (paras 1.25);
(f)   Borrower shall by June 30, 1995, establish a Directorate of Regulation within DGEED with
adequate staff and funds and necessary functions and power to administer and oversee compliance with
regulations (including pricing and technical regulations) regarding the electricity sector (para 1.29);
(g)   Borrower shall: (a) undertake and complete a study on regulatory reform in the power sector
in accordance with terms of reference and in a manner satisfactory to the Bank; (b) upon completion of
the said study, furnish the same for review and comments to, and discuss the results and
recommendations thereof with, the Bank; and (c) based on the said study's results and recommendations
and subsequent review, comments and discussions: (i) prepare draft regulations for the power sector, (ii)
furnish the said draft regulations for review and comments to the Bank, and (iii) by June 30, 1996,
finalize, adopt and enforce the same taking into account the comments, if any, thereon by the Bank (para
1.30); and
(h)   Borrower shall ensure that any transfer of profits from PLN to the DPS shall not prevent
PLN from implementing its agreed investment program (para 5.11).
Agreements Reached with PLN
7.2        During the negotiations, the following agreements were reached with PLN:
(a)   PLN shall: (a) by October 31 of each year commencing in 1995, until completion of the
project, furnish to the Bank for its review and comments PLN's draft annual electrification development
and investment plans; and (b) thereafter finalize the said development plans taking into account the
comments, if any, thereon by the Bank (para 4.4);
(b)   PLN shall select villages to be electrified each year under the project in accordance with
criteria and procedures agreed with the Bank (para 4.24);
(c)   PLN shall maintain the project Management Unit within its Rural Electrification Division
with functions, powers, staff, and funds necessary to coordinate and supervise and to ensure the
implementation of the project (para 4.27);
(d)   PLN shall, by June 30 (or such other date as may be agreed by the Bank) every year
commencing in 1995, revise and publish the small power purchase tariff schedule including the form of
the standard power purchase contract in accordance with the methodology agreed to by the Bank (para
2.32);
(e)   PLN shall, by June 30 (or such other date as may be agreed by the Bank) every year
commencing in 1995, revise and publish the bulk supply tariff schedule in accordance with the
methodology agreed to by the Bank (para 2.34);
(f)   PLN shall: (a) furnish to the Bank, (i) such periodic reports concerning the reorganization
of PLN as reasonably requested by the Bank, and (ii) a draft time bound corporate reorganization and



55
restructuring action plan, based on terms of reference agreed with the Bank, for PLN to operate according
to best utility practices (including an action plan for restructuring specific to rural electrification); (b)
discuss the said report(s) and draft action plan with the Bank; (c) finalize the said action plan taking into
account the comments, if any, thereon by the Bank, and (d) by June 30, 1995, take appropriate steps to
implement the said action plan taking into consideration the comments, if any, thereon by the Bank (para
3.6);
(g)   PLN shall, from time to time, take all such measures, satisfactory to the Bank, as shall be
required for it to realize, for all of its operations, for its fiscal year 1995 and in each fiscal year
thereafter, an annual rate of return of not less than eight percent of the average net revalued fixed assets
in operation (para 5.3);
(h)   PLN shall ensure that the consultants for the various TA components, identified in Table
4.1, will be appointed under terms-of-reference and conditions satisfactory to the Bank, and by no later
than the corresponding dates identified in the Project Management and Implementation Plan (PMIP) report
(para 4.6);
(i)   PLN shall: (a) by October 31, 1995, establish principles and procedures for technical audit
of Subprojects in consultation with the Bank: (b) commencing in 1996, until completion of the Project,
carry out an annual technical audit of the completed parts of the project, through independent technical
auditors acceptable to the Bank in accordance with the principles and procedures acceptable to the Bank,
focussing on the quality and standards of the goods, works, and services financed out of the proceeds of
the Loan including effective utilization thereof, operation and maintenance of the equipment, and the
environmental and resettlement aspects of the Subprojects; and (c) furnish to the Bank as soon as
available, but in case not later than September 30 of each such year, the report of such technical audit
by such auditors and such other information relating thereto in such detail as the Bank may reasonably
request (para 4.28); and
(j)   PLN shall: (a) undertake environmental screening, and resettlement and rehabilitation
planning for subprojects to be financed under the Loan in accordance with criteria and procedures
satisfactory to the Bank; (b) submit, for Bank's approval, a proposal for each Subproject under the mini-
hydro and mini-geothermal component of the project, to be financed out of the proceeds of the Loan
including environmental mitigation and/or resettlement and rehabilitation measures therefor; (c) ensure
that during the execution of each subproject the environmental mitigation and resettlement and
rehabilitation measures therefor are carried out in a manner satisfactory to the Bank; and (d) by April 30
every year commencing in 1996, furnish such information about the environmental mitigation and
resettlement and rehabilitation measures taken during the preceding fiscal year of the Borrower in such
detail as the Bank may reasonably request (paras 4.31, 4.32 and 4.33).
7.3         The following agreements with respect to PLN's financial performance, reached under the
Sumatera and Kalimantan Power Project (Loan 3761-IND), were reaffirmed:
(a)   PLN shall submit audited accounts and reports no later than six months after the end of each
fiscal year (para 3.11);
(b)   PLN shall not incur any additional debt unless a reasonable forecast of its net revenues after
expenditures for each year during the term of the debt to be incurred shall be at least 1.5 times its
projected debt service requirements in such year on all debt of PLN including the debt to be incurred



56
(para 5.5);
(c)   PLN shall: (a) by October 31 every year commencing in 1995 review its long run marginal
costs with a view to formulating its tariff structure; and (b) discuss the results of such review with the
Borrower and the Bank; and (c) thereafter, take appropriate steps to revise the tariff structure taking into
account the views, if any, thereon of the Borrower and the Bank (para 5.10);
(d)   PLN shall prepare a rolling ten-year financial forecast and for the first five years of the
forecast, prepare a financing plan (para 5.13); and
(e)   PLN shall annually revalue its fixed assets and accumulated depreciation (para 5.17).
Condition of effectiveness
7.4        Execution of a subsidiary loan agreement between Borrower and PLN satisfactory to the
Bank (para 4.15).
Actions to be Taken before Withdrawal of Loan Proceeds
7.5        PLN shall complete, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank, a report on the resettlement
impacts, measures and compensation rates for the projected affected persons in the mini-hydro
component, before any disbursements would be approved for a sub-project within that component of the
project (para 4.32); and
7.6        PLN shall complete, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank, the environmental screening and
conceptual engineering design documents including all required environmental mitigation measures for
well test drilling in the mini-geothermal component, before any disbursements would be approved for that
component of the project (para 4.33).
Recommendation
7.7        With the above agreements and conditions, the proposed project is suitable for a loan of US$
398.0 million equivalent to the Republic of Indonesia, for a period of 20 years including a grace period
of 5 years, at the Bank's standard variable interest rate.

Page 1
Government of Indonesia
Ministry of Mines and Energy
GOALS AND POLICEES FOR THE DEVELOPMENT OF
THE ELECTRIC POWER SUB-SECTOR
Dackuound
Indonesia's basic goals and policies for the future development of the energy sector are
described in the Outlines of State Policy (Garis-Garis Besar Haluan Negara or GBHN)
promulgated by the Peoples' Consultative Assembly (MPR) in March 1993 to guide the
formulation of the Sixth Medium-Term (Five-Year) Year Development Plan (REPEIUTA VI)
and Second Stage Long-Term (Twenty-Five Year) Development Strategy (PJPT-II). The GBHN
highlights the importance for Indonesia's sustained economic and social development of meeting
its rapidly growing energy needs efficiently--including through conservation and diversification
of primary energy sources and their more efficient utilization-and of minimizing the adverse
environmental and social impacts of energy use. In the electric power sub-sector, the GBHN
recognizes that an adequate, reliable and reasonably priced electricity supply is essential for the
country's continuing development, emphasizing its role in serving the needs of other productive
sectors and in contributing directly to improving the living stamdards of the people.
The recent development of Indonesia's power sub-sector is characterized by very rapid
growth in the demand for electricity and in the total capacity of the public power supply. Over
the last 15 years, the generating capacity owned and operated by the sole state-owned power
utility, Perum Listrik Negara (PLN). has increased from about 900 MW in 1975/76 to about
11,000 MW in 1992/93. Over this same period, the number of customers served by PLN has
grown by thirteen times. These impressive achievements have been made possible by the
continuing institutional development of PLN, and by a strong commitment on the part of
Government to ensuring that PLN obtained the necessary financial and other resources.
However, the very rapid pace of growth of power demand since the mid-1980s,
attributable to the success of the Govermment's economic policies, has-when coupled with the
country's archipelagic geography-posed challenges that have exceeded PLN's evolving
capacities. Thus while extensive power grids serve most areas of Java and Bali, the coverage
and quality of electricity supply in the outer islands generally remains very low while costs
remain stubbornly high. Moreover, even in Java and Bali, the rapid growth in industrial demand
for power has outstripped PLN's ability to supply, with the result that the capacity of privately-
owned autogeneration plant is now equivalent to around 30% of PLN's installed capacity. The
Government recognizes that the challenges confronting the power sub-sector will necessarily
intensify in the coming plan periods, and that this in turn will necessitate changes to the policies
that have guided its development in the past.
_ ~ ~ ~ ~~~~~~                                                  ~ -   -   - - - -   - - - - - - -_-- - - - - -



67                    ANNEX 1.8
PaSg 2
Policy Goals for REPELITA VI and PJPT-II
During REPELITA VI and PJPT-II the Government's policies for the electric power sub-
sector will be designed to:
(a)   improve  the  performance  of PLN, notably  through  decentralization,
commercialization and corporatization;
(b)   structure electricity tariffs so as to reflect more closely the economic costs of
supply;
(c)   reform and refine the regulatory and institutional framework so as to foster
competition and facilitate increased and more efficient private sector participation;
(d)   enable equal access to primary energy sources at market-determined prices for
publicly and privately owned power generators; and
(e)   develop and implement appropriate measures for inducing energy conservation
and improving environmental protection.
The broad policies for achieving these goals have been established as part of the process of
formulating REPELITA VI and are outlined below.
ImDroving PLN's Performance
The Government recognizes that improving the efficiency and quality of PLN's services
and equipping it to respond effectively to continuing rapid growth in demand for power will
necessitate significant changes to its present organization structure and corporate strategy. These
changes, which will be implemented in a number of carefully planned stages, will entail:
Decentralization: PLN's organization and management processes will be restructured and
decentralized so as to establish profit centers along functional (generation, transmission,
distribution) and geographical lines. Managers of profit centers will be given expanded and
more clearly defined decision-making responsibilities, and will be made accountable for their
performance. A system of contract-based objectives and incentives will be established to
encourage improvements in the operational performance of profit centers.
Commercialization: PLN will be required to function as a commercially oriented enterprise,
with individual profit centers planning and managing their activities in accordance with sound
commnercial principles. Cross-subsidy between commercial and non-commercial businesses will
be phased out, with Government providing explicit subsidies on a trasparent basis for rural
electrification and other social programs that are carefully targeted to benefit low income groups
or regions. The sub-sector regulatory and institutional framework will be enhanced, inter alia
to prevent exploitation of monopoly powers (see below).



ANNEX 1.8
68                                Page 3
Corporatization: PLN's corporate status and structure will be amended to reflect and reinforce
these initiatives and to enable it to compete on an equal basis with private power enterprises.
The present Perum entity will be converted progressively into a group of profit-oriented limited
liability companies (Perseros); these will be empowered to raise funds on the capital markets,
and private participation in their ownership will be sought as soon as their financial performance
permits. Such state-owned companies will be expected to compete in a market-oriented
environment, with Government exercising strategic control over them on an "arm's length" basis
through performance contracts that specify clear objectives and efficiency indicators.
Ructurinir Tariffs
The Government's policies on electricity tariffs are designed, inter alia, to ensure the
sustainable sound financial health of the power sub-sector and, in particular, its capacity to
respond effectively to the rapid growth in demand for electric power. As a first step towards
enabling PLN to play an expanded role in financing the needed massive investments in new and
replacement capacity, the average sales tariff will be increased to enable it to achieve at least
an 8% overall rate of return on revalued assets; this is expected to result in a self-financing ratio
of 30-35% for the period 1994-1999. To avoid erosion over time of the real value of the power
tariff, rates will be adjusted periodically to reflect changes in the costs of supply, with the
mechanism adopted being designed to promote efficiency improvements and tight control of
costs.
The present sales tariff structure will be simplified and the distortions inherent in it will
be progressively reduced so as to enable rates to reflect better the costs of supply. As part of
this process, the uniform national tariff policy will be reassessed during REPELITA VI with a
view to its replacement by a system of two or more regional tariffs. Cross-subsidies between
businesses and between consumer categories will be progressively phased out and replaced by
a transparent system of explicit Government subsidies for social programs or services that benefit
carefully targeted low income groups.
To facilitate increased private participation in the power sub-sector, the Government will
require PLN quickly to establish and publish rates for the purchase of power from small
generators through standard forms of contract, and to establish tariffs for the bulk supply of
power to distribution enterprises. The Government is currently examining mechanisms and
conditions for enabling 'third party access" to PLN transmission grids, and intends that PLN
should introduce a tariff for "power wheeling".
Reforming the Regulatora Framework and Promoting Private Participation
The Government is committed to expanding quickly private sector participation in the
power sub-sector, both to complement PLN's efforts to meet rapidly growing power demands
and to promote healthy competition that will produce improvements in efficiency and standards
of service.. It recognizes that this will require, on the one hand, a transparent, comprehensive
and consistent regulatory framework to define the rules under which state-owned and private
power enterprises will operate, and, on the other, an independent agency to oversee the
operation of these regulations and, in particular, to ensure that the natural monopoly elements



ANNEX 1.8
69                              Page 4
of the system are managed in a manner consistent with the public interest. The oversight agency
will be responsible for ensuring that regulations are administered and applied in a transparent,
timely, and consistent manner, and will be required and empowered to deal with appeals relating
to their implementation.
During 1994, the Government will review the current legal and regulatory framework
for the power sector and plans to formulate the revisions and additions that are needed to secure
the above goals. It will also initiate the establishment of a regulatory oversight agency under
the Ministry of Mining and Energy by separating those functions that relate to power sector
policy and planning from those that relate to the power industry's regulation and oversight. As
a first step, a separate Directorate of Regulation will be created within the Directorate General
of Electric Power and Energy Development. This will subsequently be converted to a higher
level agency with greater powers and independence.
The Government is currently refining its strategy for securing private participation in the
sub-sector, and is strengthening its procedures and capacities for promoting, evaluating, and
negotiating proposals from private entities. While the initial efforts in this area have focused
on the solicitation of proposals for large Build-Own-Operate (BOO) power generation projects,
the existing regulatory framework permits a much broader spectrum of private participation and
considerable attention is now being devoted to exploring other opportunities. Unsolicited
proposals for BOO generation projects are being encouraged, and several are now in preparation
under Letters of Preliminary Approval. Arrangements for permitting small independent power
generators to sell electricity to PLN at published tariffs through standard forms of contract are
being developed and will be finalized shortly. These arrangements are expected encourage more
efficient use of existing autogeneration capacity and to promote development of cogeneration
capacity by industrial plants.
Active consideration is now also being given to expanding the role of private enterprises
and cooperatives in the distribution / supply function, and to permitting private participation in
the shortly-to-be-corporatized elements of PLN. In addition, the Government intends also to
require PLN to provide "third party access" to its transmission grids for independent power
producers. The conditions and charges for such access will be clearly defined and their
implementation will be monitored by the regulatory oversight agency.
Enabling Egual Access to Primary Energy Sources
The Government recognizes that success in securing efficient private participation in large
scale power generation will be contingent on intending private power enterprises being able to
enjoy the same access to and prices for primary energy as are enjoyed by PLN. Accordingly
it intends to review and, where necessary, to revise its current policies in these areas,
particularly with respect to natural gas and geothermal steam, so as to make the markets for
primary energy resources open and transparent.



ANNEX 1.8
70                               Page S
Promoting Energy Conservation and Environmental Protection
Energy conservation is a central theme of the Government's energy policies, and it is
recognized that the appropriate pricing of primary energy and of electric power as outlined above
will play a key role in promoting the economically efficient use of scarce resources. The
Government's commitment to such pricing policies is reflected in the statement of the President,
made in January 1993, concerning the principles that will in future govern the pricing of refined
oil fuels for supply to the domestic market. It recognizes, however, that the proper pricing of
energy products will need to be complemented by other initiatives and measures designed to
encourage and induce sound energy use decisions, particularly on the part of industrial,
commercial, and transport enterprises. In the power sub-sector, the Government is now
developing a strategy for encouraging the more efficient utilization of electricity and the
implementation of this strategy will be accorded high priority during REPELITA VI.
Encouraging conservation and diversification in the use of energy resources is an
important element in the Government's strategy for reducing the adverse environmental and
social impacts of energy consumption. More generally, the Government recognizes that
safeguarding the environment is a prerequisite for sustainable development, and accordingly has
put in place the regulatory framework and institutions needed to achieve this.  During
REPELITA VI, considerable attention and resources will be directed to ensuring that these
regulations are implemented as intended. Within the electric power sub-sector, attention will
be focused on the careful planning and design of new generation, transmission and distribution
facilities so as to minimize and mitigate their impacts on the natural environment and adjacent
communities, and on effective monitoring designed to ensure proper compliance with applicable
standards and practices.
Implementadion
The Government will implement the above policies through a comprehensive and
carefully phased medium-term action plan whose preparation is being coordinated by the
Ministry of Mines and Energy. Other concerned institutions and agencies, most notably
representatives of the People's Representative Assembly and the Ministry of Finance, are being
consulted on those aspects of the action plan that extend beyond the jurisdiction of the Ministry
of Mines and Energy. The action plan is conceived as an integral component of Ministry's
strategy for the development of the energy sector during REPELITA VI, and it is therefore
intended that its key elements will be finalized by the end of 1993.

INDONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRIFICATION PROJECT
Increasing Cost Effectiveness in Service Delivery
System Design Considerations and Pilot Projects
Distribution System Design Changes
1.    A primary technical challenge in RE implementation centers around the need for
continuous research and improvements in equipment and network designs in order to reduce the
cost burden. This means developing lighter and "low tech" equipment designs, and using
construction techniques and management approaches that provide strong incentives for
contractors to reduce the equipment and erection costs of distribution networks. A TA provided
under the RE I project -- on RE design standards and practices -- has paved the way for
introducing lighter and lower cost designs in the proposed project than those employed under
RE I. The major design changes described below, that were determined and agreed to with PLN
for full scale implementation in the proposed project, are conservatively estimated to yield
savings in materials and equipment of about US$40 million (excluding contingencies and taxes)
for the distribution component; for which the estimated material and equipment cost in the
proposed project is about US$350 million:
o     For concrete poles, the specifications in tender documents will be sufficiently
general so as nWt to preclude rectangular and/or Verendiel concrete poles or other
types of concrete poles that meet performance standards. This will be explicitly
stated in the tender document language.
o     For procurement of concrete poles, the option will exist for transport and erection
of such poles by either the construction contractor or the pole manufacture. Pole
manufacturers will be asked to bid ex-factory price and separately a fixed price
for delivery and erection in specific geographical areas. The pole supplier will
be contractually obligated to deliver and erect quantities later nominated by PLN
should this be required. During the construction stage, construction contractors
will be advised of suppliers price for delivery and erection and they would be
given the choice of bidding on the basis of poles already delivered and erected by
the pole supplier, or the construction contractor taking delivery at the factory gate
and erecting the poles themselves. PLN would accept the lower cost alternative.
o     For MV and LV lines, strict application of the ruling spans formula allowing an
increase in average span length from 45 to 50 meters and 40 to 50m respectively.



80
ANNEX 4.2
Page 2
o     Increase in the ratio of single phase overhead lines (OHL) to 3 phase OHL to
reduce conductor quantities.
o     For MV/LV substation transformers, the protection has been modified by using
less LBS's and more fuse cut outs on the MV as well as replacing LVCB's by
disconnecting switch fuses on the LV sides, ensuring reliability of supply and
adequate protection.
The following table summarizes and compares the technical particulars for key components used
in PLN's urban distribution operations and for RE operations.
Pilot Projects
3.    Several areas with potential for further and significant cost reduction were identified
during the course of RE I. While based on demonstrated experience elsewhere they no doubt
would yield cost savings, it was decided to first test and evaluate their adaptability to the
Indonesian environment and alongside advance the state of technical and management readiness
to adopt such measures, if proven to be successful in Indonesia. Specifically, five pilot projects
have been incorporated under PLN's component: (i) Single Wire Earth Return (SWER), (ii)
reinforced concrete (RCC) poles, (iii) low cost substation tap off, (iv) Contract Administration
Company (CAC) and (v) Wood poles.
5.    The SWER system. This pilot involves stringing one single wire for MV with a return
path through earth. Two applications will be tested in the pilot project: (i) low cost spur
extensions to hamlets which surround existing electrified villages (low cost "intensification");
and (ii) spur extension through remote, mountainous terrain with poor or no road access (low
cost "extensification"). The sites chosen for these are, respectively, in Bali and Sulawesi. It
is anticipated that the cost savings as compared with conventional single phase, two wire spurs
would be 50% and 44%, respectively.
6.    Reinforced concrete (RCC) poles. This pilot project involves the manufacture of
reinforced concrete poles in relocatable low cost factories temporally located close to the line
erection site, and using simple light wood moulds. Savings of between 10% and 15% are
expected as compared with poles manufactured in a central factory and then transported over
long distances to the line erection site.
7.    Low cost substation tap off. This pilot project involves the installation of 10MVA
150/20 kV power transformer substations with minimum acceptable protection and facilities and
located midway between conventional zone substations. Savings of about 20% as compared with
conventional substations are expected.
8.    Contract Administration Company (CAC). This pilot project involves letting one
contract for all the distribution construction in a large area -- to be erected to a specified



81
ANNEX 4.2
Page 3
standard and at a fixed price -- to a company which is well skilled in administering contracts.
This company (CAC) then subcontracts the work to smaller construction contractors on a design
and build basis; while guaranteeing the schedule, price and quality standards. This approach for
managing the design and construction puts strong commercial pressures on the CAC and the
construction contractors and would result in smarter (lower cost) designs being implemented as
well as reducing delays in construction. Savings of about 20% of total construction costs are
expected. Savings of between 30% and 40% have been experienced where this model has been
developed in other countries, where even procurement of materials is left to the CAC and/or the
construction contractor.
9.    Wood poles. The objective of this pilot project is to gauge  the feasibility of re-
establishment of a domestic wood power line pole supply industry; specifically as a low cost
alternative to steel poles that presently have to used where use of concrete poles is not feasible
because of access or transportation costs. For this purpose, PLN proposes to issue a small lot
tender for such poles. The expected savings are 60% as compared with steel poles.

INDONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRMICATION PROJECT
PRODUCTIVE USES COMPONENT
Introduction
1.    Productive use consumers are customers in rural electrification areas who use electricity to
produce marketable goods or services. The most common, intensive business consumers of energy are
in the food and beverages, light engineering, garments, and wood based industry sub-sectors, including
rice mills and enterprises producing soybean-based tahu and tempe foods, small tools and other metal
products, wooden furniture, bamboo and rattan products, handicrafts, and roof tiles and other building
materials. The businesses typically are family owned and operated, have less than twenty employees and
are based at the home or adjacent to the home. Most use electricity for lighting. However in many areas
businesses continue to use alternative sources of energy for driveshaft power and few use electrically
powered equipment. Results of surveys of more than 4,000 small businesses carried out in 1992 in the
grid supplied rural areas covered under SBS in North Sumatra, South Sulawesi and Central Java indicate
that their average monthly consumption of productive use energy is in the range of of 40 - 50 kWh, with
the consumption of the more intensive small industry units ranging up to 600 kWh per month. The
studies found that PLN currently supplies only 8 - 15% of this productive use energy market.
2.    Productive uses and sustainability of rural electrification. Productive uses are essential to
establish the long term viability of rural electrification investments and service in Indonesia, particularly
outside Java where the PLN cost structure is higher. Electrification of the poorer, more dispersed, less
densely populated rural areas is undertaken initially often for largely social and political reasons related
to equity concerns and nation building. It is capital intensive and, with the present tariff structure,
requires financial subsidies. Increased productive uses contributes both to lower costs and higher benefit
levels. On the PLN cost side, load development improvements within existing grid systems directly
improve the rate of return of rural electrification. More intensive use through increased productive uses,
which largely occur during daytime hours when residential demand is low, results in greater loads density
and load factors which lower the cost of electricity per kWh. However, while increased productive uses
and other measures may moderate the financial losses, without an overhaul of the tariff structure, revenue
will continue to be less than the LRMC for many rural areas. In this situation, the economic benefits of
expanded and new economic activities and of cost savings which accrue from using PLN for productive
uses provide a principal justification for rural electrification.
3.    This annex is organized as follows. This introduction is followed by a section on the status of
productive uses in rural Indonesia and of the constraints on achieving increases; including the experience
of the SBS component of RE I in promoting productive uses. The rationale, objectives, activities, targets,
costs, benefits and risks of the RBS component of RE II are described in the balance of the annex.



87                                ANNEX 4.S
Page 2
Small Business Services (SBS) Pilot Component of RE I
4.     PLN's share of the existing productive use energy market of small industries, although small, is
larger than indicated by PLN statistics. Admistratively PLN classifies almost all small industries to
residential tariff categories. The explanation appears to be that as PLN has been encouraged increasingly
to operate with a more commercial orientation it resists putting small industries into the I-1 category (450
VA to 2.2 kVA), which fits the great majority, as this is PLN's most subsidized tariff category. In 1993,
PLN reported 7.5 million rural customers of which 11,400 were in an industrial tariff category, of which
350 were classified as I-1. In 1989, PLN had estimated that 244,000 residential customers could be
reclassified to the I-1 tariff category. As PLN statistics on industrial consumers do not reflect actual
levels of small industry productive uses, monitoring under SBS and RBS of kWh consumption is done
for individual enterprises participating in the programs regardless of tariff classification.
5.     Constraints to productive uses. Achieving increases in productive uses in RE areas confronts
constraints on both the supply (PLN) and demand (business) side. On the PLN side, these have included:
(i) the selection criteria for rural electrification areaas, which do not explicitly include existing or
potential productive uses; (ii) the lack of 24 hour service in some isolated systems; (iii) service quality,
particularly outages and voltage fluctuations; (iv) delays in responding to business requests for
connections and upgrading of contracted power; (v) tariff administration, namely the reluctance to use
the I-1 tariff category; (vi) the tariff structure which hinders both initial entry to PLN and the expansion
of installed capacity (the initial deposit; the high charges and no installment payment arrangements for
new business connections and increases in contracted power; and the fixed demand charges); (vii) an
incentive structure which does not encourage PLN staff to promote productive uses; and (viii) the PLN
approach to marketing which emphasises administratively processing and classifing customer requests
rather than promoting PLN services.
6.     On the demand side, constraints include technology, skills, capital, markets, indeed the full range
of internal and extemal enterprise development constraints which hamper business entry and expansion.
The key demand side constraints are in important aspects information constraints (e.g., lack of
information available to potential users on equipment options and sources, PLN prices and connection
requirements, sources of skills training, small loan opportunities and business support services). Many
enterprises have an incomplete or incorrect understanding of what they might do with electricity, how
they might do it, and what it would cost. These information constraints arise given: (i) the passive
marketing of PLN, which has a financial disincentive to promote productive uses as its tariffs for small
productive users (1-1, I-2, R-1, R-2) do not recover even the operating financial costs of providing the
service; (ii) the weak development of supporting social infrastructure and complementary development
programs in many rural areas; and (iii) the absence of the agglomeration economies which occur in more
developed, usually urban areas with higher and more diverse concentrations of business units.
7.     Pilot program  experience todate with SBS, described below, suggests that demand side
information constraints are the key ones. Productive uses increase once they are addressed by active
marketing which communicates the competitiveness of PLN services and provides technical solutions for
customers.  This response is in line with international experience, for example the U.S. rural
electrification progam and more recently as reported for Thailand, which indicates that advertizing and
other promotional efforts can leverage significant increases in productive uses. The importance of
addressing other constraints would increase should the PLN tariff structure be overhauled so that the
energy charge rates paid by small productive users were raised by 15% - 20% to PLN's variable costs



88                             ANNEXA.5
Page 3
level. This would erode much of PLN's current price advantage over diesel (para. 12). In this situation,
in addition to continuing an active marketing program which would emphasise the quality and
convenience of PLN service along with providing technical solutions, it would be appropriate to reduce,
possibly subsidize, the cost of access to PLN by significantly lowering the deposit, the charges for new
connections and increases in contracted power, and the fixed demand charge. The objective would be
a balanced tariff structure with entry and expansion rates which were affordable by higher numbers of
businesses and operating rates which recovered the PLN variable cost of supply, and possibly more to
permit tariffs paid by small productive users to make a contribution to PLN's fixed costs.
8.    SBS strategy. Under the SBS component, PLN actively promotes productive uses of electricity
through the provision of price and technical information in Sibolga and Binjai in North Sumatra, Ujung
Pandung in South Sulawesi and Yogyakarta and Solo in Central Java. The proposition of SBS is that
addressing demand side information constraints through improved marketing by PLN will increase
productive uses of electricity by small businesses in rural areas. After delays in start-up, promotional
work began in early 1993 with field workers of non-governmental organizations (NGOs), through group
and individual techniques, providing potential and existing productive users with tariff and equipment
information, guidance on the comparative costs and other aspects of PLN and diesel power sources, and
demonstrations of appropriate electricity using equipment. The SBS program also provides information
on sources and some assistance in accessing credit and training and, in some areas, other business
development services.
9.    NGOs. PLN contracted with five NGOs to carry out the field level marketing activities of SBS.
This PLN - NGO collaboration has been an unusual aspect of SBS and an important factor in its success.
125 NGO field workers have worked on SBS activities. As expected, they have demonstrated solid
capabilities in informing, training and motivating small rural businesses, thus complementing the technical
skills of PLN staff. With very few exceptions, the NGOs have established good working relationships
with local level PLN staff and representatives of banks, equipment suppliers, and government agencies
involved in complementary rural development activities. Four of the NGOs have been effective in
promoting productive uses. The experience has been that the stronger the field capabilities of an NGO
and the more effective its coordination with local PLN staff, the more likely it is to be a useful partner
of PLN.
10.   SBS results. SBS has been successful in generating applications to PLN from productive users
for connections and increases in contracted kVA leading ultimately to higher kWh sales. The program
has involved 27,000 enterprises; during the first year more than 10,000 have been directly advised
through visits to the enterprise sites; if present trends continue, SBS will have generated more than 4,500
new productive users in less than two years, representing an increase of 5.4 million kWh in PLN annual
sales. In most of the areas the response to the active and effective provision of information has been
rapid; SBS activities in Central Java led in one year to a waiting list of more than a thousand for
connections and increases in contracted power. In one of the five SBS areas, the response has been
considerably slower due to performance and coordination problems within the field program and to the
more limited scope for business development in that area.

i1.   The SBS activities have been effective for a number of reason:
(i)    SBS promotes a very competitively priced service. Taking into account fuel, maintenance and
capital costs, PLN is the least expensive energy source for small rural industries. Comparative cost
studies are being completed during 1994 of businesses using PLN and diesel, the main alternative energy
source for which the government removed subsidies in January, 1993. Initial results indicate that at
current tariff rates PLN is 21% less expensive than diesel fuel (PLN at Rps 120/kWh, the estimated
average small business tariff paid; diesel at the current price of Rps 380/liter and a 0.381 liter per kWh
conversion rate costs Rps 145/kWh). Maintenance cost savings are estimated to be Rps 16/kWh using
PLN rather than diesel. Capital costs savings accrue in cases in which the cost of a diesel generator set
is avoided or in which the electric motor costs the firm less than the alternative diesel motor. These can
be substantial, ranging from Rps 107 to 428/kWh for the avoided cost of a generator set (Rps 3.5 millon
for an installed 7.5 HP unit, a common small industry size; 12% opportunity cost; 15 year life; 400 to
100 kWh per month).
(ii)   The business environment is which most small industries operate is very competitive. Many
enterprises have numerous existing competitors and for those who are in activities with few barriers to
entry, the threat of competition from possible new entrants is continual. The small woodworking
enterprise, for instance, which changes to power equipment is able to respond to orders for furniture
components more quickly, with finer and more accurate pieces, in greater volume and with less wastage



90                              ANNEX 4.5
Page 5
of raw material than the competitor who continues to use a combination of diesel and manual power.
Once such a technology change is made by one firm it raises the local standard of best practices to a new
level. The experience of SBS has been that a group of similar firms soon follows suit.
(iii) Institutional and human factors, including the management and coordination of PLN's SBS staff, the
guidance and assistance of the SBS Advisor who was contracted to provide technical support for SBS,
the field capabilities of the NGOs (para. 9), and the cooperation of the PLN regional, branch and sub-
branch officers who have been involved, have been important elements in SBS performance. The
appraisal of RE I had considered that one of the risks for the project was the uncertainty over whether
there would be adequate backing from PLN for the marketing activities of SBS. In the event, the backing
has been ample and the field level cooperation has been supportive of SBS success.
12.   Lessons learned. The experience with SBS indicates that: (i) productive uses can be increased
by effectively addressing information constraints through improved PLN marketing efforts focused on
promoting the changeover by small industries from diesel or manual power sources to PLN; (ii) other
constraints can be offset in many cases by effective marketing given the current overall price, quality and
convenience competitiveness of PLN; (iii) coordination is required between PLN - NGO marketing and
PLN operational functions to avoid situations in which PLN is not able to respond to service requests
generated by successful marketing, for example as occurred in Central Java due to a local shortage of
meters, miniature circuit breakers and twisted cables; (iv) promotional efforts should be mounted in areas
in which the technical attractiveness of PLN services is adequate and not limited by a high level of
outages and voltage fluctuations.
13.   Adjustments to strengthen implementation. SBS completed its first 12 month phase in January,
1994. An internal evaluation was completed at the end of 1993 and overall performance and results were
reviewed by the mission with PLN and NGO staff and officials. It was agreed that for its second phase,
which is scheduled to be completed before the end of 1994, in order to increase both effectiveness and
efficiency a series of internal operating adjustments would be incorporated so that SBS would: (i) be more
targeted on changeovers from diesel or manual energy sources and less on the start up of new business;
(ii) be more targeted on higher energy industry sector units and less on commercial and service sector
enterprises; (iii) use more efficient screening methods and thus spend less time with enterprises not likely
to changeover to PLN; (iv) use streamlined and simpler internal monitoring and reporting systems; (v)
include comparative studies of economic and social impacts; (vi) cover four rather than five areas; and
(vii) include more involvement by PLN field officials in field operations.
Rural Business Services component of RE II.
14.   Overview. Under the proposed project, the experience with SBS will be built upon to mount
programs to promote productive uses in each of PLN's 14 regions (11 wilayah and three distfibusi). A
particular emphasis will be to develop promotion approaches which can be effective in areas outside Java.
RBS is designed to be an advance on the experimental efforts of SBS in several ways. RBS would focus
on attracting the initial group of small industries in targeted sub-sectors in an area through short, intensive
information and advisory campaigns rather than, as under SBS, seeking to convert a high proportion of
the businesses in an area through a relatively long presence in each area. Secondly, the enterprise
selection criteria would emphasise industry sector units rather than all small businesses. Thirdly, RBS
is expected to have a stronger implementation capability as a result of greater involvement of PLN branch



91                              ANNEX 4.$
Page 6
and sub-branch staff, more refined enterprise targeting and screening, improved monitoring and reporting
methods, a simpler menu of services (i.e., avoiding trying to solve all business development problems),
standardization in promotional materials, and a central NGO training capability for NGO and PLN field
workers.
