15.   RBS objective. The objective of RBS is to increase the productive use of electricity in rural
areas. Critical success indicators would be: (i) a 100% increase in the PLN share of the productive uses
kWh energy market of the rural industries in targeted sub-sectors within the program areas; and (ii)
30,000 rural industries assisted to start or enhance their use of PLN electricity for productive purposes.
The first indicator is intended to encourage the direction of RBS efforts to the more intensive energy
users; the second is intended to encourage the program to promote productive uses of electricity to a wide
number of potential and existing small industry units.
16.   Strategy. The RBS would: (i) address information constraints to productive uses by actively
advising customers and potential customers on price and technical aspects of PLN services and the
technology options for electric motors; (ii) reduce tariff administration barriers to increased productive
uses by correctly classifying or reclassifying small industries under the industry rather than residential
tariff categories; (iii) strengthen PLN corporate capabilities to provide services to rural business.; (iv)
monitor and report on results. The quality of PLN's services to rural businesses would continue to be
monitored under RBS. The approach of RBS is for PLN working with NGOs to direct marketing efforts
at selected industry sub-sectors in an estimated three rural sub-branch districts in each of up to 25 area,
11 in Java and Bali and 14 on other islands, covering all 14 PLN regions. This would be done through
six month promotional and advisory campaigns in each area.
17.   Activities. RBS implementation would comprise four main areas:



92                                 NNEX 4.5
Page 7
(i)    Marketing. A core theme of RBS would be to promote productive uses in low cost ways by:
-      using up to 25 locally based NGOs to provide PLN business customers and potential
customers with price and technical information on the use of PLN services for productive
purposes.
-      using one centrally based NGO to train field staff of local NGOs and participating cabang
and ranting level staff of PLN and to assist in carrying out preparations and guiding field
programs of local NGOs.
-      organizing intensive promotional campaigns carried out for short periods, say six months,
in each area.
-      using standardized marketing brochures, leaflets, advertisements, posters, training videos,
etc., and advisory instruments (e.g., comparative fuel costs and cash flows with and
without PLN).
-      using locally based mass communication techniques and indirect marketing channels (e.g.,
through equipment suppliers and Department of Small Industry) to attract businesses into
making contact with the program.
-      using group based and individual methods for advising businesses.
-      identifying and systematically executing in each area the preparation and implementation
of key marketing tasks, including: consultation with other agencies and review of PLN
data on electrification and productive uses leading to the detailed selection of specific
rantings as target areas and specific target industry sub-sectors; briefing of PLN field
staff and staff of other agencies; selection and contracting of local NGO through a
competitive process; training of PLN and NGO staff; carrying out of survey to establish
energy market baseline and targets; promotional activities; monthly reporting.
(ii)    Tariff administration. In addition to actively informing small industries of its pricing structure
and technical solutions, PLN would improve its attractiveness to productive users by:
providing clear and possibly revised guidance to field staff on the criteria to be applied
in classifying small industries under the II or I2 tariff code. The current practice is to
assign home-based industries an RI or R2 tariff code rather than the Il code specifically
provided for home based industries in PLN's official tariff structure.
correctly classifying new small industry customers under the ll or 12 tariff code.
reclassifying as Il or I2 small industries presently assigned an RlI/R2 tariff code.
using special promotional measures, including linking "PPELK" (PLN's program in line
with a broader national objectives to promote subcontracting between large and small
firms) with RBS activities.



93                               ANNEX4.5
Page 8
(iii)   Corporate capabilities. PLN's capabilities to provide rural business services would be increased
by:
-      contracting the services of an RBS advisor and providing the advisor with adequate
logistical, supplies, administrative, and other support.
-      staffing the PLN unit responsible for RBS with three full time PLN professionals.
-      training of PLN field staff, RBS staff and senior PLN staff. Training will be provided
for an estimated 10 PLN staff from each of the 25 areas for seven days at a PLN training
facility and for 11 PLN staff two months of overseas training.
-      issuing an operational directive setting out PLN's policies and promotional efforts for
rural businesses.
-      developing more data collection and analysis capabilities on productive uses.
-      increasing the internal PLN information flow about productive uses.
(iv)   Monitoring and reporting. During the implementation of SBS the monitoring and reporting
arrangements, after some uncertainty at the outset, were steadily improved. The arrangements for RBS
for monthly, quarterly and annual reports and annual plans would reflect further improvements by:
-      modifying as appropriate and using the management information system developed under
SBS to monitor RBS inputs and results performance.
-      using PLN computer system at wilayah and distribusi as key monitoring points.
-      setting clear targets and indicators and issuing standardized reporting formats and
software to local PLN officials and local NGOs.
-      adopting an evaluation plan and conducting three meetings of PLN and NGO participants
to systematically review progress and agree any changes in activities or direction which
may be warranted.
18.   Performance indicators. In addition to the critical success indicators (para. 15) of (i) achieving
a 100% increase in the PLN share of the productive uses kWh energy market in the selected sub-sectors
and tii) assisting 30,000 rural industries to become productive users of electricity, RBS performance
indicators would be: (iii) the number of enterprises advised, (iv) the number of enterprises assisted to
connect to PLN and to increase their contracted power, (v) the number assisted to become productive
users without the need for a new connection or increase in contracted power, and (vi) the number of
enterprises assisted to be reclassified from a residential to an industry tariff code. Also, the following
would be monitored: (vii) employment impacts in assisted enterprises; (viii) kWh sales to assisted
enterprises; (ix) service interruptions in the targeted areas; and (x) complaints of industry customers in
the targeted areas.

19.   Organization - From PLN Pusat RBS would be implemented by an RBS unit responsible for
overall management, technical direction, coordination and reporting, including selecting the target areas
and industry sub-sectors and contracting and supervising the participating NGOs. The RBS unit would
be composed of a Project Manager, a Deputy Project Manager, three professional assistants and four
support staff. The RBS unit would be advised and assisted by an RBS Advisor backed by at least one
support staff and occasional local and foreign short term consultants. The terms of reference of the
Advisor are attached. A central NGO with experience under SBS will be contracted to assist PLN with
the implementation of RBS. The central NGO will conduct training for local PLN staff and for staff of
the participating local NGOs, assist PLN in the preparatory, supervision and follow-up activities in each



ANNEX 4.5
95                                   Page 10
area, prepare and reproduce informational and mass communication materials. In each of the 25 RBS
areas, local PLN-RBS teamns composed of two professionals from the wilaya or distribusi office, three
from each participating branch and two from each participating sub-branch will coordinate the
implementation of RBS activities, including those of the local NGOs. They will be responsible for
implementing efforts to reclassify small industry consumers from residential to industry tariff categories,
assisting in the identification of target sub-branch areas and industry sub-sectors, monitoring results of
the programn and of PLN services to productive customers, increasing collaboration with other agencies
concerned with rural development, particularly with rural industries, and suggesting strategies and
adjustments to the RBS unit at PLN Pusat. Local NGOs will be contracted in the 25 areas to implement
the program including identifying constraints, carrying out baseline surveys, promoting productive uses
through activities which reduce information constraints, (for example, mass communications, group
sessions, individual counseling, comparative demonstrations, equipment demonstrations), and developing
links for enterprises with private and public sector entities to address skills and finance constraints. In
each area, there will be an NGO coordinator, three supervisors and field workers - 18 per program on
Java and Bali and 12 per program in other areas.
20.   Selection of RBS areas - Under RBS the strategy for area selection would be to concentrate on
areas which have been electrified with grid supply and which have a high potential for increasing
productive uses. High potential is defined as having a large concentration of business with the potential
to changeover from diesel or manual power to PLN. Low potential areas would be considered,
particularly off Java, on a case by case basis depending on the scope for introducing new uses. Rural
areas would be defined as rural rantings which have minimum 70% of customers and potential customers
residing in villages; which are grid supplied; and, which have a low load factor. Areas which have a low
waiting list for connections by rural businesses and in which PLN management in the area indicates
strong interest and support for promoting productive uses would be accorded special priority. RBS would
operate in areas in which PLN has the technical capability to respond to requests for connections and
increases in contracted kVA.
21.   Selection of businesses for participation in RBS. The strategy for participant selection is to
focus on existing or potential PLN customers who are operating businesses with high energy use
potential. An average 100 kWh per month consumption for productive purposes would define the target
category, as compared with an average productive use consumption for rural businesses of 40 - 50 kWh
(para. 4) Consideration would also be given to promoting new business development on a special basis
in high energy use activities. Efforts would be concentrated on businesses with high technical potential
to changeover from diesel or manual power to PLN, which are successful businesses, which have
potential for reclassification of tariff code, or which have a new use potential.  In rural areas, the
enterprises with high potential for productive uses of electricity are in the manufacturing sector, including
agro-industries, garments, wood products including furniture, and metal products sub-sectors.
22.   Scheduling. It is anticipated that SBS will be phased into RBS during the second half of 1994.
RBS would begin formally with the arrival of the RBS Advisor and the start of activities of the central
NGO in October, 1994. By February, 1995, after start-up and preparatory local training of NGOs, the
first five programs will be initiated, concluding by the end of September, 1995. Allowing for follow-up,
evaluation and redesign, ten additional programs would be carried out in calendar year 1996, and an
additional ten in 1997.
23.   Budget. The estimated cost of RBS is US$7.5 million before contingencies and other provisions.

24.   Risks. The risks with RBS are several. Implementation will take place in 14 PLN regions and
involve field workers of up to 25 NGOs. The implementation management risk is clear. The proposed
strengthening of the PLN Pusat unit responsible for RBS, the use of an experienced central NGO to train
the local NGO field workers and to assist PLN in guiding their field work, more standardized and
streamlined approaches to promotional activities and reporting, and closer supervision by PLN regional
officials to ensure that problems are spotted quickly and corrected, are measures introduced to assist PLN
meet this management challenge. Secondly, there is the risk that PLN field staff or the NGO field
workers might attempt to carry out too many tasks in trying to solve a wide range of problems
confronting businesses (e.g., credit, business management practices) and their communities. This
tendency, particularly of some NGOs which have a strong social orientation, to become an all purpose
rural development effort was apparent in some areas during the first phase of SBS. This risk has been
addressed by targeting RBS more sharply, by defining the role of the NGO field workers more clearly
and by supporting them better with training and promotional materials. Also, of course, PLN now has
experience in working with NGOs and is more skilled at managing these relationships. Thirdly, there
may be a tendency to use the services of the RBS Advisor for tasks such as administration, including
basic gathering, preliminary verification and review of information, and computer programming. When
this occurs, as it did during the first phase of SBS, it detracts from the overall guidance and analytical



97                               ANNEX4.5
Page 12
work required to be done by the Advisor to ensure that the program continues to learn and adjust to the
insights being generated and that the project contributes to increases in knowledge about the methods to
increase productive uses and their impacts. This has been addressed in discussions with PLN officials
as well as through the strengthening of the RBS unit at PLN Pusat. PLN's awareness of the need to
utilize effectively the services of the Advisor and their experiences with SBS are expected to contribute
to the Advisor being able to devote his time to his primary tasks.
25.   Benefits.  RBS would be implemented in areas which have been electrified and in which
productive uses appears to be low. It would encourage businesses, mainly small industries, to improve
their production technology by switching from manual or diesel based power to PLN electricity. It is
anticipated that most of the RBS enterprises will be switching from diesel to PLN, or will be selecting
PLN rather than diesel as they upgrade their energy source from manual. For businesses, the financial
benefits of using PLN rather than diesel are clear (para. 11). For PLN, while increased productive uses
means a more intensive use of the infrastructure, improved load factors and a lower cost per kWh, there
can be no net financial benefit of increasing service at the present subsidized rates.
26.   Economic benefits from  increased productive uses of electricity which result from  these
changeovers and upgradings include those represented by the net increase in value of production. Taking
the most conservative assumption, that a firm's output would be the same using an alternative source,
then the economic benefit would be the value of the avoided private cost of the alternative. However,
the economic benefits associated with switching to electricity go well beyond those quantified by this
approach. This technology change is an element in the structural transformation process of rural
development as communities adjust to changing competitive conditions and become more integrated in
the regional and wider economies. As anticipated based on experience in other countries, for many SBS
firms which have become PLN customers for the first time the benefits include longer working hours,
greater security, better work quality. For existing customers who formerly were using PLN only for
lighting and domestic use, the switch to electric equipment often has led to more efficient operations and
improved quality. Although in many cases there is apparently a labor productivity increase, with greater
capital investment per worker, there have been no reports of employment being reduced in the
participating enterprises as a result of adopting electricity for productive use. In sectors and areas where
there are growing markets, some enterprises report increasing their product range, employment, sales and
profits. Ultimately, these and other broad development impacts associated with the technology change
made possible by using electricity for productive purposes are a main justification of rural electrification.
While electricity on its own will not lead to a more productive rural economy, for most rural areas
development will not occur without it.
27.   Productive uses promotion. A long recognized issue in Indonesia is that the productive use of
PLN electricity in many rural areas is comparatively low. New uses do not appear to increase
substantially following electrification and existing productive users of alternative energy sources
changeover to PLN slowly or not at all. One of the constraints to productive uses has been that
insufficient information has been provided to users and potential users. This constraint was addressed
on a pilot basis by the Small Business Services (SBS) component under RE I with special promotion
programs in two of PLN's regions outside Java and in one region on Java. Working in collaboration with
NGOs, PLN actively informed existing and potential small business customers of the opportunities,
methods and advantages of using electricity for productive purposes. Results indicate that such marketing
efforts which provide price and technical information are effective in increasing the number of rural
productive users of PLN electricity. Under the Rural Business Services (RBS) component of RE H this



98                               ANNEX 4.S
Page 13
approach would be expanded to all 14 of PLN's regions using a more targeted and efficient approach
which draws upon the lessons learned under RE I.

1.          This annex identifies the points that would be reviewed in the
environmental chapter of the subproject feasibility report. It should be noted,
however, that they are often not an issue in the subproject. In those cases it should
be mentioned in the environmental chapter that these issues have been considered but
are not present in the subproject under review.
2.          Relevant Background Data.  It is assumed that the following
information is already present in the subproject feasibility report:
*     Project Plan with maps indicating route(s) and obstacles such as
mountains, forests, lakes, rivers, ravines, etc.
*     Land allotment, protected lands, protected forests and other protected
areas;
D     Project implementation including land clearing, excavation, and access
road construction;
D     List of heavy equipment if used in the project.
3.          Meteorological Data
(a)   Describe relevant meteorological data for wind direction, speed and
seasonality; Description of corridors used for the subproject and land
acquisition requirements;
(b)   List proposed crossings of forests, protected forests, natural parks,
protected areas and wetlands (including swamps);
(c)   List all corridors that are not situated along roadsides using public
owned land, such as corridors over agricultural land, over plantations,
and over other sensitive areas;
(d)   List corridor crossings of rivers, lakes and other waterways;

101                        Page 2
(e)   Indicate surface areas required for the right-of-way corridors through
the areas mentioned under paras. 4, 5 and 6.
(f)    Estimate number of trees that have to be cut in forests, protected forests
and natural parks for the right-of-way corridors. Indicate how these
corridors and the ones through protected areas and wetlands will be
maintained: if the use of herbicides cannot be avoided describe how
spraying will be done (spraying from airplanes should be avoided);
(g)   If corridors through wetlands are required indicate if mangrove forests
are touched and describe measures to prevent cutting mangrove trees;
(h)   Describe flooding risks if corridor crosses a flood area and indicate the

dwellings and communities;
(m)   Describe measures proposed to minimize impact on birds especially
migratory and rare species from river, lake and wetland corridor
crossings.
4.          Alternative Corridors. If construction of a power line requires a
corridor through environmentally sensitive areas; alternative corridors should be
discussed and evaluated, and definitive recommendations should be made for the
corridor that has the least environmental impact within reasonable economic limits.
5.          Land Acquisition

amount of land to be acquired and this should be disaggregated to show
both the type of land use and ownership; (ii) the number of people that

will need to be compensated and the number that will be displaced; and
(iii) the tenure status of and extent of impact on the affected people
(i.e., amount and proportion of land and/or house affected).
6.          Resettlement
(a)   It is assumed and should be confirmed in the environmental chapter that
no corridors shall go over domestic buildings (dwellings and others).
However, if this is unavoidable because no reasonable alternative
routing is available these families should be relocated: ;indicate the
number of families that have to be relocated or resettled;
(b)   Describe all arrangements for resettlement, and relocation taking into
account the framework for resettlement issues that has been agreed
between the Government of Indonesia and the World Bank (Annex 4.8
to this document).
7.          Compensation for land, housing, lost crops. This section should
include: (a) estimates of compensation rates for land, different categories of buildings
and major crops in the area; and (b) description of procedures for compensation that
will be applied for non-land-owners that have rented lands.

INIONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRIFICATION PROJECT
FRAMEWORK FOR PREPARATION OF
INVOLUNTARY REsIrEMEmF   CoMPoNENTs
The following basic principles and criteria have been agreed upon
between the Government of Indonesia and the World Bank. Although in first instance
agreed for road projects they are sufficiently general to apply to any project requiring
involuntary resettlement. The agreed framework is that to be eligible for financing the
action plan for subprojects involving resettlement/relocation will meet the following
requirements:
(a)   Subprojects will be designed and selected in a manner which will help
minimize displacement and adverse effect on incomes of affected
people;
(b)   All subprojects requiring resettlement/relocation, even of a few people,
will provide for appropriate compensation for assets based on market
value (including logistical support for moving and relocation as
required);
(c)   Action plans will provide for all measures and compensation required
to restore the standard of living of all affected people, as fairly and
equally as possible, whether their land rights are customary or formal;
(d)   Preparation of these action plans will be based, inter alia, on
consultation of affected people, surveys of land titles and ownership of
dwellings and of affected residents;
(e)   The action plans will have a clear implementation time frame and
construction works will not start within or adjacent to the relocation
area before required resettlement has been completed;
(f)   The process will be transparent. Information relating tao the people
affected, the compensation they are entitled to, the available options or
alternatives and the grievance and appeals procedures will be provided
by the project manager and made readily available to the executing
agency and the World Bank;
(g)   The action plans will specify the organizational responsibilities for
implementation and supervision and measures needed to strengthen the



ANNEX 4.8
104                       Page 2
capabilities of agencies involved in accordance with the requirements of
the prevailing GOI laws and regulations;
(h)   The final subproject cost estimates will include all costs required for the
satisfactory implementation, and the management and monitoring of the
land acquisition and resettlement/relocation plans and the World Bank
approval will be subject to all necessary funds having been budgeted
for.

105                       Page 1
INDONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRIFICATION PROJECT
GUIDELINES FOR THE ENVIRONMETAL SCREENIG
As REQUnIED FOR ENVIRONMENTAL ANALYSIS FOR M[IM-HYDRO PROJECTS
The annex identifies points that would be reviewed in the environmental
screening of mini-hydro projects, including related works on civil, mechanical and
electrical engineering to provide water diversion dams, powerhouses, water intake
structures, water conveyance structures, electrical systems, grid interconnection, etc.
Estimates of the areal extent of the proposed project should be included in the
environmental analysis as well.
SCOPE OF WORK FOR ENVIRONMENTAL ANALYSIS
Project Plan
1.    Provides a general project plan and its schedule and provide two maps drawn on
scale, one of the general area and one for the project area;
Climate
2.    Describe climate type, and meteorological data for temperature, rainfall
(seasonality), wind (direction, speed, seasonality);
Geology
3.    Describe land morphology, topography and geologic structure.  Is project-
locatedin an earthquake zone, if so, provide quantitative data for the frequency and
intensity of earthquakes;
Hydrology
4.    Provide river stream flow rates (monthly maxima and minima from historical
records);
5.    What data area available describing water availability in all seasons; and if
available give surface and groundwater quality data during all seasons, indicate
surface and groundwater recharge areas, all uses of water (including irrigation,
industrial and other uses), sources of water for drinking, bathing, clothes washing;
6.   If the water availability for the different uses is impaired by the project, describe
the impact and project, describe the impact and propose mitigation measures to
provide this water at the same quality;



106                        ANNEX 4.9
Page 2
7.    Describe river flow disruption during the dry season caused by the project.
Describe high sedimentation during construction and immediately after construction
and measures proposed to mitigate the situation. Describe the impacts on aquatic
organisms during construction (with high sedimentation) and after implementation
of the project;
Land and Soil
8.    Describe land stability, adaptability, layout and use, land allotment, protected lands
and soil types, structures and texture;
9.    Does the project has an impact on the land stability? If so, describe measures to
stabilize the land;
Flora and Fauna
10. Describe types of flora and fauna (land and water), and types of protected flora and
fauna;
11.   Describe impact of project on wildlife;
12.   Are surveys available describing endangered biological species that might be
threatened by the proposed MHPP?
Health
13.   What are local health facilities and are there surveys of communicable diseases in
the local communities that could be affected especially during the construction
phase of the project;
14.   Will imported labor force be checked on communicable diseases?
15.   What will be the impact of the project on water-borne diseases and parasites.
Especially for those sections of the rivers that will fall dry during periods that the
project will fully use all available river water.
Construction
16.   Provide a list of project activities, including construction, land clearing, land
excavation, access road construction, transmission line installation, etc.;
17.   Provide an estimate of the size of the area used for the project activities, including
the main structures, road, transmission lines, and penstocks;
18.   List the heavy equipment to be used in the project construction activities;



ANNEX 4.9
107                       Page 3
19.   Describe the impact of this equipment on exiting roads and measures to be taken
to repair any damage;
20.   Describe in a general way the various building materials to be used (type, quantity
or volume), where these materials are to be found and how collected, their means
of being transported to the site and their storage and the final disposal system for
any wastes generated;
21.   Describe the environmental impacts on the sites where these materials are collected,
or quarried;
22.   Describe how the MHP facilities will be operated, including any special
environmental management controls;
23.   Describe the types of equipment used in operating the generating systems, including
giving the plant's capacity rating;
24.   Describe fully the environmental impact of the construction activities;
25.   Describe the number of construction workers and the education and expertise
required, whether or not these workers can be hired locally, where they will reside
during construction and their ages and sex;
26.   Describe any training that will be given to workers;
27.  Describe the number of operating employees and the education and expertise
required, and whether some people can be hired locally;
28.   Describe any training that will be given to operators;
29.   Describe noise intensity around the power house, how much it is over ambient
noise levels and what measures will be taken to mitigate a noise problem;
30.   Describe any existing activities adjacent or near the project that could have a
positive or negative effect on the project;
31.   Do project plans provide for an adequate buffer between project (construction and
operation) and any conflicting adjacent land uses? What mitigation is planned for
such cases (vegetative screening)?
Cultural-socio-economic-aesthetic impacts
32.   Describe population profiles (age, sex, education, religion, income, health), attitude
and perception of the community toward development, condition of the socio-
cultural heritage and other relevant information.



ANNEX4.9
108                       Pogo 4
33.   Are there surveys for the area's archaeological, aesthetic and cultural resources that
might be threatened by the MHPP?
34.   Describe cultural impacts (including on local religion, on local archeological
treasures and loss of riparian vegetational used for artisan or nmdicinal purposes);
It should be noted that not all the impacts mentioned will be prsent in all
the MHP projects under consideration. If not existent the ES should mention this. Some
other impacts could also be quite positive.

INDONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRIFICATION PROJECT
NoTES AND ASSumpTONS FOR THE FINANCIAL FoRzcAm
1.        Inflation and Rupiah to US dollar exchange rate have been taken into account
as follows:
(a)  for foreign costs (including indirect foreign costs, excluding fuels), 2.1 percent
per annum in 1991, 4.3 percent per annum in 1992, -0.6 percent per annum in
1993, and 2.2 percent per annum each year thereafter; and
(b)  for local costs (excluding indirect foreign costs and fuels) at 9.4 percent per
annum in 1991, 7.5 percent per annum in 1992, and 9.7 percent per annum in
1993 and 5.0 percent per annum each year thereafter.
(c)  a constant exchange rate of 2,155 was used throughout the forecast period.
2.        Separation of accounts of PLN's operations in Java and outsideJavaisbased
on individual accounts maintained by PLN for each of its regional units. These accounts are
separate except for overhead expenses, which, for the purposes of financial projections, have
been allocated proportionately on the basis of unit sales of energy.
3.        Energy sales are based on the sales forecasts in Annexes 2.5 and 2.6.
4.        Revenues for FY93 are based on the estimated average revenue per unit sold in
that year.
5.        Fuel/purchased power costs for FY93 are based on the estimated outcome for
that year, and for FY94 and beyond are derived from PLN's computer simulation of system
operation. The fuel costs are calculated on the basis of current domestic fuel prices
(established in July 1993) charged to PLN: fiel oil - Rp 240/1 plus transportation charges of
about Rp 24/1 for Java and Rp 34/1 for Outside Java; diesel - Rp 380/t plus transportation
charges of about Rp 27/1 for Java and about Rp 32./1 for Outside Java; coal - Rp 70/kg for
Java and Rp 59.2/kg for Outside Java; geothermui - Rp 53.76/kWh for plants at Kamojang,
Dieng and Outside Java, and US$0.047/kWh for plants at Salak and Drajat; and natural gas -
US$2.53/thousand standard cubic feet (MSCF) for Java and US$3.00/MSCF for Outside Java.
The fuel prices to PLN are assumed to remain at their current level throughout 1994; from
1995 onwards they are escalated on the assumption that prices for fuel oil, diesel and coal will
change in line with international prices and the prices for natural gas and geothermal will
remain constant in dollar terms. Purchase of power from private investors who would
construct generating plant in Indonesia on a 'Build, Own and Operate" (BOO) basis, which
is projected to commence from 1997, is assumed to be at US$0.082AkWh (in 1993/94 prices).



ANNEX 5.2
119                       Page 2
6.         PLN's diesel and fuel oil consumption in Java-Bali is expected to decrease from
about 2,080 thousand kiloliters (kl) and about 2,730 thousand id respectively in 1993 to 490
thousand Il and 880 thousand kl in 1994, due partly to the retiring of all diesel generators
(about 115 MW), and partly to the conversion of some gas turbine and combined cycle power
plants from oil to natural gas. This decrease is expected to be followed by a sharp increase
in diesel use in 1995 to 2,400 thousand kl, due to the commissioning of combined-cycle (1,500
MW) and gas turbine (600 MW) power plants, which will be temporarily operated on oil.
7.        Operating expenses, other than fuel and personnel expenses, have been forecast
taking into account the facilities expected to be in use each year, and subject to foreign and
local cost inflation, as appropriate. Personnel expenses are estimated to increase about
19 percent per annum to provide for inflation, volume growth and staff training. Repair and
maintenance expenses for PLN's Java and outside-Java operations are estimated at about
2.1 percent and about 3.5 percent of their respective gross fixed assets in operation during the
preceding fiscal year.
8.        Depreciation is based on the application of PLN's normal rates (straight-line
method) to assets revalued annually. For purposes of corporate income tax, the current
Indonesian tax code permits the use of the accelerated method of depreciation of assets in
operation instead of the straight-line method.
9.        Financing. Terms of foreign borrowings are assumed to be 20 years including
5 years' grace, at average interest rates of 13.5 percent per annum. Terms of local borrowings
are assumed to be 12 years including two years' grace, at interest rates of 20 percent per
annum; local bonds are assumed to be issued at similar interest rates with a maturity of five
years.
10.       Capital expenditures are based on PLN's investment program (Annexes 2.7 and
2.8), and exclude capital expenditures for generation projects proposed to be implemented by
private investors, and the costs of geothermal steam field development, which are assumed to
be incurred by other agencies. The 10 percent Value Added Tax (VAT) on the foreign
contracts is assumed to be borne by Government, and on the local contracts it is assumed to
by borne by PLN.
11.       Government equity contributions are assumed to be equal to about 23 percent
of PLN's capital expenditures in 1994/95, and to 15 percent of annual capital expenditures
from 1995/96 onwards.
12.       Gross fixed assets, work in progress and consumers' contributions are
revalued annually, up to FY86 according to Government Regulation No. 45/1986, dated
October 2, 1986, and from FY87 onwards, according to weighted annual escalation rates
(foreign and local). The rate base is the average of the revalued net fixed assets in operation,
less revalued consumers' contributions. The rate base does not include work in progress.
13.       Customer accounts receivables are assumed at about 36 days of energy revenues,
which is the current level.



ANNEX 5.2
120                       Page 3
14.       Transmission and Distribution (T&D) system losses are estimated to be about
12.2 percent for Java in FY93/94, and are assumed to decline to 12.0 percent in 1994/95 and
stabilize thereafter. T&D losses for outside-Java are estimated to be about 15 percent in
FY93/94, and are assumed to decline gradually to about 13 percent in FY98/99.
15.       In accordance with the prevailing Indonesian tax legislation effective since 1984,
PLN is subject to a 10 percent value added tax (VAT) and to corporate income tax. The
Government currently bears the VAT on the foreign contracts, and PLN bears it on the local
contracts; this policy is assumed to continue throughout the projection period. With respect
to corporate income tax, the current Indonesian tax code permits the use of accelerated
depreciation of assets in operation, instead of the straight-line method used by PLN in its
financial accounting. As a result, PLN does not expect to pay any corporate income tax for
its fiscal year 1992, and during the projection period up to 1997. In calculating the rate of
return, however, any income tax payments will be taken into account.
16.       By the Minister of Finance decree No. 1232/KMK.013/1989 dated November 11,
1989, PLN is required to contribute to a fund for the Development of Cooperatives and Weak
Economic Businesses (DCWEB) an amount equal to 5.0 percent of its annual net income after
corporate tax. Such contributions for DCWEB commenced from 1991.
17.       With the conversion of PLN from Perum to Persero on August 1, 1994, PLN is
no longer under the obligation to contribute 55 percent of its annual net income, after
contribution for DCWEB (para. 15) and corporate tax, to the Overall Development Fund
(DPS). However, since contributions to DPS due for 1990 onwards are paid in five equal
installments commencing two years after the year for which the payment to DPS is due, PLN
is still obliged to pay the remaining installments for DPS incurred before August 1, 1994 (until
FY2001). Following Government Regulation Number 12 of 1969, PLN as a "Persero" is
obliged to pay a dividend, the policy for which is to be determined by MOF by year-end 1994,
to its shareholders (currently MOF/State Treasury). A covenant was agreed that would limit
PLN from declaring any dividend or making any other distribution with respect to its share
capital, unless, after such dividend has been paid or other distribution has been made, the
current assets of the Borrower would equal or exceed 1.0 times the current liabilities of the
Borrower.

INDONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRIFICATION PROJECT
INTERNAL ECONOMIC RATE OF RETURN (IERR):
RE TIME SLICE INVESTMENT
Benefits
1.          Reddental Consumers. The methodology used to estimate economic benefits for
residential consumers can be explained with the help of Figure 1, which shows a demand
function for a household defined by two points. One end of the function is anchored by the
price-quantity pair (Q,,P,), where Q, denotes household consumption of electricity at PLN's
average price, P,, to this consumer class. To anchor the "upper end" of the demand function,
which represents consumer willingness to pay for higher valued uses of electricity, the price-
quantity pair (Q0,P0) is estimated assuming that kerosene would be used for lighting absent
availability of electricity. Household survey data on kerosene consumption and expenditure for
lighting and other data were utilized to establish the monthly kWh consumption (QD) that would
yield an equivalent amount of lighting intensity (lumens) prior to the availability of electricity;
as well as to convert the unit financial price of kerosene to an equivalent financial cost per kWh
of lighting (Pk). Thus, prior to electrification, Q0 units of kerosene (in kWh equivalence) are
consumed at a financial price of Pk. Following electrification, Q1 units of electricity are
consumed at a price of P,.
2.          The net economic benefit associated with released resources is estimated as (Pk,-
PIe)Q0, where Pk, represents the economic price of kerosene per kWh of equivalent lighting, P1,,
is the economic cost of electricity supply, and Q0 is defined above.
3.          The incremental consumer surplus on the diverted market is represented by the
shaded area in Figure 1. A linear demand function is postulated between the points (Ql, PI) and
(Q0, Pk) for the purpose of estimating the consumer surplus.  This specification tends to
underestimate the willingness to pay since it does not account for the additional value of the
higher quality of light obtained from electricity.
4.          Using the method outlined in the preceding paragraphs, the average willingness
to pay for residential consumers is estimated to be Rp. 401/kWh (19.04 UScents/kWh) in Java-
Bali, of which consumer surplus is estimated to be Rp. 275/kWh (13.05 c/kWh); and Rp.
395/kWh (18.76 c/kWh) Outside Java, of which consumer surplus is estimated to be Rp.
269/kWh (12.77 c/kWh). These estimates assume average monthly consumption of 45 kWh per
residential consumer in Java-Bali (540 kWh per year), and 40 kWh per residential consumer
(480 kWh per year) Outside Java.



127
ANNEX 6.1
Page 2
5.          Non-residential consumers. For non-residential consumers, the basis used for
establishing willingness to pay is the cost of self generation; which has been estimated to be Rp.
354/kWh (16.84 c/kWh) for Java-Bali, and Rp. 345/kWh (16.37 c/kWh) for Outside-Java. Non-
residential consumption is assumed to be 125 kWh per month.
Costs
6.          Supply cost. Energy supply cost are calculated on the basis of the economic cost
of bulk power delivered to the 20 kV network; and have been estimated on the basis of PLN's
LRMC study for Java-Bali, and the report of consultants hired by PLN to develop estimates of
PLN's avoided cost by region, for the purposes of developing the published small power
purchase tariff. For the Java-Bali system, the LRMC for power delivered to the 20 kV network
(excluding losses in the MV network), is 9.20 UScents/kWh for residential consumers, and 8.40
c/kWh for non-residential consumers. Corresponding estimates for the average LRMC of supply
from the seven regional grids in the Outer-Islands are, 10.40 c/kWh for residential consumers,
and 9.70 c/kWh for non-residential consumers. LRMCs for supply from mini-grids with diesel
generation only are estimated to be 12.60 c/kWh for residential consumers and 11.7 c/kWh for
non-residential consumers. O&M costs were provided by PLN based on experience.
7.          Based on the assumptions identified in the preceding, the IERR for the two-year
time slice investment for the distribution component of the proposed project has been estimated
to be 21.1 %. When electricity consumption for residential users was reduced to 42 kWh/month
(500 kWh per year) in Java-Bali, and to 37.5 kWh per month (450 kWh per year) in the Outer
islands, the IERR reduces to 19.1%. With cpital costs in the base case increased by 10%, the
IERR reduces to 19.6%.

INDONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRFICATION PROJECT
LEAST COST AND ECONOMIC RATE OF RETURN ANALYSES
OF MINIHYDRO PROJECTS
1.    A least cost analysis was performed for each of the five minihydro projects comparing
costs with and without the project. The 'with project" case assumed a minihydro/diesel powered
grid compared to the "without project" case of a diesel powered grid. The net present value of
annual costs for operation with and without the minihydro plant were compared within this
analysis to identify the least cost option. The economic rate of return analysis is based on the
costs and benefits specific to each project.
Capital Costs
2.    Cost estimates account for the capital, O&M, and fuel costs within the 25 year time
period for the minihydro and diesel generators connected to the grid. Capital expenditures
within the first four years in the "with project" case reflect the capital costs of the minihydro
plant, including civil works, generating and switch yard equipment, medium voltage line
required for interconnection, indirect costs, a 10% contingency on consulting costs and a 15%
contingency on civil works and electro-mechanical equipment, and interest during construction
(IDC). These cost figures are based on engineering feasibility studies which were reviewed and
updated in early 19941. Price contingencies, tariffs, and taxes are excluded from the cost basis.
In the "with project" case capital expenses beyond the four year period reflect additional diesel
capacity which is required to keep pace with load growth. All capital expenses in the "without
project" case are for diesel capacity.
3.    Diesel capital costs are based on 1993 PLN estimates and are Indonesia-wide averages
including the engine/generator, enclosure, fuel tanks, and mechanical/electrical work. These
costs range from $1,780/kW for a 20 kW unit to $625/kW for 220 kW and larger diesels2.
Operation and Maintenance Costs
4.    Operations and maintenance (O&M) estimates account for all O&M associated with the
minihydro and diesel units as well as costs for transmission and distribution expansion and
maintenance. Minihydro O&M is dominated by maintenance personnel costs, with annual
'PLN data, January 1994.
2PLN RE Planning, Sector Program Loan V 1992/93, spreadsheet supporting data.



131                           ANNEX 6.2
Page 2
expenses ranging from 0.4% to 1.4% of system capital costs, depending on system size and
configuration of civil works. Diesel O&M expenses include PLN-paid maintenance personnel
salary and least 2 at each generation site. The staffing level increases with the number of diesels
at the site.  Diesel overhaul frequency is based on cumulative run living expenses
(-$3,000/person/year). PLN requires a maintenance staff of at hours for each year. PLN
performs major overhauls every 18,000 run hours at an approximate cost of $118/kW of
capacity. Overhauls will occur sooner and more frequently without the minihydro unit serving
the grid. Expenses for consumables such as oil and air filters are estimated at $0.0006 per kWh.
The minihydro units are conservatively assumed to operate with a capacity factor of 60%, based
on flow duration curve analysis. The long-run average incremental cost of grid expansion and
maintenance was estimated at $0.015/kWh, corresponding to networks with voltages of 20 kV
and below. Grid energy losses are conservatively estimated at 10% for all analyses.
Fuel Costs
5.    Diesel fuel economic costs of $0.19/liter and lube-oil costs of $1.38/liter are based on
1993 Indonesian prices and are assumed to remain constant. Fuel and lube-oil transportation
costs of up to $0.03/liter, were estimated from PLN Wilayah-level data. The specific fuel
consumption (SFC) and specific lube-oil consumption (SLC) were assumed to be .3 liters/kWh
and .003 liters/kWh respectively for all sites except Lokomboro. At Lokomboro, an SFC of .42
and an SLC of 0.006 were used to account for lower efficiency operation due to the lighter
loading of the smaller diesels on this grid.
Benefits
6.    For this analysis, energy sales from a project were assumed to be 85 % residential, at a
price of 5.99C/kWh and a consumer surplus of 12.77ClkWh; with commercial sales accounting
for the balance, with energy prices of 6.39C/kWh and a consumer surplus of 9.98C/kWh. Load
growth ranged from 5.8% to 8.9% based on historical data for each site.
Results and Sensitivity Analysis
7.    The summary tables below give the results of the least cost and project IERR analyses;
with the least cost analysis based on the net present value (NPV) with and without the project.
Base case values reflect the expected project conditions as described above. To test the
robustness of the economic viability of the proposed projects, cost estimates were increased from
the base case by 10%. In addition, load growth estimates were reduced by 50% (with all other
values at base case levels). In all cases, the 'with project" case is the least cost option and
project EIRRs remained well over 12%.

INDONESIA
SECOND RURAL ELECTRIFICATION PROJECT
LEAST COST AND ECONOMIC RATE OF RETURN ANALYSIS
OF GEOTHERMAL PROJECTS
1.    The least cost and economic rate of return analyses of the two geothermal projects were
performed on the same basis as the minihydro projects (see Annex 6.2). The "with project" case
assumes a geothermal/diesel powered grid, the "without project" grid assumes the existing diesel
grid.
Costs and Performance Estinates
2.    Both the Sembalun and Tulehu geothermal projects are in the initial stages of
development. For this reason, cost and performance have been estimated based on expected
conditions at the sites. Capital costs include field testing (geoscientific field studies, exploratory
well drilling, well monitoring and testing), steam field development, power plant construction
(at $2000/kW for the first 5 MW and $1000/kW for the next 5 MW). Interest during
construction (IDC) is also included. Data from geoscientific studies and test well drilling will
be used to refine these estimates. A physical contingency of 25% is included in field testing
costs outlined above. Power plant and steam field costs are conservatively estimated, and
include a 10% contingency. The plants are assumed to operate in a baseload mode, with a
capacity factor of 80%. Operation and maintenance costs are estimated at 5% of plant capital
costs. The long-run average incremental cost of grid expansion and maintenance was estimated
at $0.015/kWh, corresponding to networks with voltages of 20 kV and below. Grid energy
losses are estimated at 10%. Diesel capital, O&M, and fuel costs are estimated as in the
minihydro analysis described above.
Results and Sensitivity Analysis
3.    Least cost and cost-benefit analysis summary tables, similar to those described in Annex
6.3 for the mini-hydro projects, are given below. To test the robustness of the economic
viability of the proposed projects, cost estimates were increased from the basecase by 10%. In
addition, load growth estimates were reduced by 50% (with all other values at base case levels).
Results for these also are given with the base case values in the tables below. In all cases, the
"with project" case is the least cost option and project EIRRs remained in well over 12%,
indicating the merit of pursuing these projects at least through the site investigation phase. Once
test drilling has been completed and more accurate cost and performance estimates are available,
the projects would be re-evaluated, and the economic viability re-estimated using the revised
data.
